- Round 2 on plan2: 8 critical, 22 high, 58 total findings
- Round 3 on plan3: 9 critical, 22 high, 56 total findings
- Round 4 on plan4: 9 critical, 20 high, 55 total findings
- Hit max rounds; revising once more into final candidate.
- [stall] agent "revise:plan5" stalled (no progress) after 401s — retrying (1/5)


## Review of plan1  (10 critical / 21 high)


### Lens: Distributed-systems & scalability

*Verdict:* The plan gets the macro-shape right and is unusually disciplined about the one thing that matters most: it never lets eventual consistency touch a confidentiality decision (read-time re-check at the owning node under ZedToken). That single invariant, plus Outbox+CDC instead of dual-write, is the correct backbone and genuinely defuses the scariest class of distributed bug. But the plan is hand-wavy or silent on almost every OTHER distributed-systems concern that actually determines whether this scales past a demo. The central discovery index is presented as "thin" and "stateless" while it is in fact a write-amplified, fan-in, multi-tenant Lucene/vector store that grows with the union of all institutions' publishable corpora — its sizing, sharding, and write-path are never modeled. "Brokered drill-down" papers over a synchronous cross-node RPC whose tail latency is governed by the slowest owning node and whose availability is the PRODUCT of two nodes' availabilities, yet the SLOs assume independence. ZedToken strongly-consistent checks are hand-waved as "p99 < 50ms" with the latency spike literally deferred to Open Question #5 — meaning the core security path is unbenchmarked. Hot tenants, the cross-region exchange round-trip, CDC lag bounding revocation enforcement, and SpiceDB as a single global consistency bottleneck are all under-attacked. The federation does not obviously scale to N=50+ nodes the way it's drawn; it scales to N=2 (the demo). Below are the concrete gaps ranked by how badly they'd hurt.

- **[CRITICAL]** Revocation correctness depends on a CDC fast-path the plan never bounds, and the 'read-time re-check makes correctness independent of propagation latency' claim is only HALF true. Read-time re-check at the owning node protects the DRILL-DOWN path (Section 4.3 mechanism 2). But the central discovery index itself is populated by Outbox+CDC and CAN surface a confidential-adjacent record's EXISTENCE/metadata that should have been pulled after a grant or classification change. The plan admits 'stale shareable metadata after revocation is a leak vector' (10.2) then never specifies how a REVOKE or a tier reclassification (private->confidential, or un-sharing previously-published metadata) is propagated to DELETE from the central index. CDC is append-only log-based; tombstone/delete propagation, ordering vs the original publish, and the window during which the central index still serves a now-revoked pointer are all unspecified. A revoke that fast-paths the GRANT tuple (so drill-down denies) but leaves the metadata row in the central index still leaks the fact-of-existence and the publishable abstract/fingerprint — which for an export-controlled or confidential project IS the leak.
  - *Why:* Confidentiality is the locked, first-class, existential requirement. 'Read-time re-check' only covers the byte fetch, not the metadata that was already replicated outward. There is a real, unmodeled staleness window in the central index for revocations and reclassifications. This is exactly the new-enemy problem the plan congratulates itself on solving — but it solved it for the authz check, not for the replicated index.
  - *Fix:* Specify the revocation/reclassification path for the central index explicitly: (a) emit CDC tombstones for un-publish/revoke/reclassify, with a monotonic per-record version (e.g., grantor-node Lamport/HLC) so consumers reject out-of-order resurrections; (b) make the central index entry itself carry a short TTL / lease so it self-expires absent re-publish, bounding worst-case staleness even if a tombstone is lost; (c) gate any surfacing of a metadata hit through a lightweight 'still-publishable?' check that can be answered from a fast, strongly-consistent revocation set (not the eventually-consistent index); (d) define and SLO the metadata-revocation propagation bound and treat exceeding it as a P1 incident, since 'read-time re-check' does NOT cover it.
- **[CRITICAL]** SpiceDB is drawn as a single, globally-consistent, cross-tenant relationship store in the SHARED control plane, and every confidential drill-down does a ZedToken-consistent Check against it. This makes SpiceDB (a) a global consistency bottleneck and single failure domain for ALL cross-institution access, and (b) a cross-region latency tax on the hot path. The plan's own Section 7.4 says 'confidential PDP evaluation executes inside the owning cell ... never depends on the shared plane' — but Section 4.3 mechanism 2 shows PEPB calling SPICE (control plane) for the grant Check on every drill-down. These two statements contradict: the confidential check DOES depend on the shared plane for the grant relationship. If SpiceDB is down or partitioned, either cross-institution confidential access fails closed (federation outage) or you fall back to cached/eventual (re-introducing new-enemy). For an EU cell drilling into a US-owned record, the ZedToken-consistent Check is a cross-region round-trip on the synchronous path.
  - *Why:* It collapses the control/data-plane isolation story the whole architecture rests on, makes the 'confidential checks never depend on the shared plane' claim false, and turns a single SpiceDB cluster into the availability and latency ceiling for the entire federation. Strong consistency (ZedToken) at global scale across regions is precisely where SpiceDB/Zanzibar latency blows up — and the plan defers benchmarking it to Open Question #5, i.e., the core security path is unvalidated.
  - *Fix:* Resolve the contradiction: either (a) replicate/cache the relevant sharing_grant tuples INTO each owning cell with a strongly-consistent, monotonic mechanism (cell-local SpiceDB with grant tuples pushed via authenticated, ordered replication + ZedToken minted at the grant's home), so the confidential Check is cell-local and survives control-plane partition; or (b) explicitly accept that cross-institution confidential access has a hard dependency on the exchange plane and define its multi-region SLO, partition behavior (fail-closed), and the SpiceDB topology (single global cluster vs regional clusters + cross-region grant replication). Benchmark ZedToken p99 across regions at realistic fan-out BEFORE committing the 50ms SLO; that spike belongs in Phase 0, not Open Question #5.
- **[HIGH]** The central metadata/discovery index is repeatedly described as 'thin', 'stateless', 'modest fixed cost' (Sections 4.1, 16.1) — but it is the fan-in of EVERY institution's publishable profiles, abstracts, capability tags, AND embeddings of publishable text, plus pointers. That is a large, growing, write-amplified multi-tenant vector+text index, not a thin stateless service. Its growth is the UNION of all nodes' public-tier corpora (OpenAlex-scale per institution x N institutions), it ingests a continuous CDC firehose from N nodes, and it must serve cross-institution discovery search with the 800ms p95 SLO. There is no sharding model, no per-tenant partitioning strategy for it, no embedding-dimension/RAM budget, no write-throughput-vs-query-latency tradeoff, and no statement of whether it is Qdrant/OpenSearch and how it scales horizontally. Calling the control plane 'mostly stateless' is simply wrong given this index lives there.
  - *Why:* Horizontal scalability is a hard non-functional requirement. The single largest stateful component in the system is mislabeled as thin and left unsized. At N=50 R1s each with ~50k–500k publishable works/profiles plus embeddings, this is tens-to-hundreds of GB of vector+text index with a heavy continuous write load — its scaling determines control-plane cost and the discovery SLO, and it's currently a black box.
  - *Fix:* Model the central index as a first-class stateful, horizontally-sharded store: choose the engine, define shard key (by tenant? by topic? by embedding cluster?), state the embedding dimension after MRL truncation, estimate index size and write QPS at N=10/50/200 nodes, and define how query latency holds under continuous CDC ingest. Decide whether discovery search is scatter-gather across shards (and bound that fan-out latency) or pre-aggregated. Remove 'thin/stateless/modest' framing for the control plane and give it a real capacity model.
- **[HIGH]** 'Brokered drill-down' (Section 4.3 mechanism 2, 14.2) is a synchronous cross-node RPC whose availability and latency are governed by the OWNING node — yet the plan's failure-domain analysis (14.3) claims clean cell isolation and the SLOs (14.4) treat operations as independent. A drill-down from A into B's record requires A's PEP, the control plane (SpiceDB + index), AND B's cell to all be up: availability is the PRODUCT, not the min. Self-hosted/on-prem sovereign nodes (Section 13) make this worse — they may be air-gapped, behind university firewalls, on flaky campus networks, or simply offline at 2am. The plan acknowledges 'slowest-node tail latency' once (14.2) and then sets a discovery p95 of 800ms that only holds for the prebuilt-index path, not the brokered path, whose tail is unbounded by design (it waits on an arbitrary university's node).
  - *Why:* Cross-institution drill-down is a core feature (discovery, workspaces). If its latency/availability is dominated by the least-reliable participating node and that's not modeled, the federation feels broken precisely when it spans many heterogeneous, university-operated nodes — which is the GTM endgame (consortium, sovereign editions). 'Cell-isolated failure domains' is true for intra-node ops but false for the cross-node feature that justifies the product.
  - *Fix:* Separate SLOs for index-path vs brokered-path operations. Give brokered drill-down explicit timeout/circuit-breaker/degraded-result semantics ('owning node unreachable -> show publishable metadata only, mark detail unavailable'). Model cross-node operation availability as a product and state the target (e.g., a brokered request touching 3 components at 99.9% each is ~99.7%). Add per-owning-node health/SLA tracking and define behavior when a sovereign/air-gapped node is offline. Bound the brokered tail explicitly rather than implying the 800ms applies.
- **[HIGH]** Hot-tenant / hot-partition handling is essentially absent despite the prompt and locked requirements making it obvious (a handful of mega-R1s like MIT/Stanford will dwarf a small college in corpus size, query volume, and publishable-metadata firehose). The plan's only multi-tenancy lever is 'dedicated cell vs shared cell' (Section 2.3) and 'per-tenant rate limits' (14.2). But (a) the SHARED control-plane components — central index, SpiceDB, TEE broker, CDC pipeline — are pooled across ALL tenants and have no noisy-neighbor isolation story; a single mega-tenant's CDC backfill or query storm degrades discovery for everyone. (b) Shared/soft-isolated PLG cells (Lab edition) explicitly co-tenant multiple labs — yet there's no per-tenant resource quota for vector RAM or local-LLM GPU time, which Section 16/Open-Q4 admit is the dominant COGS. A single PLG lab running a large batch distill can starve co-tenants' inference.
  - *Why:* Horizontal scalability with heterogeneous tenants is locked. Without hot-tenant isolation, the shared planes become the contention point and the multi-tenant PLG economics (the MVP wedge revenue) break under one noisy lab. This is a classic SaaS-at-scale failure the plan doesn't engage.
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit noisy-neighbor model: per-tenant quotas and fair-scheduling on the central index write/query path and on shared-cell GPU/vector RAM; CDC per-tenant partitioning with bounded lag SLOs so one tenant's backfill can't starve others; a 'cell-split / promote to dedicated' trigger when a tenant crosses a size/QPS threshold. Define what happens to SpiceDB and the TEE broker under a single tenant's load spike. State the shared-cell isolation guarantee precisely (it currently undermines the 'confidential' claim — see Open Q4).
- **[HIGH]** Per-tenant knowledge-graph and vector-index GROWTH inside each cell is unmodeled beyond 'MRL truncation + Qdrant sharding'. Apache AGE (Cypher-on-Postgres) is chosen for the per-cell graph, but AGE's traversal performance on multi-hop queries over a full institutional + ingested-OpenAlex-subset graph (millions of author/paper/citation edges) is known to degrade badly compared to native graph engines — and the plan layers HippoRAG2 Personalized-PageRank ON TOP of AGE in Phase 2. PPR over a large graph in Postgres is not free; '~1k tokens/query' bounds the LLM cost, not the graph-compute cost. There's no statement of graph size per cell, no index strategy for the citation edges, and no acknowledgment that co-locating pgvector + AGE + authz tables in one Postgres (Section 6.2/12) couples three very different workloads (HNSW vector RAM, graph traversal CPU, OLTP authz) onto one instance's resource envelope.
  - *Why:* Graph + vector growth and cross-node latency are explicitly in scope for this lens. Choosing AGE 'because it co-locates with pgvector and authz' optimizes for deployment tidiness at the cost of the actual scaling characteristic of the graph workload. If multi-hop PPR queries are slow or the co-located Postgres becomes a resource-contention single point per cell, the lit-intelligence/discovery latency SLOs are at risk as corpora grow.
  - *Fix:* Size the per-cell graph (edges/nodes at a realistic R1) and benchmark AGE multi-hop + PPR latency at that scale before locking it; have a real fallback trigger to Neo4j/Memgraph if AGE doesn't hold (the plan lists them as fallbacks but with no switch criterion). Reconsider co-locating HNSW vector + graph traversal + authz OLTP in one Postgres — define resource isolation or split them. State the graph-compute (not token) cost of HippoRAG2 PPR per query at target scale.
- **[MEDIUM]** The consistency model is stated as a clean binary (strong for confidentiality decisions, eventual for discovery metadata) but several flows straddle the line without resolution. Examples: (1) The deterministic knowledge graph and expertise fingerprints are built from CDC-replicated data — are GNN link predictions / collaborator recommendations computed on possibly-stale federated data, and is that acceptable? (2) The grant-team-assembly (Pillar 4) operates 'over the federated public-tier graph' — assembled from eventually-consistent replicas, so a team recommendation could include a researcher whose affiliation/availability just changed. (3) Sharing-grant CREATION via Temporal is a multi-day workflow, but the moment a grant tuple is written, does the grantee's central-index view update consistently, or is there a window where the grant exists but the index hasn't surfaced the now-shareable record? The plan only ever discusses the REVOKE direction's staleness, never the GRANT direction.
  - *Why:* A clean one-line consistency rule is good marketing but the system has more than two consistency classes in practice. Unstated staleness in the grant-creation and recommendation paths leads to confusing UX ('I was granted access but can't see it yet') and, worse, recommendations built on stale federated state that a buyer will notice.
  - *Fix:* Enumerate every cross-node data flow and assign each an explicit consistency class with a staleness bound: discovery-index population, grant creation visibility, revoke/reclassify propagation, graph/GNN feature freshness, recommendation inputs. Define the grant-creation visibility window symmetric to the revoke window. Make the 'one-line rule' a table, not a slogan.
- **[MEDIUM]** CDC/event-bus operational reality is under-specified for a system that depends on it for correctness, not just convenience. Debezium + Kafka/Redpanda is named, but: ordering guarantees across the Outbox of a node (per-record vs global), exactly-once vs at-least-once into the central index (idempotent consumers are mentioned, but idempotency keys / dedup window are not), schema-registry compatibility handling across N independently-upgrading nodes, and backpressure behavior when the central index can't keep up with N firehoses are all unaddressed. The plan says 'idempotent consumers + schema registry' as if naming the pattern resolves it. With self-hosted nodes on different release versions (Section 13.2 'university-pulled signed releases'), event schema skew across nodes is guaranteed, not hypothetical.
  - *Why:* The federation seam's integrity literally rides on this pipeline. At-least-once + non-idempotent index writes = duplicate/zombie metadata entries; lost tombstones = leak window (see finding #1); version skew across self-hosted nodes = consumer breakage or silent drops. These are the failure modes that bite at N>5, not at the demo.
  - *Fix:* Specify: per-record monotonic versioning + idempotency keys for index upserts/deletes; the compatibility policy (forward+backward) the registry enforces and how the central consumer handles an event from a node running an OLDER schema; bounded-lag alerting on each node's CDC stream; and backpressure/shedding policy that NEVER drops tombstones (delete events must be durable even when publish events are shed).
- **[MEDIUM]** Cross-region latency is mentioned only as 'EU in-region cell' for residency, but the latency consequences of a geo-distributed federation on the synchronous paths are not analyzed. A US researcher drilling into an EU-owned confidential record triggers: US-cell PEP -> control-plane SpiceDB ZedToken Check (which region?) -> EU-cell PEPB re-check -> EU local model inference -> response back to US. That's multiple cross-Atlantic round-trips (~80–150ms each) on a path the plan SLOs at sub-second. The control-plane is multi-region (13.1) but it's not stated whether SpiceDB is single-region (cross-region tax) or multi-region (then cross-region strong-consistency replication cost). The PSI/TEE match (mechanism 3) between a US and an EU node is also a cross-region, multi-round-trip cryptographic protocol with no latency budget.
  - *Why:* The product's whole point is CROSS-institution, and institutions are geo-distributed. Cross-region round-trips on strongly-consistent paths can blow the 50ms Check SLO and the 4s Q&A SLO by themselves. This is the literal 'cross-node latency' item the lens demands and the plan barely touches it.
  - *Fix:* Draw the geo-distributed sequence for a cross-region confidential drill-down and sum the round-trips against the SLO. Decide SpiceDB region topology and its strong-consistency replication cost. Give PSI/TEE cross-region protocols a latency budget and consider async/batched modes for them. If sub-second cross-region strong checks are infeasible, say so and adjust the SLO honestly.
- **[MEDIUM]** The TEE match broker is a single shared component in the control plane handling cross-institution PSI/clean-room matches for ALL tenant pairs, but its scaling, queueing, and failure behavior are unmodeled. PSI and clean-room computations are CPU/crypto-heavy and can be long-running; N choose 2 institution pairs in a large consortium is a combinatorial match surface. There's no statement of how many concurrent PSI sessions the broker sustains, what happens under contention, whether matches are queued/batched, or how a single enclave's capacity scales horizontally (enclaves don't trivially autoscale, and attestation per-enclave adds setup cost). It's also a single trust-and-availability chokepoint for all confidential cross-institution analytics.
  - *Why:* Pillars 3 and 4 (workspaces, grant team assembly over confidential data) route through this broker. If it can't scale concurrent crypto-heavy sessions across many tenant pairs, the confidential-collaboration features — the actual moat — bottleneck at one enclave fleet.
  - *Fix:* Define the TEE broker as a horizontally-scaled enclave fleet with a session scheduler, state per-session resource cost and max concurrency, and specify queueing/backpressure for match requests. Clarify whether matches are interactive (latency-bound) or batch (throughput-bound) and budget accordingly. Address attestation/enclave warm-pool cost so cold-start doesn't dominate.
- **[LOW]** SLO targets in 14.4 are asserted with no derivation and some are internally inconsistent with the architecture. 'Confidential Check p99 < 50ms (ZedToken-consistent)' is stated while the same value's feasibility is deferred to Open Q5 — i.e., the SLO precedes the spike that would validate it. 'Revocation propagation < 5s (fast path)' coexists with 'read-time re-check makes correctness independent of propagation latency' — but per finding #1 that independence does NOT hold for the central index, so the 5s is actually a leak-window bound and should be treated as security-critical, not a soft target. 'Discovery p95 < 800ms' doesn't distinguish index-path from brokered-path (finding #4).
  - *Why:* SLOs that aren't derived from a capacity/latency model are aspirational and will mislead capacity planning and customer commitments. Two of them are entangled with unresolved correctness issues above.
  - *Fix:* Tie each SLO to a measured or modeled budget; split discovery SLOs by path; re-label revocation-propagation as a security-critical leak-window bound for the index path with alerting; and move the ZedToken benchmark ahead of asserting its SLO.

### Lens: Security, privacy & confidentiality — enforcement of the public/private/confidential model end-to-end, authz completeness (ABAC+ReBAC), cross-institution sharing/revocation, confidential-tier crypto/BYOK, federation confused-deputy/IDOR risks, and FERPA/GDPR/export-control correctness.

*Verdict:* This plan is unusually security-literate for a Round 1 architecture: it correctly identifies the federation seam as the existential risk, picks ZedToken-consistent ReBAC for revocation correctness, mandates read-time re-check at the owning node, crypto-shreds via per-tenant KEK, and weaponizes "data never leaves your node." The mechanics of the happy path are sound. But the plan repeatedly conflates *enforcement* with *classification*, and that gap is where the leaks live. The single largest structural flaw is that the entire confidentiality guarantee rests on the data-classification engine correctly tagging every record at ingest — yet the plan treats classification as a thin kernel primitive with no discussion of misclassification handling, default tier on failure, re-classification/declassification, or derived-data tiering (embeddings, summaries, graph edges, expertise fingerprints, BM25 term stats). A record that is mis-tagged or whose *derivative* is mis-tagged flows straight out the Outbox into the central index with no second gate. Secondary critical issues: the central discovery index is a plaintext aggregation honeypot outside any tenant boundary with no stated encryption/access model; "publishable metadata only" is asserted but never enforced by an independent egress filter (the Outbox writer is trusted, not checked); revocation is correct for *future* reads but the plan never addresses already-disclosed/cached/derived data (embeddings computed cross-tenant, answers already synthesized, central-index entries built from since-revoked grants); and the export-control "deemed export" model collects nationality but never defines how it is verified, who attests it, or how a foreign-national PEP bypass is prevented. ABAC+ReBAC composition order is also under-specified in a way that can fail-open. The plan is fundable and the bones are right, but several controls are asserted as invariants without an enforcing mechanism — and an asserted invariant is not a control.

- **[CRITICAL]** The entire confidentiality guarantee is single-point-of-failure on the classification engine, with no defined behavior for misclassification, classification failure, default tier, or derived-data tiering. The plan says 'classifies every record as public|private|confidential; emits the routing attribute that binds everything' (Section 3.2, 8.1) but never specifies: (a) what tier a record gets if classification fails or is ambiguous (fail-open to public = catastrophic leak; the only safe default is confidential/deny); (b) how a record gets RE-classified when its sensitivity changes (a public preprint that becomes ITAR-controlled, a private doc shared into a confidential project); (c) the tier of DERIVED artifacts — embeddings of confidential text, LLM summaries/research-cards, graph edges, BM25 term-frequency statistics, expertise fingerprints. A confidential document's embedding or its co-author graph edge can leak the underlying content (embedding-inversion attacks are real) yet may be tagged public-by-omission and flow out the Outbox.
  - *Why:* If classification is the binding attribute for the model router, the Outbox egress filter, and every PEP decision, then a single mis-tag or an untagged derivative silently defeats every downstream control — local-model routing, egress block, and central-index exclusion all key off the same tag. The plan's own invariant ('confidential bytes never leave their cell') is only as true as the weakest classifier decision, and classifiers are probabilistic. There is no second, independent gate. This can sink the platform on first incident.
  - *Fix:* Make classification fail-CLOSED: any record without a high-confidence tier defaults to confidential (deny egress). Define an explicit derived-data tiering rule: every derivative inherits the MAX tier of its inputs (embedding of confidential text is confidential; a graph edge touching a confidential node is at least private). Add a re-classification/declassification workflow with mandatory human approval for any tier-DOWN transition, audited. Add an independent egress-classification scan (Section 8.4 mentions output scanning — extend it to the Outbox path) so the central index is gated by a SECOND mechanism, not just the ingest tag.
- **[CRITICAL]** The 'publishable metadata only' invariant on the Outbox->CDC->central-index path is asserted but has no independent enforcing mechanism — the Outbox writer is a TRUSTED component, not a CHECKED one. Section 4.2/5.2 list 'IPublishMetadata (Outbox writer)' as a kernel contract and Section 10.2 says 'Confidential bytes never enter the outbox,' but nothing validates this at the egress boundary. If any module (or a bug, or a misclassified record per Finding 1) writes confidential content to the outbox, Debezium faithfully replicates it to the central control plane, which by design 'holds NO confidential bytes.' The CDC layer cannot tell publishable from confidential — it ships whatever is in the outbox table.
  - *Why:* This is the existential federation-seam leak (the plan's own Risk #1) but the mitigation is circular: the invariant is enforced by 'we only write publishable things to the outbox,' which is exactly the thing that needs enforcing. A single buggy module, a schema-evolution mistake, or a misclassified field leaks confidential bytes off-tenant into a centralized store, and CDC makes it instantaneous and replicated. Worse, once in the central index it is outside the owning tenant's cell and outside its key boundary.
  - *Fix:* Insert an independent, deny-by-default egress filter (a PEP) between the outbox and CDC that re-evaluates each outbound record's classification against a strict allowlist schema of publishable fields, strips/rejects anything else, and audits every emission. Treat the outbox as an untrusted source. Add the federation-seam golden-path leak test (Section 15.3 mentions it) as a CONTINUOUS runtime assertion, not just a CI test — sample outbound records in production and alarm on any confidential tag reaching the boundary. Consider per-field classification, not per-record, so a private doc with one public abstract publishes ONLY the abstract.
- **[CRITICAL]** The central metadata/discovery index is an unencrypted cross-institution aggregation honeypot living OUTSIDE every tenant boundary, with no stated access-control, encryption-at-rest, or tenant-scoping model. Section 4.1/4.2 place 'publishable profiles, abstracts, capability tags, embeddings of publishable text' in the shared control plane. Even if every record is correctly public-tier, the AGGREGATE — who-works-on-what across all institutions, capability tags, embeddings — is a high-value intelligence target (competitive, foreign-adversary, export-control reconnaissance). The plan never says the central index enforces per-query authz, never says it is encrypted, never scopes which tenant can see which other tenant's published metadata (publishing to discovery != consenting to be discovered by EVERY connected institution).
  - *Why:* Aggregation of individually-public data creates confidential insight (the mosaic effect — well-recognized in export-control and intelligence contexts). A breach of the central index, or an over-broad query, exposes the entire federation's research-capability map at once. For an export-controlled/defense beachhead (Section 3.4) this aggregate is itself sensitive even when each row is unclassified. There is also no notion that University A might publish to the discovery layer but only want Universities in its consortium (not all N nodes) to see it — discovery visibility is treated as binary public, ignoring institution-scoped or consortium-scoped discoverability.
  - *Fix:* Apply a PEP to the central index itself: queries are authz-checked, results are scoped by a discoverability grant (who may discover this record), and the index is encrypted at rest with control-plane keys distinct from tenant keys. Add a 'discoverability scope' attribute to published records (public-web | federation-wide | named-consortium | named-tenants) enforced at query time. For defense/export-controlled tenants, allow opting OUT of central indexing entirely (live federated drill-down only). Treat the aggregate as at least private-tier and threat-model the mosaic effect explicitly.
- **[HIGH]** Revocation is correct only for FUTURE reads; the plan has no story for already-disclosed, cached, derived, or in-flight data after a grant is revoked. Section 4.3/7.2/14.4 repeatedly state 'reads re-check at the owning node -> stale index entries can never grant access' and 'read-time re-check makes correctness independent of propagation latency.' True for the next read. But: (a) the central index entry built WHILE a now-revoked grant was active persists until CDC catches up — and that entry is metadata the grantee already has; (b) embeddings or PSI overlaps computed during the grant window are already in the grantee's possession or were used to synthesize an answer; (c) a synthesized LLM answer or downloaded artifact from before revocation is gone from the owner's control entirely. 'Revocable' is sold as a moat and a compliance feature, but it only revokes prospective access, not disclosure.
  - *Why:* Buyers (and FERPA/GDPR/DUA obligations) will hear 'revocable sharing grants' as 'I can pull back what I shared.' For confidential project IP and PII this gap is both a security and a legal-representation risk. GDPR right-to-erasure (Section 11.3) is reconciled via crypto-shred for data the owner holds, but says nothing about copies legitimately disclosed to a grantee tenant under a since-revoked grant — those live in the grantee's cell under the grantee's keys.
  - *Fix:* Define revocation semantics precisely and state them in the product: revocation = no further access + central-index entry purged on fast path (not lazy CDC) + obligation on grantee tenant to crypto-shred derived copies (enforceable only via DUA + audit, so say so). Distinguish 'revoke access' from 'revoke disclosure' explicitly. For confidential workspaces, prefer zero-copy query-where-data-resides (the plan mentions this) so no durable copy ever lands in the grantee cell — make that the DEFAULT for confidential cross-tenant work, not an option.
- **[HIGH]** ABAC+ReBAC composition order and fail-mode are under-specified, creating a fail-open risk. Section 7.2/7.4 describe a 'two-stage check: SpiceDB relationship path -> Cedar ABAC caveat' and say ABAC attributes 'gate whether a relationship even counts.' But the PIP that supplies ABAC attributes (export_controlled, contains_PHI, residency_region, nationality) is a separate per-cell service (Section 7.4). What happens when the PIP is unavailable, returns a missing attribute, or returns stale data? If a missing 'export_controlled' attribute is treated as 'none' (the enum default in Section 6.1 DOCUMENT), an ITAR record with an unset attribute fails OPEN. The plan does not state that absent/indeterminate attributes deny. Also unclear: is the SpiceDB check authoritative and Cedar only narrows (safe), or can a Cedar caveat ever WIDEN access (unsafe)? Cedar's default-deny helps, but the composition contract is not pinned.
  - *Why:* The hardest compliance attributes (export control, PHI) are exactly the ones most likely to be unset on legacy/ingested records, and 'unset enum -> none -> allow' is a classic fail-open. For a deemed-export scenario this is a felony-grade leak (foreign national gets controlled technical data because the attribute was null).
  - *Fix:* Specify: (1) indeterminate/missing ABAC attribute => DENY for confidential and export-controlled tiers (fail-closed PIP); (2) ABAC can only NARROW the ReBAC grant, never widen — make this a tested invariant; (3) PIP unavailability on a confidential check => deny, not cache-fallback; (4) change the DOCUMENT.export_controlled default from an implicit 'none' to 'unknown' which the PEP treats as controlled until adjudicated.
- **[HIGH]** The export-control 'deemed export' design collects nationality as an access attribute but never defines how nationality/citizenship is established, attested, verified, or kept current — and the federated IdP makes this worse. Section 6.1/7.2/11.3 add 'nationality'/'country' as a subject attribute for deemed-export gating 'where lawfully collected.' But federated identity (CILogon -> Keycloak, JIT provisioning, eduPersonScopedAffiliation) does NOT carry verified citizenship; home institutions do not release citizenship in SAML assertions, and self-asserted nationality is worthless for ITAR. The plan routes the deemed-export decision through the owning node's PEP using a subject attribute it has no trustworthy source for. A confused-deputy angle compounds it: University A asserts an affiliation for a user, and University B's PEP must make a US-person determination about A's user with no authoritative citizenship source.
  - *Why:* ITAR deemed-export violations are criminal. Gating access on an unverified, self-asserted, or absent nationality attribute is worse than not claiming the capability — it creates documented evidence the platform PURPORTED to enforce export control and failed. This directly undermines the high-ACV defense-lab beachhead the plan leans on (Section 3.4).
  - *Fix:* Do not treat nationality as an SSO-carried attribute. Require export-controlled access to be gated by an explicit, institution-attested US-person determination recorded as a signed assertion by the owning tenant's export-control officer (a sharing_grant caveat with an attestation reference), not by a subject claim. Make export-controlled projects fully opt-in AND require the owning node to enumerate authorized persons explicitly (allowlist), never an attribute-derived implicit set. State clearly that the platform enforces the access-control mechanism but the institution owns the US-person determination.
- **[HIGH]** BYOK/HYOK key model has unspecified DEK lifecycle gaps and an inference-time exposure window that undercuts the crypto guarantee. Section 11.1 says 'Revoke KEK = cryptographic lockout / crypto-shred' and 'per-tenant envelope encryption (DEK/KEK) via Vault.' But: (a) for the platform to run RAG/inference over confidential data, the DEK must be available in plaintext in cell memory — so 'we never can decrypt' (HYOK, Section 13.2) is false during active processing; the plan does not address protecting plaintext-at-use (the vLLM process, the embedding index, OS swap, core dumps). (b) Crypto-shred only erases data encrypted under that KEK — but embeddings in Qdrant, BM25 postings in OpenSearch, and graph nodes in AGE are typically NOT individually envelope-encrypted; the plan never says these stores are encrypted under the tenant KEK, so KEK revocation does NOT shred the searchable derivatives. (c) No key rotation, no DEK-per-record vs DEK-per-tenant decision, no story for what happens to in-flight queries at revocation.
  - *Why:* Crypto-shred is the GDPR-erasure and revocation backbone (Sections 4.3, 11.1, 11.3). If the embedding/BM25/graph derivatives are not under the same key, revoking the KEK leaves a fully-searchable, content-revealing copy of 'erased' confidential data in the vector and search indices. And 'university holds the key, we never decrypt' (the sovereign-edition sales claim) is contradicted by the need to decrypt for inference.
  - *Fix:* State the plaintext-at-use boundary honestly: confidential plaintext is exposed in cell memory during inference; mitigate with TEE-backed inference (memory encryption), disabled swap, disabled core dumps, and ephemeral DEK caching. Require that ALL derivative stores (Qdrant, OpenSearch, AGE, object storage) for confidential data are encrypted under the tenant KEK so crypto-shred actually shreds them. Define key rotation and DEK granularity. Correct the HYOK claim to 'university controls key custody and can revoke; plaintext is processed transiently in-cell under TEE' rather than 'we never can decrypt.'
- **[HIGH]** PSI/clean-room aggregate outputs and 'embeddings of publishable text' leak confidential information through inference even when raw bytes never move. Section 4.3 mechanism 3 returns 'overlap / aggregate only (never raw sets)' for 'Do A and B share collaborators?' and the privacy-tech ranking (4.4) lists '2-party PSI + DP for aggregates.' But DP is mentioned ONCE and never wired in: a bare PSI intersection size, or repeated overlap queries, reconstructs membership (a classic differencing/intersection-attack). Likewise 'embeddings of publishable text' in the central index are subject to embedding-inversion (reconstructing source text) — and if any non-public text's embedding slips in (Finding 1), the embedding itself is a leak.
  - *Why:* The federation's whole premise is 'compute over confidential data without exposing it.' If aggregate/overlap queries lack a privacy budget, an adversary at one institution mines the membership and relationships of another through repeated 'harmless aggregate' queries — a leak that bypasses every PEP because each individual query is authorized. This is the subtle, hard-to-detect failure that erodes the core claim.
  - *Fix:* Make differential privacy a REQUIRED, budgeted control on all cross-institution aggregate/PSI outputs, not a footnote: per-tenant-pair privacy budget, minimum aggregation thresholds (k-anonymity floor before any overlap is returned), and query-template pre-approval (the plan mentions templates — make them mandatory and rate-limited). Threat-model embedding inversion: do not publish raw embeddings to the central index for anything but lowest-sensitivity public text, or publish only quantized/dimension-reduced vectors, and never publish embeddings of private/confidential derivatives.
- **[MEDIUM]** Confused-deputy / token-audience handling at the federation seam is named but incompletely specified for the brokered drill-down. Section 4.3 shows University A's PEP making a 'brokered request to OWNING node' (University B's PEP), which then checks SpiceDB + Cedar. The threat-model row (11.5) cites 'SP-initiated SSO; strict issuer/audience validation; scope assertions to asserting tenant; DPoP.' Good, but the drill-down is a SERVICE-to-SERVICE call between two tenant cells — what identity does B's PEP trust? If A's cell asserts 'this is user U from tenant A,' B must NOT trust A's self-assertion about U's authorization; B must independently validate a token issued by the central IdP for U, audience-scoped to B, sender-constrained. The plan does not state that B re-derives U's identity from a control-plane-issued token rather than trusting A's brokering claim. If B trusts A's assertion, A is a confused deputy / can impersonate.
  - *Why:* Cross-tenant brokering where the requesting tenant asserts the end-user's identity is the textbook confused-deputy/IDOR setup. A compromised or malicious cell A could request University B's confidential details on behalf of a user who never authorized it, or escalate to other users.
  - *Fix:* Specify that brokered drill-down carries a control-plane-issued, audience=B, DPoP-bound, short-lived token for the actual end user; B's PEP validates the token signature/audience/binding and resolves the subject from the token, NEVER from A's request body. The sharing_grant is checked against the token subject. Add an explicit test: cell A cannot obtain B's confidential data by asserting an arbitrary subject.
- **[MEDIUM]** FERPA processor/'school official' framing is asserted but the architecture has a structural conflict: cross-institution sharing means University A's PEP can release records that may contain another student's/researcher's FERPA-protected education records to University B. Section 11.3 frames the vendor as the institution's agent under 'direct control' — fine for single-tenant. But the federation's entire value prop is moving identifiable data ACROSS institutions. FERPA permits disclosure to another school official only under narrow conditions; a sharing_grant created by a PI does NOT satisfy FERPA's institutional-consent or legitimate-educational-interest requirements for student data. The plan's sharing_grant has a 'classification_ceiling' (private max) but no concept of 'contains FERPA-protected third-party education records' as a gating attribute, and PIs are not authorized to make FERPA disclosure decisions.
  - *Why:* If confidential/private project corpora contain student records (advisees, grad researchers, course-linked data), a researcher-initiated cross-institution grant can effect an unauthorized FERPA disclosure. FERPA liability stays with the institution (the plan acknowledges this) — meaning the platform's sharing mechanism can put the BUYER in violation, which kills institutional sales on legal review.
  - *Fix:* Add a FERPA-protected flag (and an IRB/PII flag) to DOCUMENT and make cross-institution grants on FERPA-flagged data require an institutional-role approval (registrar/compliance), not a PI's grant. Default: confidential/private records containing identifiable student data are NOT cross-institution-shareable via PI grant alone. Encode FERPA's directory-vs-education-record distinction in the classification engine.
- **[MEDIUM]** Audit log integrity, tenancy, and confidentiality of the audit itself are under-specified, and a cross-tenant access decision logically belongs to TWO tenants. Section 11.2 says 'append-only, tamper-evident audit log of every access decision.' But: where does it live (in-cell, control plane, both)? For a cross-institution drill-down, the decision is made at B's PEP — does A's tenant get an audit record of its user's cross-institution access? Who can read the audit (audit logs of confidential access can themselves reveal confidential relationships — 'User X at A accessed Project Y at B' is sensitive)? 'Tamper-evident' is asserted but no mechanism (hash chain, external anchoring, write-once storage) is named. For incident forensics and compliance (SOC2/FERPA/DUA), an audit log that the operator or a tenant admin can alter, or that leaks cross-tenant relationships, fails its purpose.
  - *Why:* Audit is a kernel/MVP control and a compliance gate (SOC2, DUA enforcement, breach forensics). If it is mutable, single-tenant when the event is cross-tenant, or readable by parties who shouldn't see the relationship, it undermines both the compliance posture and the confidentiality model.
  - *Fix:* Specify hash-chained append-only logs with periodic external anchoring (or WORM storage); per-tenant audit partitions with a cross-tenant access producing a record on BOTH the accessor and owner sides; audit-read authz so confidential-relationship metadata in logs is itself access-controlled; and confidential-tier audit content kept in-cell (Section 15.1 hints at this for telemetry — extend it explicitly to audit).
- **[MEDIUM]** BYO-provider/keys (per-institution cloud LLM keys) creates an egress and exfiltration path that can bypass the confidential local-only routing if not strictly bound to tier. Section 8.2 allows 'Per-institution BYO provider/keys throughout' and the router has a 'BYO configured' branch parallel to the confidential/public branches. The diagram shows BYO as a third route — but if an institution configures BYO and a confidential-tagged request is allowed to take the BYO branch (sending confidential data to the institution's chosen cloud provider), the 'confidential never leaves the boundary' invariant is broken by configuration. The plan says confidential is constrained to 'open, self-hostable' and BYO is 'public-tier or explicit BYO-key only' (8.2) — but the routing diagram and the per-institution BYO premium tier invite a config where BYO applies to confidential. The precedence (tier-routing ALWAYS dominates BYO) is implied, not pinned.
  - *Why:* A premium feature (BYO keys) that can be misconfigured to route confidential data to an external API is the kind of foot-gun that produces a breach via a checkbox. The whole locked AI-layer requirement is that confidential data uses in-boundary models; BYO must be a strict subset of the public/low-risk path.
  - *Fix:* Pin the precedence as an invariant: classification routing is evaluated FIRST and confidential ALWAYS routes local; BYO keys are only selectable for public/low-risk tiers and the UI/config CANNOT attach a BYO cloud provider to the confidential tier. Enforce at the router with a tested assertion ('confidential + BYO-cloud configured => still routes local, BYO ignored, audited'). Allow BYO to point at a self-hosted endpoint for confidential only if the institution attests it is in-boundary.
- **[MEDIUM]** Prompt-injection / indirect-injection across the trust boundary is mentioned as a guardrail but not threat-modeled for the federation, where it becomes a confidentiality exfiltration vector. Section 8.4 lists 'prompt-injection defense' generically. But in this product, retrieved documents (including cross-institution shared content, or public papers, or another tenant's published abstracts in the central index) feed the LLM. A malicious or poisoned document in the corpus can instruct the model to exfiltrate confidential context into its output, or to mislabel classification, or to cause the model to include confidential retrieved chunks in a response surfaced cross-tier. The output 'confidential-leak scanning before any cross-tier surfacing' (8.4) is the only backstop and it is a probabilistic LLM/PII scan, not a hard boundary.
  - *Why:* RAG over mixed-tier corpora with an LLM means content from a lower-trust source can manipulate generation over higher-trust context. If a confidential and a public chunk co-occur in one synthesis call, injection in the public chunk can leak the confidential chunk into an output destined for a lower-clearance surface. This is a realistic, documented attack class against RAG.
  - *Fix:* Enforce tier-segregated synthesis: a single generation call must not mix chunks across a tier boundary unless the output is clamped to the highest tier present (and routed/surfaced accordingly). Treat retrieved content as untrusted data, never instructions (delimit/structure context). Make the output-classification gate a HARD policy check on the destination surface's clearance, with confidential outputs unable to reach lower-tier surfaces regardless of LLM scan result. Add injection-corpus tests to the eval harness.
- **[LOW]** GDPR controller/processor framing and cross-border specifics have gaps for the federated case. Section 11.3 sets University = controller, vendor = processor, EU data in-region cell. But in a cross-institution share from an EU university to a US university, the EU university's lawful basis and the transfer mechanism (SCC/adequacy) must cover the DATA SUBJECTS in the shared records, and the platform mediates that transfer. The plan mentions 'cross-border needs adequacy/SCCs' but does not encode a transfer-legality gate into the sharing_grant (e.g., a grant from an EU cell to a non-adequate-country cell should be blocked or require an SCC reference attribute). Also, processor obligations include sub-processor disclosure (CILogon, cloud KMS, TEE provider) which is not mentioned.
  - *Why:* A federated share is a cross-border personal-data transfer; without a transfer-legality attribute on the grant, the platform can facilitate an unlawful transfer, exposing the EU buyer. Sub-processor transparency is a standard DPA requirement that institutional procurement will demand.
  - *Fix:* Add a transfer-legality caveat to cross-institution sharing_grants involving personal data (adequacy/SCC reference required when source and destination regions differ), enforced by Cedar against residency_region attributes. Maintain and publish a sub-processor list as part of the DPA package.

### Lens: Maintainability & modularity: module boundaries, coupling, blast radius, interface clarity, testability, swappability of AI-router and retrieval

*Verdict:* This plan is unusually disciplined about modularity intent — it names a kernel-contract layer (`shared/contracts/` as "the law"), forbids cross-module storage access, defines a module manifest, and gives credible add/remove playbooks. The kernel-vs-module split and the rule "a module never imports another module" are genuinely good and would resist rot if enforced. However, the plan repeatedly mistakes *naming an interface* for *decoupling*, and several of the most load-bearing seams are under-specified or actively coupled in ways that will rot. The biggest gaps: (1) the kernel itself is a monolith of 8 contracts with hidden mutual dependencies and no story for evolving them without lockstep redeploys; (2) the "swappable" model router and IRetrieval are coupled to data-classification semantics, tier enums, and per-tenant fusion state that leak the abstraction; (3) modules declare dependencies on *other modules* (mod-discovery depends on mod-workspaces' graph, mod-funding depends on mod-discovery) directly contradicting "a module never imports another module"; (4) the contract-versioning scheme (semver + deprecation window serving both) has no concrete compatibility-testing or consumer-contract mechanism, so it will degrade into de-facto lockstep; (5) testability of the federation seam — the existential risk — rests on "golden-path leak tests" that are described but never made falsifiable. The plan is strong on vocabulary and weak on the enforcement machinery that actually prevents rot. It will modularize well at the pillar level and rot at the kernel and cross-cutting-concern level.

- **[CRITICAL]** Module dependency graph contradicts the stated 'a module never imports another module' rule. Section 3.1 lists mod-discovery depending on 'Graph' and mod-funding depending on 'Discovery, Graph'; Section 3.5 says GNN link prediction and cross-institution team assembly (mod-funding) sit 'over the heterogeneous graph' built by discovery; Section 9.3 chains expertise->GNN->team-assembly as one evolving surface. Yet Section 5.7 declares 'A module never imports another module' and 5.2 says modules talk only via events or kernel services. These cannot both be true: mod-funding's team assembly is semantically a consumer of mod-discovery's expertise fingerprints and graph features, not of a kernel primitive. Either the shared graph/expertise surface must be promoted INTO the kernel (making the kernel fatter and the 'thin kernel' claim false), or modules will import each other in practice.
  - *Why:* This is the central modularity claim of the whole plan ('modular to the bone', LOCKED non-functional requirement). If pillar modules secretly depend on each other's domain models, blast radius is NOT minimal: changing discovery's expertise representation breaks funding, and you cannot remove discovery while keeping funding. The architecture rots exactly where the plan claims it is strongest.
  - *Fix:* Make the dependency direction explicit and acyclic. Promote the shared scholarly graph + expertise-fingerprint representation to a kernel contract (e.g. IExpertiseGraph) with its own versioned schema, OR define an inter-module event/read-model contract in shared/contracts/ that funding consumes (a published 'expertise read-model' projection), never discovery's internals. Add a CI check that fails on any import edge between modules/ packages. Document the true module DAG with directed edges and prove it is acyclic.
- **[HIGH]** The kernel is presented as 8 separate contracts (IClassifier, IModelRouter, IPolicyEnforcement, IAuditSink, IRetrieval, IPublishMetadata, IIdentityResolution, IEventBus) but they are mutually entangled and versioned as one 'kernel' unit with no independent evolution story. IModelRouter routing decisions depend on IClassifier's tier enum; IPublishMetadata depends on IClassifier output to decide what is FRE-safe; IRetrieval's fusion is 'tenant-local' and the router selects local-vs-cloud per tier inside the retrieval flow; IPolicyEnforcement consumes classification attributes. A change to the tier model (e.g. adding a 4th tier, or sub-classifying confidential by export regime) ripples through classifier, router, publish-metadata, authz, AND audit simultaneously. Section 5.6 says 'kernel contracts are semver; modules pin a compatible major' but says nothing about how the EIGHT kernel contracts version relative to EACH OTHER.
  - *Why:* A kernel where the classification tier enum is a shared type threaded through 5 contracts is a distributed monolith, not a modular kernel. The single most likely real-world change — refining data classification (the plan itself flags export-control sub-tiers, PHI, residency) — forces a coordinated multi-contract release. That is lockstep coupling masquerading as 8 clean interfaces.
  - *Fix:* Decouple the tier model from a hardcoded enum into a versioned classification *capability descriptor* that router/publish/authz query rather than switch on. Specify intra-kernel contract compatibility rules (which kernel contracts may change independently vs must move together) and publish a kernel-contract dependency matrix. Consider splitting the kernel into 'policy kernel' (classification/authz/audit — must be coherent) vs 'retrieval/serving kernel' (router/retrieval — can evolve faster) with a narrow, stable boundary between them.
- **[HIGH]** The 'provider-agnostic model router stays swappable' claim is undermined by leaking classification and key-custody semantics into the router. Section 8.2 makes the router enforce 'confidential => in-boundary local only, network egress blocked, per-tenant key-scoped so cloud routing is impossible' AND host guardrails (PII filter, prompt-injection, output classification) AND be 'router-aware' so the eval judge LLM must be the local model on the confidential tier. The router is simultaneously: a routing policy engine, a compliance enforcement point, a guardrail pipeline, and an eval-coupling point. That is four responsibilities in one 'swappable' component. Swapping the router now means re-implementing compliance egress enforcement and guardrails.
  - *Why:* LOCKED requirement: 'provider-agnostic model router ... ALSO supports bring-your-own provider/keys'. A router that has absorbed egress-blocking, key-scoping, guardrails, and eval-judge selection is not provider-agnostic or swappable — it is a god-object on the confidential critical path. Adding a new provider (the locked BYO requirement) forces re-verification of compliance enforcement, which is exactly the high-stakes path you least want to churn.
  - *Fix:* Separate concerns: (a) IModelRouter = pure provider selection given (tier, tenant policy, capability needs) returning a provider handle; (b) a separate ITransportPolicy/egress-enforcement layer at the network/key boundary that is provider-independent and tested as a compliance control (the plan already has egress-block CI tests — make THAT the enforcement, not the router); (c) guardrails as a pluggable IGuardrail chain composed around any provider. Then 'swap the router' truly means swap selection logic only.
- **[HIGH]** Contract versioning has no concrete consumer-contract or compatibility-testing mechanism, so 'minimal blast radius' is aspirational. Section 5.6 / 15.3 say kernel contracts are semver, breaking changes get 'a deprecation window where both are served', events carry schema versions, and there are 'contract tests against shared/contracts/'. But there is no mention of consumer-driven contract testing (e.g. Pact), no per-consumer compatibility matrix, no mechanism to know WHICH modules still depend on a deprecated contract version before retiring it, and 'both are served' implies running two implementations of a kernel service simultaneously with no detail on state/auth coherence across versions.
  - *Why:* Without machinery that proves a contract change is backward-compatible against the actual set of consumers, teams default to fear-driven lockstep deploys ('change everything together to be safe'). That is precisely how interface-rich architectures rot into distributed monoliths. The plan asserts blast radius is minimal but provides no falsifiable way to verify it.
  - *Fix:* Specify consumer-driven contract testing (provider verifies against recorded consumer contracts) as a CI gate. Maintain a machine-readable contract-version usage registry (which module pins which kernel-contract major/minor) so a deprecation can be safely retired only when zero consumers remain. Define exactly what 'both served' means for stateful kernel services (authz, audit) — dual-write/dual-read semantics, or a translation shim — because serving two versions of the authz spine has security implications, not just compatibility ones.
- **[HIGH]** IRetrieval is claimed to be a 'thin clean interface over LlamaIndex' but the plan piles tenant-local fusion weights, MRL truncation, two embedding spaces (Qwen3 + SPECTER2), reranking, Adaptive-RAG difficulty routing, HippoRAG2 PPR, CRAG corrective loops, and per-tenant learned convex fusion behind it (Sections 9.1-9.4). These are not implementation details hidden behind one interface — they have divergent inputs/outputs (a corrective loop re-queries; PPR needs the graph; learned fusion needs click feedback state; difficulty routing changes control flow). A single IRetrieval signature cannot stay 'thin' across single-shot hybrid AND multi-hop graph AND corrective-loop AND multi-agent decomposition without becoming a leaky catch-all.
  - *Why:* LOCKED requirement: retrieval must stay swappable/pluggable. If IRetrieval is thin, it cannot express PPR/CRAG/learned-fusion; if it is rich enough to express them, it is no longer thin and swapping the engine means re-implementing all of it. Either way the abstraction leaks, and the 'defer the rest as pluggable modules behind IRetrieval' promise (9.4) is unsubstantiated — the plan never shows the interface tolerates these as drop-ins.
  - *Fix:* Define IRetrieval as a small stable core (query -> ranked passages with scores+provenance) and model the advanced behaviors as a composable RETRIEVAL PIPELINE of stages (retriever -> fuser -> reranker -> corrective-loop -> graph-augment), each its own micro-interface, so CRAG/PPR/learned-fusion are pipeline stages not IRetrieval reimplementations. State-bearing concerns (per-tenant learned fusion weights, click feedback) must be an explicit IFeedbackStore dependency, not hidden inside a 'thin' retriever. Show the worked interface for at least the single-shot and the corrective-loop case to prove the seam holds.
- **[HIGH]** The federation seam — explicitly the existential risk (#1) — has no testable contract for what 'publishable metadata only' means, so the most important boundary in the system is unenforceable by construction. Section 4.3/10.2/15.3 rely on 'only FRE-safe / explicitly-shared records flow out via Outbox+CDC' and 'golden-path leak tests' that 'confidential bytes never appear in the outbox'. But the determination of what is publishable is made by IClassifier + per-record tier, and the Outbox is written by the Dagster pipeline (Section 10.1, CLASSIFY -> OUTBOX). There is no schema-level guarantee, no allowlist of publishable field projections, no type-system separation between a 'confidential record' and its 'publishable projection'. A new module or a schema change that adds a field to the outbox event can silently leak.
  - *Why:* This is a LOCKED requirement ('confidentiality is a first-class enforced concept end to end') and the plan's own #1 existential risk. 'Tests assert confidential bytes never appear' is a negative test against an open-ended space — it can only catch known leak shapes, never prove absence. Maintainability angle: every future field added to any entity is a potential leak, so the blast radius of ANY data-model change extends to the federation seam. That is the opposite of minimal blast radius.
  - *Fix:* Make publishability a structural type, not a runtime tag: define an explicit PublishableProjection schema per entity in shared/contracts/ that is an allowlist of fields; the Outbox writer can ONLY emit PublishableProjection objects, constructed via a total function from the internal record (fields default to non-published). Then 'no confidential leak' becomes a type guarantee plus a single audited projection function, not an infinite negative test surface. Add a CI gate that any new field on an internal entity is non-publishable unless explicitly added to the projection with a classification review.
- **[MEDIUM]** Two orchestrators (Dagster + Temporal, Section 10.1) with overlapping responsibilities create a maintainability seam the plan hand-waves as 'complementary, not redundant'. The revocation fast-path appears in BOTH worlds: Temporal owns 'revocation fast-path' (10.1 REVWF) while Section 10.2/14.4 say revocation propagates on a 'separate fast path' enforced at read time, and the authz spine (SpiceDB tuple delete) is the actual mechanism. So revocation logic is potentially spread across SpiceDB (tuple delete), Temporal (workflow), and read-time PEP re-check (kernel). Ownership of the single most security-critical operation is diffuse.
  - *Why:* When the most security-sensitive operation (revocation) spans three subsystems with no single source of truth, every change to revocation semantics touches three codebases and three teams' mental models. That is a rot magnet and an incident risk. 'Complementary' is asserted but the responsibility split (which orchestrator owns what, what is purely kernel) is never drawn as a boundary.
  - *Fix:* Draw an explicit responsibility map: SpiceDB tuple delete is the authoritative revocation; read-time PEP re-check is the enforcement guarantee (makes correctness latency-independent, as the plan notes); Temporal should only orchestrate SIDE EFFECTS of revocation (notifications, downstream cleanup, key-rotation), never be on the correctness path. State this as an invariant so no one later puts revocation correctness inside a Temporal workflow.
- **[MEDIUM]** Edition packaging via 'config toggles, not forks' is asserted but the soft-isolated shared cell (Lab/PLG) vs dedicated cell vs self-hosted node is a fundamentally different ISOLATION POSTURE, not a feature flag. Section 2.3 admits PLG runs a 'managed shared-control, soft-isolated cell' while Institution runs 'dedicated cell OR self-hosted'. The confidentiality enforcement model (per-tenant keys, egress block, local PEP/PIP executing in-boundary) is architecturally different when multiple tenants share a cell. A 'toggle' cannot turn a shared cell into a structurally-isolated one.
  - *Why:* The GTM requirement is one product via config, but if the lowest tier weakens the structural isolation that the entire confidentiality story (LOCKED) rests on, then either (a) PLG cannot honestly carry 'confidential' tier — which Open Question #4 admits — or (b) you maintain two isolation codepaths, which is a fork in all but name. This couples the GTM model to the security model in a way that will produce divergent codepaths over time.
  - *Fix:* Decide and state plainly: the confidential tier is UNAVAILABLE on shared/soft-isolated cells (PLG = public+private only), so isolation posture is a deployment substrate choice, not a per-feature toggle, and the SAME enforcement code runs everywhere — it simply has no confidential data to guard on PLG. Make 'tier availability' a property derived from isolation posture, enforced in one place, rather than letting PLG claim confidential support. This keeps it one codebase honestly.
- **[MEDIUM]** The plan picks a co-located Postgres for confidential+private data, Apache AGE (graph) on Postgres, and pgvector as a fallback (Sections 4.2, 6.2, 12) — co-locating authz-adjacent data, graph, and vectors in one engine per cell. This is operationally simple but couples three independently-scaling concerns (OLTP rows, graph traversal, ANN vector search) into one failure/scaling domain per tenant, while ALSO using Qdrant + OpenSearch as separate stores. So a cell runs Postgres+AGE+pgvector(maybe)+Qdrant+OpenSearch — and the data model is split across them with no stated consistency contract between, e.g., the AGE graph and the Qdrant index after an update.
  - *Why:* Maintainability: when an entity is updated, it must be consistently reflected across Postgres (rows), AGE (graph nodes/edges), Qdrant (vectors), and OpenSearch (BM25). The plan's only consistency mechanism is the Dagster pipeline + Outbox, but that is for OUTBOUND publish, not intra-cell store coherence. Divergence between the BM25 index, vector index, and graph is a classic rot source that silently degrades retrieval quality with no failing test.
  - *Fix:* Define an intra-cell consistency contract: a single ingestion transaction boundary or a per-cell outbox that fans out to Qdrant/OpenSearch/AGE with idempotent, replayable indexers and a reconciliation/repair job. State which store is the system of record (Postgres) and that all derived indexes are rebuildable from it. Reconsider whether AGE-on-Postgres + separate Qdrant + separate OpenSearch is worth three index-sync paths, or whether OpenSearch's hybrid BM25+vector collapses two of them.
- **[MEDIUM]** IClassifier is the single most load-bearing kernel contract (every differentiator 'hangs off classification', Section 8.1) yet its interface is never specified beyond 'classifies every record as public|private|confidential and emits the routing attribute'. Classification is treated as a pure per-record function, but real classification needs context (who is asking, project membership, IRB/DUA status, export regime, residency) — the plan even lists all these as ABAC attributes in Section 7.2. There is ambiguity about whether classification is a static record property (set at ingest) or a contextual decision (evaluated per access). The data model (Section 6.1 DOCUMENT.tier) implies static; the routing/authz flows imply contextual.
  - *Why:* If the most foundational contract conflates a static stored tier with a contextual access decision, every downstream consumer (router, publish-metadata, authz) inherits the ambiguity, and reconciling it later is a kernel-wide change — the exact retrofit the plan warns against (Risk #7). This is where the architecture rots from the center.
  - *Fix:* Split the concept explicitly: (a) record-intrinsic classification (immutable-ish tier stored on DOCUMENT, set at ingest, drives the model-router egress decision) vs (b) contextual access authorization (ReBAC+ABAC at the PEP, per request). Specify IClassifier's signature and whether it is invoked at ingest only or also at access time, and make clear the model router keys off (a) while the PEP keys off (a)+(b). Pin this in shared/contracts/ before any module is built.
- **[LOW]** The plan inherits TigerBuddy's pytest marker idiom (-m unit / -m integration, Section 15.3) and FastAPI/Dagster idioms as 'TigerBuddy idiom', but the existing codebase it learns from has a 617-line query_engine.py and a hybrid_retriever.py that directly imports the concrete VectorStore (from ..database.vector_store import VectorStore) — i.e. the predecessor has exactly the tight coupling the new plan claims to avoid. The plan never acknowledges that the inspiration codebase violates the abstraction the plan depends on, so there is no stated discipline for preventing the same drift (concrete imports creeping past the IRetrieval seam).
  - *Why:* Architectures rot precisely because the easy path (import the concrete class) is always available. The predecessor demonstrates the team's actual default behavior under deadline. Without an enforced dependency rule, the new platform will reacquire the same coupling, and 'thin interface over LlamaIndex' becomes 'LlamaIndex types leak through IRetrieval everywhere'.
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit architectural-fitness-function CI gate (e.g. import-linter / dependency-cruiser equivalent for Python) that forbids: modules importing modules, anything importing a concrete store/engine outside its kernel adapter, and LlamaIndex/Qdrant/SpiceDB SDK types appearing in shared/contracts/. Encode the 'shared/contracts is the law' rule as a machine check, not a convention, given the predecessor shows conventions were not honored.
- **[LOW]** IEventBus (Outbox + CDC + schema registry) is listed as a kernel contract, but Debezium/Kafka CDC is an operational pattern, not a clean swappable interface — and the plan couples module async communication, cross-tenant publish, AND revocation fast-path to it. If IEventBus is truly an interface, swapping Kafka/Redpanda/Debezium for something else (e.g. on a constrained self-hosted/air-gapped sovereign node, Section 13.2) is non-trivial because schema-registry semantics and CDC log-based capture are Debezium-specific. The sovereign air-gapped edition may not be able to run the same event substrate.
  - *Why:* The self-hosted/air-gapped sovereign node is a locked deployment target (export-controlled labs). If the event substrate assumes Debezium-on-Postgres-WAL + Kafka, the air-gapped node either runs a divergent substrate (a fork) or imports operational complexity it cannot sustain. The 'one architecture everywhere' claim weakens at the hardest deployment edge.
  - *Fix:* Verify and state that the Outbox pattern's consumer contract (idempotent, versioned events) is substrate-independent, and provide a lightweight event-transport profile for constrained/air-gapped nodes (e.g. polling the outbox table directly, no Kafka) behind the same IEventBus contract. Confirm the schema-registry dependency is optional/embeddable for sovereign nodes so the architecture genuinely stays constant across substrates.

### Lens: Product, GTM & business viability

*Verdict:* This is a well-researched plan with a genuinely defensible technical white-space thesis (confidential cross-institution federation + research-native AI), and the editions-as-config-toggles framing is the right way to satisfy the locked "one product, four buyers" requirement. The competitive map is thorough and the "integrate don't displace" instinct is sound. But the GTM core has a load-bearing crack: the MVP wedge it actually ships (single-tenant lit-intelligence + discovery over one lab's own corpus) delivers ZERO of the federation/confidentiality value that is the entire moat — it lands the company directly in the most commoditized, most outfunded arena (public-literature AI vs Elicit/Consensus/Scholar-QA) while claiming the moat as a deferred Phase-2 bet. The beachhead buyer economics are likely upside-down (a $179-499/mo lab cannot amortize a dedicated confidential GPU cell), the PLG-below-committee motion is contradicted by the day-one HECVAT/SOC2/data-governance burden the same buyer triggers, and the cold-start of the network effect is hand-waved as "single-tenant pillars deliver standalone value" without confronting that those standalone pillars are precisely where the company is weakest and least differentiated. The plan is honest about several of these in Section 19, but listing a fatal unit-economics question as "Open Question #4" does not de-risk it — it means the central revenue thesis is unvalidated. Revenue-bearing? Plausibly, at low ACV with brutal churn risk. Moat-bearing at the point of first revenue? No.

- **[CRITICAL]** The MVP wedge delivers none of the moat and lands the company in the single most commoditized, outfunded arena. The plan's own positioning (Section 1.4, 2.5) repeatedly says 'don't lead with public-literature AI — commoditized, outfunded.' Yet the actual MVP (Section 3.3) is exactly that: single-tenant grounded Q&A + semantic search + expert discovery over one lab's own corpus, public+private tiers, confidential merely 'defined in schema + demoed.' At first revenue the product IS a single-tenant RAG-over-papers tool competing head-on with Elicit, Consensus, SciSpace, Scholar-QA, Undermind — all better-funded, public-data specialists. The federation/confidentiality moat (the entire reason this company exists) is deferred to Phase 2, gated on N>=2 nodes. So the company earns its first dollars in the arena it explicitly says it must avoid, with no differentiation, and asks customers to pay for a worse version of free/cheap incumbents while the actual value proposition is a promise.
  - *Why:* A wedge that earns revenue but in a commoditized, undifferentiated space produces high churn, no pricing power, and no signal that the real product works. It validates the wrong thing. Worse, it burns the runway and team months building lit-intelligence parity instead of proving the confidential-federation thesis that justifies the raise. If a fundable narrative is 'we own confidential cross-institution federation,' the MVP must demonstrate that, not RAG-over-PDFs.
  - *Fix:* Re-cut the MVP so first revenue is tied to differentiated value, not commoditized parity. Two credible options: (a) Lead with the confidential single-lab local-model-isolation as a FUNCTIONAL product, not a demo — sell 'your confidential research corpus, AI Q&A, provably never leaves your boundary' to one security-sensitive lab where no public-tool competitor can play; price the security guarantee, not the RAG. (b) Skip true PLG and land a single 2-node consortium design partner (two collaborating labs at different institutions who already co-author) so the federation seam is exercised and de-risked at first revenue. Either way, do not let first revenue come from undifferentiated public-literature Q&A.
- **[CRITICAL]** Beachhead unit economics are likely inverted and the plan knows it but files it as Open Question #4 instead of resolving it pre-commit. The beachhead is a department/lab at $179-499/mo (Section 3.4), but the confidential value prop requires a local GPU running vLLM (Section 16.1 explicitly names confidential local GPU as 'the COGS driver'). A dedicated isolated cell with a GPU sufficient for an 8B+ embedding model plus a served LLM cannot be delivered for ~$300/mo gross — a single always-on cloud GPU instance alone exceeds that several times over. The plan's escape hatch is 'shared-cell soft-isolation for PLG,' but it then concedes (Open Q#4) this 'may not satisfy confidential claims' — which collapses the differentiation back into the commodity-RAG problem above.
  - *Why:* If the beachhead is sold at a price that cannot cover its own COGS without abandoning the confidentiality guarantee, then either the company loses money on every beachhead customer or it ships a product indistinguishable from commodity public RAG. There is no price point in the $179-499 band that simultaneously (a) clears below the procurement-committee threshold, (b) funds a dedicated confidential GPU cell, and (c) preserves the 'never leaves your boundary' claim. This is the revenue thesis, and it is structurally unproven.
  - *Fix:* Resolve this BEFORE building, not after. Concretely: (1) Build a real per-cell COGS model for the smallest viable confidential configuration (smallest acceptable local LLM + embedding + vector RAM + Postgres/AGE/OpenSearch) on actual cloud GPU pricing; (2) If COGS > ~$300/mo, the PLG-confidential beachhead is dead — pivot the beachhead to a higher-ACV institutional or grant-funded buyer (an export-controlled lab or a center with a security-line-item budget) where $2-5k+/mo is defensible and the GPU amortizes; (3) Investigate whether the confidential local model can run on the customer's own hardware (the M4 Max / on-prem path) at PLG tier, shifting COGS to the buyer, and whether that is operationally tolerable for a self-serve motion.
- **[HIGH]** The PLG 'below-committee-threshold' motion directly contradicts the day-one data-governance and security burden the plan itself imposes. Section 2.5 and Risk #4 stake PLG land on staying below the procurement committee (credit card / dept PO). But the moment a PLG lab ingests its OWN internal/private corpus (the actual pitch), the institution's data-governance, IT security, and IRB/research-compliance functions are triggered — FERPA exposure if student data is in the corpus, DPA requirements under GDPR (the plan itself names the university as controller / vendor as processor in 11.3), and IT review for any tool touching institutional data. 'A researcher pays $300 on a department card and uploads the lab's confidential corpus to a third-party SaaS' is precisely the shadow-IT pattern university CISOs exist to stop. The plan's own veto-holder persona (Research IT / CISO, Section 2.1) will block exactly this.
  - *Why:* The PLG motion is the foundation of the GTM sequence (land -> expand). If self-serve PLG with real institutional data is structurally incompatible with how universities govern data, the 'land below committee' step cannot happen, and the whole sequence collapses to a top-down institutional sale from day one — which has a 6-18 month cycle the lean team and runway are not scoped for.
  - *Fix:* Be explicit about what data a PLG buyer can use without triggering institutional review. Realistically PLG can only safely operate on (a) public literature and (b) the individual researcher's OWN materials (their drafts, their notes) — NOT institutional/private records. If so, the PLG tier is again commodity public RAG (see critical #1). Either (1) accept PLG is public-tier-only and find differentiation elsewhere for that tier, or (2) accept that the confidential pitch is inherently a top-down institutional sale and scope runway/team/cycle-length accordingly. Do not market a self-serve confidential-corpus PLG motion that CISOs will reflexively block.
- **[HIGH]** Network-effect cold-start is asserted, not solved — and the proposed bridge (single-tenant standalone value) is the company's weakest, least-differentiated surface. The moat (Section 1.5) is explicitly 'worthless single-tenant, defensible at N>=2.'
