The mitigation for the chicken-and-egg (Risk #9, Open Q#3) is 'single-tenant pillars deliver standalone value before exchange flips on.' But those single-tenant pillars are lit-intelligence and intra-institution discovery — precisely the commoditized space (critical #1) where the company is weakest. There is no concrete plan for HOW the first 2 nodes get connected: which two institutions, why they'd connect, what the first cross-institution share is, or how the sharing-grant friction (legal DUA, both CISOs, both research offices agreeing) gets overcome for the very first edge. Cross-institution data sharing between universities is notoriously slow (DUAs take months); the plan needs the SECOND node live and a legal sharing agreement executed before ANY network value appears.
  - *Why:* Marketplaces/networks die in the cold-start. Here the cold-start is worse than a typical two-sided market because each new edge requires bilateral legal agreements between risk-averse institutions, not just a signup. If the bridge to N>=2 is 'sell undifferentiated single-tenant value first,' and that value can't sustain revenue (Open Q#3 admits this is unvalidated), the company may never reach the N where its actual product exists.
  - *Fix:* Design the cold-start explicitly. (1) Identify a seed dyad/cluster that ALREADY collaborates and already has a DUA or master agreement (e.g., an existing NSF/NIH multi-site center, a university-system or alliance like a CIC/Big Ten Academic Alliance, a CTSA hub) — sell into that pre-existing collaboration so the legal seam is pre-solved and N>=2 is the FIRST deployment, not a later phase. (2) Make the first cross-institution use case one that needs zero confidential sharing to demonstrate value (public-tier federated expert discovery across the two members), then upsell confidential workspaces. (3) Treat consortia (a locked buyer) as the cold-start solution, not a Phase-3 monetization afterthought.
- **[HIGH]** Pricing benchmarks are borrowed from non-analogous products and the multi-axis model (base + per-active-user-per-module + exchange-add-on + BYO premium) creates quote complexity that fights the PLG self-serve motion. The plan anchors institutional pricing to Starmind (~$4k/mo base) and Interfolio (~$28k/yr) and department pricing to Instrumentl/Atom (~$179-499/mo) — but Starmind is an enterprise expertise-network, Interfolio is faculty-activity-reporting, Atom is a public-data grant tool; none of them carry a per-tenant confidential GPU cell as COGS, so their price points say nothing about what THIS product must charge to be viable (see critical #2). Separately, a four-axis pricing model is a sales-engineering and quoting burden incompatible with credit-card PLG; PLG requires a single, legible, self-serve price.
  - *Why:* Pricing anchored to the wrong comparables understates required price and overstates achievable margin. A complex multi-axis quote model cannot be sold self-serve, undermining the PLG motion that is the foundation of the GTM sequence. Mispriced + hard-to-quote = slow sales and negative-margin customers.
  - *Fix:* (1) Derive price bottom-up from the real confidential-cell COGS (critical #2), then validate against willingness-to-pay in beachhead interviews — do not anchor on incumbents in different categories. (2) For PLG, define ONE flat self-serve price with a fixed module bundle; reserve the multi-axis configurable pricing for the institution/consortium sales-assisted motions only. (3) Validate (per Open Q#12) that the per-active-user metric is even acceptable to research buyers, who frequently resist per-seat models for fluctuating lab populations and prefer FTE-capped site licenses.
- **[MEDIUM]** Differentiation vs the named AI grant fast-follower (Atom Grants) is asserted as a 'structural moat they cannot bolt on cheaply,' but the moat only exists in pillars (confidential workspaces, federation) that don't ship until Phase 2/3 — while Atom already has '50+ institutions' and is in the RD offices TODAY. By the time this plan reaches pillar 4 (grant team assembly, Phase 3), Atom has multi-year incumbency, RD-office relationships, and could add a 'private mode' or partner for confidentiality. 'They can't bolt it on cheaply' is an engineering claim, not a market claim — incumbents with distribution routinely ship 'good enough' versions of a challenger's differentiator before the challenger reaches the same buyers.
  - *Why:* The plan sequences the moat-bearing pillars LAST (Phase 3), giving the named fast-follower years of unobstructed land-grab in the exact buyer (RD office) and pillars (1+4) where the plan eventually wants to compete. Distribution beats architecture in slow-moving institutional markets.
  - *Fix:* Either (1) bring a thin slice of the differentiated capability (confidential or federated) forward to coincide with the first RD-office conversation so the moat is demonstrable while Atom is still public-only, or (2) explicitly choose NOT to compete with Atom on pillars 1+4 early and instead win a buyer/pillar where there is no incumbent (confidential workspaces, pillar 3) — then expand into grants from a position of installed-base strength rather than entering Atom's turf late and behind.
- **[MEDIUM]** Procurement friction is acknowledged (HECVAT 321 questions, SOC2, DPA) but the timeline and cost are treated as a checklist, not a gating constraint on the revenue plan. SOC2 Type II is 'started day one, achieved Phase 1' — but Type II requires an observation window (typically 6-12 months) of operating controls, meaning no SOC2 Type II report exists until ~a year in, and the plan itself says SOC2 'gates the first institutional contract.' Combined with HECVAT and per-institution DPA negotiation, the realistic time-to-first-institutional-revenue is likely 12-18+ months, but the lean 3-eng/1-GTM team and implied runway are scoped as if institutional revenue follows shortly after the PLG land.
  - *Why:* If institutional revenue (the only revenue that can cover the confidential-cell COGS, per critical #2) cannot legally close until a SOC2 Type II window completes and per-institution DPAs are negotiated, then the company must survive 12-18 months on PLG revenue that critical #1-3 argue is undifferentiated, possibly negative-margin, and possibly blocked by the same CISOs. The financing/runway plan is implicitly built on a revenue ramp that the procurement reality contradicts.
  - *Fix:* Build an explicit time-to-revenue model per buyer that incorporates SOC2 Type II observation-window timing, HECVAT turnaround, and DPA negotiation cycles. If institutional revenue is realistically 12-18 months out, the seed raise and team plan must be sized to bridge that on either (a) genuinely differentiated, margin-positive PLG/design-partner revenue, or (b) a pre-existing-consortium design-partner contract (see high #3/#4) that can be sold under an existing master agreement, short-circuiting fresh procurement.
- **[MEDIUM]** Free, well-funded incumbents in the public-federation and public-AI tiers cap willingness-to-pay for the MVP and are under-weighted. VIVO/CTSAsearch already federates expert discovery across 64 institutions for free (OSS, public-tier). OpenAlex/ORCID are free. Elicit/Consensus have generous free tiers. The MVP (public+private discovery + lit Q&A) competes against these on the public-tier surface where customers can get 80% of the value at $0. The plan treats VIVO as 'lacking AI and confidential tier' but does not confront that for the public-tier MVP use cases, 'free and good enough' is the real competitor, and AI grounded Q&A over public papers is increasingly a free commodity feature.
  - *Why:* If the MVP's differentiated value (confidential/federated) is deferred, the shipping product competes on a surface where the price ceiling is set by free incumbents. That makes the PLG revenue thesis even shakier and reinforces critical #1: the company is selling a paid version of free.
  - *Fix:* Explicitly map each MVP capability against the free incumbent that already provides it (VIVO for expert discovery, Elicit/Consensus for public Q&A, OpenAlex for graph) and articulate the specific, demonstrable increment a buyer pays for at MVP. If the honest answer is 'the increment is confidential-corpus support,' then confidential must be functional at MVP (critical #1), not demoed — otherwise there is no paid wedge.
- **[LOW]** The single-SKU-serves-four-buyers requirement is satisfied at the architecture/packaging layer, but the plan does not address that four buyer motions require four distinct GO-TO-MARKET org capabilities the lean team cannot staff simultaneously. One product can serve all four buyers (locked requirement met), but PLG (self-serve growth/funnel), top-down institutional (enterprise field sales + security navigation), consortia (multi-party BD + legal), and campus-wide (executive selling) are four different sales engines. The plan has ONE GTM hire. Trying to run all four motions at once with one person dilutes focus — the very 'unfocused' failure the lens warns about, displaced from product to distribution.
  - *Why:* Even with one product, attempting all four sales motions early splits a one-person GTM function four ways and likely executes none well. The locked requirement is that the PRODUCT serve all four eventually via packaging — it does not require running all four MOTIONS at once.
  - *Fix:* Sequence the MOTIONS, not just the build order: pick ONE buyer motion for the wedge (the plan implies PLG, but high #2/#3 suggest a consortium/design-partner motion is more aligned with the moat), staff for that one motion, and explicitly defer the other three motions to later phases even though the product supports them. Make the packaging-vs-motion distinction explicit so 'one product, four buyers' is not misread as 'four simultaneous sales engines on a 4-person team.'

### Lens: Cost, ops & founder feasibility

*Verdict:* The plan is intellectually impressive and unusually self-aware — Section 5's modular kernel, Section 3.3's MVP wedge framing, and Section 19's open questions show real discipline, and the "build the architecture, sequence the build order" instinct is correct. It genuinely handles one feasibility concern well: it explicitly defers the expensive AI machinery (MS-GraphRAG, FHE, multi-agent, learned fusion) to later phases. BUT through a cost/ops/founder lens it commits a fatal error: it confuses "designing the full architecture" with "the MVP must instantiate the full kernel." The Phase-0 MVP as scoped (SpiceDB+Cedar two-stage PEP, audit spine, isolation primitive, model router, identity resolution, classification engine, Dagster, RAGAS-in-CI, SOC2 readiness, AGE+Qdrant+OpenSearch+Vault) is 6+ named infra systems and a SOC2 program for a single paying lab at $179-499/mo — that is 12-18 months of work for a 3-engineer team before first dollar, not an MVP. The unit economics are also likely upside-down: a dedicated confidential-tier GPU cell cannot be served profitably at PLG/department price points, and the plan's own Open Question #4 admits this is unvalidated while the entire GTM wedge depends on it. The self-host burden per university node (k8s + Vault + vLLM/GPU + SpiceDB + Postgres/AGE + OpenSearch + Qdrant + egress controls + signed-bundle updates) is a full platform-engineering team's job that university Research IT will not absorb. This is a fundable Series-A architecture being asked to ship as a seed/pre-seed MVP, and the build order does not actually de-risk cost or time-to-revenue enough.

- **[CRITICAL]** The Phase-0 'MVP wedge' is not an MVP — it mandates the entire kernel (classification engine, model router, tenant isolation primitive, SpiceDB+Cedar two-stage authz, append-only tamper-evident audit spine, identity resolution service) PLUS hybrid retrieval + reranking + RAGAS-in-CI + AGE knowledge graph + OpenAlex/ORCID ingestion + SOC2 readiness, all before the first paying lab. That is 6+ distinct infra systems (Postgres/AGE, Qdrant, OpenSearch, SpiceDB, Vault, Keycloak/CILogon) plus a compliance program, justified by the claim these primitives 'cannot be retrofitted' (Risk #7). For a single-tenant PLG lab buying lit-intelligence, most of this is dead weight at ship time.
  - *Why:* A 3-engineer team building this reaches first revenue in roughly 12-18 months, not the implied fast wedge. The 'can't be retrofitted' argument is doing enormous load-bearing work and is largely false for a single-tenant deployment: SpiceDB ReBAC, cross-tenant grants, the audit spine's tamper-evidence, and the isolation cell boundary deliver ZERO value to one lab querying its own corpus — a single-tenant app needs simple RBAC and a Postgres audit table. You can ship the INTERFACE (IPolicyEnforcement, IAuditSink) as a thin shim over trivial implementations and swap in SpiceDB/tamper-evident logging when N≥2 actually arrives, with bounded blast radius — which is exactly what the plan's own modularity model (Section 5) is supposed to enable. The plan contradicts itself: it preaches pluggable interfaces but then hard-wires production implementations of every kernel service into the MVP.
  - *Fix:* Cut Phase-0 to: thin IPolicyEnforcement over library-level RBAC (not SpiceDB), IAuditSink over an append-only Postgres table (not a tamper-evident merkle log), classification as a column + a model-router if/else (confidential->local, else cloud), hybrid retrieval+rerank, single-tenant discovery. Defer SpiceDB, Cedar, the cross-tenant grant object, the isolation cell primitive, identity-resolution-as-a-service, and SOC2 to Phase 1 when an institutional buyer actually demands them. Keep the interfaces stable; swap implementations behind them. This is the difference between shipping in 4 months and 16.
- **[CRITICAL]** Unit economics are probably inverted and the plan knows it but buries it as Open Question #4. The confidential tier REQUIRES an in-boundary GPU running vLLM (network-egress-blocked, per-tenant key-scoped). A dedicated GPU cell (even one A10G/L4 at ~$400-900/mo cloud, or an A100 far more) cannot be served at the stated PLG seat ($12-20/seat) or department band ($179-499/mo) and remain gross-margin-positive — a single department subscription would be consumed by one idle GPU before any other COGS (Qdrant RAM, Postgres/AGE, OpenSearch, SpiceDB, control-plane share, SOC2 amortization).
  - *Why:* The ENTIRE GTM wedge (Section 2.5: land PLG/department first, prove the local-model isolation demo) is built on a price point that the COGS structure likely cannot support. If the confidential local-model story is the differentiator AND it requires dedicated GPU AND the beachhead can't afford dedicated GPU, then the wedge either (a) runs at negative margin to acquire logos, burning runway, or (b) uses shared/soft-isolated GPU — which directly undermines the 'confidential data never leaves your isolated boundary' claim that is the whole moat and the strongest HECVAT answer. The plan flags this as 'the riskiest unit-economics question' but proceeds to architect everything around the wedge anyway.
  - *Fix:* Validate COGS BEFORE committing to the wedge sequence, not after. Concretely: (1) Spec the cheapest viable confidential-inference path — a small quantized model on shared CPU (no GPU) for the demo tier, or batched multi-tenant GPU with cryptographic per-tenant isolation, and prove that still satisfies 'data never leaves boundary'; (2) Reframe the MVP so the confidential tier is BYO-compute / self-host (university pays for its own GPU) at PLG, and we only charge for software — this fixes the margin problem and matches the sovereign-edition philosophy; (3) If neither works, the honest conclusion is the beachhead is NOT a price-sensitive PLG lab but a funded R1 department/institution that can absorb dedicated-cell cost, which changes the entire GTM-sequencing claim.
- **[HIGH]** The self-hosted / sovereign node operational burden is grossly understated and dumped on university Research IT. Section 13.2 hand-waves the on-prem node as 'University Research IT operates it,' but the node stack is: Kubernetes + Helm sub-charts per module + Vault (HYOK key custody) + vLLM on GPU + SpiceDB + Postgres/AGE + OpenSearch + Qdrant + Debezium/Kafka + egress-block network policy + signed-bundle (cosign) air-gapped update pulls + OpenTelemetry/Prometheus/Grafana/Loki/Tempo. That is a platform-engineering team's full-time job.
  - *Why:* Most university Research IT orgs cannot operate a multi-service Kubernetes + Kafka + Vault + GPU-inference stack with air-gapped signed updates. This either (a) makes the sovereign edition unsellable without a professional-services arm the founding team doesn't have, or (b) forces the startup to operate it remotely, which contradicts 'no confidential bytes traverse to us' and reintroduces the operational cost the on-prem model was supposed to offload. The day-2 ops (upgrades, SpiceDB schema migrations, Debezium connector failures, GPU driver issues, cert rotation) on a customer-operated air-gapped node will generate a support load that crushes a 3-person team.
  - *Fix:* Drastically shrink the self-host footprint: collapse to the fewest possible services (e.g., Postgres-with-pgvector-and-AGE as a single datastore eliminating Qdrant+OpenSearch+separate-graph for small nodes; embedded authz instead of SpiceDB; skip Kafka via Postgres-LISTEN/NOTIFY or outbox-polling for low-volume nodes). Ship the node as a single signed appliance image (Docker Compose or a k3s single-binary, not full k8s + Helm + Argo). Defer the sovereign/on-prem edition entirely out of the first 18 months — it is the highest-ops, lowest-count buyer. Sell managed cells first where YOU control the stack.
- **[HIGH]** SOC2 Type II 'started day one' is both a cost and a timeline trap the plan treats as free. SOC2 Type II requires a 6-12 month observation window (not the '3-6 mo readiness' stated), costs ~$30-60k/yr in audit + tooling (Vanta/Drata ~$10-25k/yr) + significant founder time on policies/evidence, and crucially requires the controls to actually be RUNNING during the observation window — which means the full kernel must be in production early, reinforcing the over-scoped-MVP problem.
  - *Why:* The plan correctly notes SOC2 'gates the first institutional contract,' but it is positioned as a Phase-0/Phase-1 parallel track for a pre-revenue team. SOC2 Type II evidence collection over a year, on a stack still being built, with a 3-engineer team, is a major distraction and cash drain that competes directly with shipping. The 3-6 month figure conflates Type I (point-in-time) with Type II (observation window). Getting this wrong means promising institutional buyers a report that arrives 6-9 months later than implied.
  - *Fix:* Sequence it honestly: PLG/department wedge does NOT need SOC2 (it's below committee threshold by the plan's own design — that's the whole point of the wedge). Pursue SOC2 Type I first (point-in-time, weeks not a year) to unblock early institutional conversations, then Type II only once revenue justifies the audit spend and the controls are stable. Budget the real number (~$40-70k/yr all-in) as a hard COGS/opex line in Section 16, which currently lists it only qualitatively.
- **[HIGH]** Two orchestrators (Dagster AND Temporal) plus Kafka/Redpanda plus Debezium CDC plus a schema registry is over-engineered eventing for an MVP and a permanent ops tax. Section 10.1 justifies both orchestrators as 'complementary,' but for a wedge serving a few labs, Temporal (durable cross-institution workflows spanning days-months) has NO use case until Phase 2 federation exists, and the full Outbox+Debezium+Kafka+schema-registry pipeline only matters once there's a central index to feed (also Phase 2).
  - *Why:* Running Dagster + Temporal + Kafka + Debezium + a schema registry is at least 5 stateful distributed systems to operate, monitor, upgrade, and debug — each with its own failure modes — for a team of three, in service of an MVP that has one tenant and no federation. This is precisely the 'over-engineering' the plan warns about in Risk #6 but only applies to the AI core, not the infra. Debezium/Kafka in particular is operationally heavy (connector management, offset handling, rebalancing) and is dead weight pre-federation.
  - *Fix:* MVP uses Dagster ONLY (it's the inherited idiom and covers crawl->distill->embed->index->graph). Replace the entire Outbox+Debezium+Kafka+schema-registry stack with a simple transactional-outbox-polling-to-the-central-index, introduced only in Phase 2 when the central index first exists. Introduce Temporal only when the first cross-institution grant lifecycle is built (Phase 2/3). Each of these is genuinely pluggable per the plan's own modularity claims — so defer them and prove the architecture's modularity is real rather than aspirational.
- **[HIGH]** TEE / confidential-compute as a Phase-2 commitment carries hidden cost, fragmentation, and ops burden the plan under-prices. Section 19 Open Question #8 admits Nitro (AWS-only) vs SEV-SNP/TDX (multi-cloud/on-prem) fragments the story, but the plan still bakes a TEE match-broker into the core exchange architecture (Section 4.1/4.2). TEE-enabled instances cost a premium, attestation infrastructure is non-trivial to build and operate, and a one-TEE-abstraction-over-three-substrates layer is a multi-month engineering project on its own.
  - *Why:* The TEE broker is presented as central to the cross-institution privacy story (operator-can't-read-match-inputs), but building and operating attestation across Nitro + SEV-SNP + TDX, plus the PSI implementation, is specialist work that the named 3-engineer founding team (one of whom also owns SOC2, HECVAT, k8s, IaC, BYOK/HYOK, AND TEE per Section 16.2) cannot realistically deliver alongside everything else. This is a staffing/cost mismatch: the security/infra engineer is assigned ~5 jobs.
  - *Fix:* For early federation (Phase 2), replace the TEE match-broker with a far simpler model: brokered drill-down to the owning node (which the plan already has) + plain 2-party PSI for overlap queries, with the central exchange operating under contractual + audit controls rather than hardware attestation. Add TEE only when a specific buyer's threat model demands operator-zero-trust AND pays for it. Re-staff Section 16: the security/infra role as scoped is 2-3 people, not one; either grow the team in the cost model or cut scope to match three engineers.
- **[MEDIUM]** The M4 Max 36GB dev constraint vs production divergence is acknowledged (Ollama dev / vLLM prod) but the resulting dev/prod parity gap and its hidden cost are not addressed. Confidential-tier behavior, GPU batching, vLLM-specific features (PagedAttention, continuous batching), TEE attestation, multi-cell isolation, and Kafka/Debezium all cannot be exercised on a single 36GB Mac. The team will be developing blind against prod behavior for the most expensive and risky parts of the system.
  - *Why:* A 36GB unified-memory Mac cannot host a representative confidential cell (GPU vLLM + Qdrant per-tenant RAM + Postgres/AGE + OpenSearch + SpiceDB + Vault) at any realistic scale, and certainly cannot run two cells + a control plane to test the federation seam — which is the existential Risk #1. This means the most leak-prone code paths get little local testing, pushing verification into a cloud staging environment that is itself a recurring cost line (GPU instances, multi-region control plane) absent from Section 16's cost model.
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit, budgeted cloud staging/integration environment to Section 16 as a fixed opex line (it is required to test federation and confidential routing at all). Define which behaviors are dev-testable on M4 Max (retrieval logic, classification, RRF, rerank, single-tenant flows) vs which REQUIRE cloud (vLLM batching, two-cell federation seam, egress-block enforcement, TEE) and gate the latter behind CI in cloud. Do not let the seductive 'local-first' M4 Max story hide that the confidentiality moat can only be validated in paid cloud infra.
- **[MEDIUM]** The technology stack lists ~25 named systems (Section 12), each with a fallback, and the team is three engineers. Even granting modularity, the surface area of vendoring, version management, security patching, and operational expertise across CILogon, Keycloak, SpiceDB, Cedar, Qdrant, AGE, OpenSearch, vLLM, Ollama, Qwen3-Embedding, SPECTER2, BGE-reranker, Dagster, Temporal, FastAPI, LlamaIndex, LangGraph, Vault, TEE, OpenAlex/Crossref/OpenCitations/ORCID ingestion, Debezium/Kafka, and OTel/Prometheus/Grafana/Loki/Tempo is enormous.
  - *Why:* Each system is a learning curve, an upgrade treadmill, a CVE surface, and a potential 3am page. Three engineers cannot maintain deep operational competence across 25 systems. The fallbacks double the decision surface. This is the classic seed-stage failure mode: choosing the theoretically-best tool for every layer instead of the fewest tools that cover the most layers. The plan optimizes each layer independently rather than minimizing total operational entropy.
  - *Fix:* Aggressively consolidate for the MVP: Postgres can be vector store (pgvector) + graph (AGE) + audit + RLS + outbox + BM25-ish (tsvector/ParadeDB) — collapsing Qdrant, OpenSearch, and AGE-as-separate into ONE datastore for small tenants (the plan even lists pgvector as a fallback but doesn't commit to the consolidation). Drop Temporal, Kafka/Debezium, SpiceDB, Cedar, CILogon, and TEE from MVP per prior findings. Target <8 operational systems for Phase 0. Re-introduce best-of-breed (Qdrant, OpenSearch, SpiceDB) only when scale or a buyer requirement forces it.
- **[MEDIUM]** Licensing/cost traps are well-researched on the data side (CC0 snapshots, ORCID non-commercial API, Elasticsearch AGPL avoidance) but two recurring fixed-cost lines are flagged without numbers and could gate the PLG margin: CILogon paid commercial subscription and the cumulative third-party-license/audit footprint. Section 16.1 lists them only qualitatively ('precise figures are an Open Question').
  - *Why:* For a PLG/department tier at $179-499/mo, a fixed CILogon subscription + SOC2 audit amortization + Vault Enterprise (if BYOK/HYOK needs the paid tier — Vault OSS lacks some KMS/namespace features) can individually exceed the per-customer revenue at low customer counts. 'Figures are an open question' is not acceptable for the line items that determine whether the wedge is profitable, because they are knowable today via vendor pricing pages.
  - *Fix:* Before committing to the wedge, get hard quotes/published prices for: CILogon commercial, Vault (OSS vs Enterprise feature gap for per-tenant KMS/namespaces — confirm OSS suffices or budget Enterprise), any managed-Kafka/SpiceDB-Cloud if not self-running them, and SOC2 audit + Drata/Vanta. Put real annual numbers in Section 16 and compute breakeven customer count per edition. Confirm Vault OSS (MPL-2.0, post-BSL-relicense status as of the cutoff) actually provides BYOK/HYOK envelope encryption without the Enterprise tier — this is a hallucinated-capability risk per the project's own no-fabricated-APIs constraint.
- **[MEDIUM]** The founding team in Section 16.2 is mis-sized against the architecture. Three engineers are assigned: (1) distributed systems — authz spine, isolation, exchange, CDC, Temporal; (2) ML/RAG — retrieval, router, embeddings, serving, eval; (3) security/compliance+infra — BYOK/HYOK, TEE, SOC2, HECVAT, k8s, IaC, egress. That third role alone is 2-3 specialized hires (a compliance/GRC person, a security engineer, and a platform/SRE are distinct skill sets).
  - *Why:* The plan asserts 'three engineers + one GTM + fractional counsel is the lean wedge team' and that 'the modular architecture lets later pillar modules be built by added engineers without kernel churn.' But the KERNEL itself — which Phase 0 mandates in full — requires more than three people to build correctly in a reasonable timeframe, especially the security/compliance/infra bundle. Under-staffing the kernel build is how the 'fast wedge' becomes an 18-month death march.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) grow the stated team and adjust the burn/runway expectations and fundraise accordingly, or (b) — better — cut Phase-0 scope per findings 1, 5, 6, so the reduced kernel (RBAC shim, Postgres audit table, if/else router, consolidated Postgres datastore, no TEE, no SOC2-Type-II-yet) IS buildable by three engineers. The team size and the MVP scope must be made consistent; right now they are not.
- **[LOW]** Per-tenant learned convex fusion, GNN link prediction, HippoRAG2 PPR, CRAG, and adaptive RAG are all deferred but still claimed as modules behind IRetrieval — yet each requires per-tenant training data / click feedback / gold sets that a small federation of early-stage tenants will not generate for a long time. The plan acknowledges this for fusion (Risk: needs click feedback) but presents the others as merely a sequencing choice rather than a data-availability blocker.
  - *Why:* Several Phase-2/3 'modules' are not just build-effort-deferred — they are blocked on data volume that the cost-constrained early customer base structurally won't produce. This matters for cost/feasibility because building the infrastructure to collect per-tenant relevance feedback (without confidential data leaving the cell, per Open Question #11) is itself a non-trivial project, and the roadmap implies these features arrive on a build-time schedule when they actually arrive on a data-accrual schedule.
  - *Fix:* Mark these features explicitly as data-gated, not build-gated, in the roadmap, and confirm none of them are load-bearing for any contracted deliverable. Ensure the MVP's RRF + rerank baseline is the contractually-promised quality bar, with learned improvements as upside, so no revenue depends on data that won't exist for 1-2 years.

## Review of plan2  (8 critical / 22 high)


### Lens: Distributed-systems & scalability: federation seam, control/data-plane split, consistency model, sync mechanics, bottlenecks, failure domains, hot tenants, KG + vector-index growth, cross-node latency

*Verdict:* This is a substantially more honest distributed-systems design than the v1 it critiques — the central index is correctly reclassified as first-class sharded stateful state, the confidential Check is moved cell-local to survive control-plane partition, brokered-op availability is modeled as a product not a min, hot-tenant quotas exist, and the revocation path is given three independent mechanisms with an SLO'd leak window. Those are real wins and I won't pretend otherwise. But the plan's central distributed-systems claim — that a "strongly-consistent revocation set" gates every metadata surface fast enough to treat staleness as a bounded security leak-window — is still hand-wavy at exactly the point where it matters: it asserts a <5s strongly-consistent global no-list replicated to every cell AND checked inline on the hot discovery path, without ever reconciling "strongly consistent" with "replicated to N geo-distributed cells" (those are in tension by CAP) or costing the per-query revocation-set lookup at scatter-gather fan-out. Several other load-bearing mechanisms (HLC monotonicity across independently-clocked self-hosted nodes, ordered authenticated grant replication into cells with a fail-closed staleness bound, scatter-gather discovery latency under continuous CDC ingest, the cell-local SpiceDB replica's revocation propagation) are named and SLO-tagged but their actual mechanics and failure interactions are deferred to "spikes." The design is directionally correct; the danger is that the hardest consistency problems have been relabeled as solved-by-naming. Below, ranked by severity.

- **[CRITICAL]** The 'strongly-consistent revocation set' is a global mutable no-list that the design simultaneously requires to be (a) strongly consistent, (b) replicated to every geo-distributed cell AND the central index, and (c) checked inline before surfacing every discovery hit — with a <5s propagation SLO. These properties are in direct CAP tension and the plan never reconciles them. A strongly-consistent set replicated synchronously to EU+US cells means the revoke write blocks on cross-region consensus (kills the O(1) cell-local revoke claim and the revoke-write latency), OR it is asynchronously replicated (then it is NOT strongly consistent at the cell, and a cell can serve a revoked record during the replication gap — exactly the leak the design claims to close). 'Strongly consistent' and 'replicated to cells, checked locally, <5s' cannot both hold under partition.
  - *Why:* This is the single mechanism the plan elevates to 'correctness, independent of purge lag' and stamps as the P1 security-critical control closing the v1 metadata-leak gap. If its consistency model is internally contradictory, the entire revocation-correctness story collapses back to 'eventual with a hopeful bound' — i.e., the v1 flaw the plan claims to have retracted, now hidden behind a stronger-sounding label.
  - *Fix:* Pick an explicit model and state its failure behavior. Realistic options: (1) The revocation set is the AUTHORITATIVE strongly-consistent store ONLY at the central index (single region, linearizable), and the index gate is a synchronous read against it — then cell-local replicas are a cache and the cell must fail-closed (deny drill-down) when its replica is staler than the bound, with the bound being a hard liveness check, not a wish. (2) Use a monotonically-growing revocation log with per-record version vectors so a cell can prove freshness; surfacing requires the index to have observed a version >= the record's last-known-revoke-version, else fail-closed. Either way: define what happens to in-flight discovery queries during a revoke-set partition (must be deny/degrade, not stale-serve), and cost the inline lookup (next finding).
- **[CRITICAL]** Per-record HLC monotonicity is the linchpin of revoke-ordering safety (tombstone version > resurrecting publish ⇒ reject), grant-replication monotonicity, and idempotent CDC consumers — yet the nodes generating these HLCs are independently-operated, self-hosted, possibly air-gapped sovereign cells that 'upgrade independently' and may have unsynchronized or adversarially-skewed clocks. HLC bounds skew only if clocks are loosely synced (NTP); a sovereign node with a 10-minute-fast clock can stamp a publish with an HLC that dominates a legitimate later tombstone from the central authority, resurrecting revoked metadata and defeating the ordering guarantee. The plan asserts HLC monotonicity as if it were a closed property of a single logical clock domain; it is not, across mutually-distrusting institutions.
  - *Why:* Revocation ordering safety is mechanism (b) of the three-mechanism leak defense. If a clock-skewed or malicious node can mint a dominating version, the tombstone-rejection logic becomes an attack vector for un-revoking confidential metadata — turning a safety mechanism into a leak amplifier. This is precisely the kind of cross-trust-boundary timing assumption that breaks federations.
  - *Fix:* Do not let the data-owning cell be the sole source of the version that orders its own revocation against the authority's tombstone. Anchor revoke/tombstone ordering to a version assigned by the central revocation-set authority (a single logical clock / monotonic counter the authority controls), so a tombstone always carries an authority-monotonic version a node cannot outrun. Bound and clamp accepted HLC skew (reject events from nodes beyond a max-skew window, alarm on it). State the trust model explicitly: nodes are mutually distrusting, so no node's self-asserted timestamp may order a security decision in its own favor.
- **[HIGH]** The discovery query path's latency is modeled as scatter-gather across tenant shards with bounded fan-out and an 800ms p95 SLO, but the plan adds an inline per-hit revocation-set gate AND a per-query Discovery PEP authz + discoverability-scope filter on top, and never composes these costs. At N=50-200 tenants with topic-routed shards, a single broad discovery query fans out to many shards, each returning candidate hits, EACH of which must then be (a) authz-checked by the Discovery PEP and (b) gated through the revocation set. If the revocation-set check is a remote lookup, that is potentially thousands of serialized/batched lookups per query inside an 800ms budget; if it's a local bloom/set, its memory and freshness at every query node is unaddressed. The 'partial results flagged incomplete on slow/absent shards' escape hatch also silently interacts with security: an incomplete result set could omit a hit that WOULD have been revoked-checked, but more dangerously, the design never says whether a shard that is up but whose revocation-replica is stale is treated as 'incomplete' (safe) or 'complete' (leak).
  - *Why:* Discovery is feature pillar (1) and the wedge demo. An 800ms SLO that doesn't account for per-hit security gating at fan-out scale is fiction; under load it will either blow the SLO or, worse, the team will move the revocation gate off the hot path to hit the SLO — re-opening the leak. The interaction between 'partial results' and 'stale revocation replica' is an unspecified leak surface.
  - *Fix:* Cost the gated query end to end: model fan-out width at N=50/200, hits-per-shard, and per-hit PEP+revocation cost; decide where the revocation gate lives (push it INTO the shard's index as a filtered query against a locally-materialized revocation bitmap refreshed within the leak-window, so it's one indexed filter not N lookups). Define the freshness contract for a shard: a shard whose revocation bitmap is staler than the leak-window must return 'incomplete' (fail-closed), and 'incomplete' must be a security state, not just a quality flag.
- **[HIGH]** Ordered, authenticated grant replication INTO each cell with a home-minted ZedToken and a 'fail-closed if replica stale beyond a bound' is asserted but its liveness mechanics are undefined. How does a cell KNOW its grant replica is stale beyond the bound? SpiceDB ZedTokens give you monotonic read-after-write within a cluster, not a heartbeat proving 'I have seen all grants up to wall-clock now.' To fail-closed on staleness, the cell needs a freshness oracle: either the SoR pushes a monotonic watermark the cell compares against (requires the SoR to be reachable — re-coupling confidential checks to the control plane the design fought to decouple), or the cell uses a local TTL (then a grant created/revoked during a partition is invisible and the 'survives control-plane partition' claim means it survives by serving STALE authz). For revocation specifically, a partition means the cell cannot learn of a new revoke — so 'cell-local check survives partition' is true for the grant-EXISTS case but is a LEAK for the grant-REVOKED case unless the cell fails closed on partition, which contradicts 'confidential intra/cross access continues during control-plane degradation.'
  - *Why:* This is the core tension of the whole control/data-plane split: you cannot have both 'confidential cross-institution access keeps working during a control-plane partition' AND 'revocations take effect within the leak-window during that same partition.' The plan claims both. One of them must give, and the safe choice (fail-closed on stale grant replica) directly degrades the availability the plan markets.
  - *Fix:* Make the partition tradeoff explicit and asymmetric: grant-GRANTS may be served from a cell-local replica during partition (availability), but the cell must treat its revocation knowledge as authoritative-only-if-fresh — i.e., require a recent revocation-set watermark to authorize a CONFIDENTIAL drill-down, and fail-closed (deny, return publishable-metadata-only) when the watermark is older than the leak-window. State plainly: during a control-plane/revocation-set partition, confidential cross-tenant drill-down DEGRADES TO DENY, not stale-allow. Update Section 14.3's 'confidential cross-institution access continues' to 'continues for already-granted reads only within the freshness bound; denies beyond it.'
- **[HIGH]** The central discovery index capacity model is order-of-magnitude only and omits the write-amplification reality of its own architecture. Every publishable record carries a lease/TTL and 'self-expires absent re-publish.' At 2.5M-25M projections each needing periodic re-publish to stay alive, the steady-state write load is not just 'snapshot-refresh bursts' — it is a continuous re-lease firehose proportional to corpus size / lease duration. A 24h lease on 25M records = ~290 lease-refresh writes/sec floor, BEFORE actual content changes, CDC tombstones, and re-embeddings. The plan waves at 'smoothed by per-tenant CDC partitioning' but never sizes the re-lease load, and OpenSearch is notoriously write-amplifying (segment merges) under high update churn against a vector+BM25 hybrid index. The 30-150GB storage estimate is plausible; the WRITE throughput and merge pressure are the unmodeled bottleneck.
  - *Why:* The lease mechanism is mechanism (c) of the leak defense AND a continuous cost driver. If lease duration is short (to bound leak), re-publish write load explodes; if long (to reduce writes), the lease backstop is weak and leak-window depends entirely on tombstones+revocation-set. This is a direct tension between the leak-window SLO and index write scalability that determines central-plane COGS and the 800ms discovery SLO (query latency degrades under heavy merge load).
  - *Fix:* Decouple liveness from re-write: instead of re-publishing records to refresh leases, maintain leases as a separate lightweight expiry index keyed by record-version (a single TTL store / sorted set), so refresh is a cheap timestamp bump, not a full document re-index against the hybrid engine. Size the actual write QPS at N=50/200 for tombstones + content updates + lease refresh, and state the OpenSearch merge/refresh-interval tuning. Reconcile lease duration explicitly against the leak-window SLO (lease is a backstop measured in hours; revocation-set is the fast path measured in seconds — say so).
- **[HIGH]** Cross-region confidential drill-down latency is 'budgeted explicitly' against the 4s Q&A SLO, but the budget is hand-waved as '~1-2 cross-region hops.' A US researcher drilling into an EU-resident confidential detail triggers: token mint (control plane, some region) + brokered request US→EU (cross-Atlantic ~80-150ms RTT) + EU cell-local SpiceDB Check + EU cell-local revocation-set freshness check + EU local vLLM inference (seconds, not ms, for a generated answer) + response cross-Atlantic. The plan correctly removed the cross-Atlantic ZedToken round-trip, but the GENERATED-answer case puts a multi-second TEE-backed local inference behind a cross-region broker, and 'sum against the 4s SLO' is asserted without showing the arithmetic survives when inference alone can be 2-4s on a quantized 7-8B model. Worse, the brokered-path SLO (p95 2.5s, 5s timeout) and the Q&A SLO (4s) describe overlapping flows and are never reconciled for the cross-region GENERATED case.
  - *Why:* The headline UX (grounded cross-institution Q&A) is exactly the flow that stacks cross-region network + cold-ish local inference + multi-stage authz. If the real p95 is 6-8s, the wedge demo feels broken, and there is no stated fallback (stream tokens? pre-warm? region-affinity routing?). SLOs that don't compose across the actual call graph are not SLOs.
  - *Fix:* Produce the explicit latency budget for the worst realistic flow (US subject, EU confidential data, generated answer) as a summed table: token mint + cross-region RTT×hops + cell-local Check + revocation freshness + vLLM TTFT + generation + return. Reconcile the brokered-path SLO and the Q&A SLO into ONE number for this composite flow. If it exceeds 4s, state the mitigation (token streaming so perceived latency is TTFT not full-generation; region-affinity so the requesting user is routed to compute near the data; explicit 'cross-region generated answers have a 6s SLO' honesty).
- **[MEDIUM]** Tenant-sharding the central index 'by tenant' is presented as clean isolation, but mega-R1s (MIT/Stanford, explicitly called out) with 500k+ works create severe shard skew: a per-tenant shard for a mega-R1 is orders of magnitude larger than a small college's, so the scatter-gather fan-out has a long-tail shard that dominates p95, and the 'topic-level routing within large tenants' is the real complexity (re-sharding a hot tenant) that gets one clause. Tenant-sharding also makes CROSS-tenant discovery queries (the entire point — 'who across the consortium works on X') a fan-out to ALL tenant shards, which is the worst case for scatter-gather, not the best. The shard key optimizes offboarding/quota (admin ops) at the expense of the primary read pattern (cross-tenant topical search).
  - *Why:* The dominant query is cross-tenant topical discovery, and tenant-sharding is pessimal for it: every broad query touches every shard. Combined with mega-R1 skew, the 800ms p95 is at risk from the slowest single shard on every query. This is a classic 'sharded by the wrong dimension' bottleneck.
  - *Fix:* Reconsider the shard key for the read pattern: either route by topic/embedding-cluster (co-locates topically-similar work cross-tenant, so a topical query hits few shards) with tenant as a filter+quota dimension, or use a two-tier design (a global topical ANN layer for candidate generation + per-tenant authz/scope filtering). If tenant-sharding is kept for isolation reasons, explicitly cap mega-tenant shards via topic-subsharding from day one (not 'within large tenants' as an afterthought) and model the all-shard fan-out cost for the canonical cross-consortium query.
- **[MEDIUM]** The intra-cell consistency contract (Postgres SoR; pgvector/AGE/tsvector are rebuildable derived indexes fanned out via a per-cell outbox + reconciliation/repair job) is sound in shape, but the consolidated-Postgres MVP runs the OLTP rows, the HNSW vector index, the AGE graph traversal, the tsvector BM25, the audit hash-chain, AND the outbox in ONE Postgres. The reconciliation job that 'heals BM25↔vector↔graph divergence' is itself reading and writing that same instance. Under the confidential-tier crypto requirement (ALL derivatives encrypted under tenant KEK), every index rebuild/repair must decrypt-and-re-encrypt, and HNSW rebuilds are memory+CPU heavy. On the M4 Max dev target (36GB) this is fine; in a 'smallest viable' cloud cell sized for COGS floor (~$1k/mo, single small GPU, modest RAM), a graph PPR query + an HNSW segment build + a reconciliation sweep contend for the same box with no isolation until the undefined 'split trigger.' The split trigger is named but its threshold is never given.
  - *Why:* The plan sells operational simplicity (one store) for small cells, but small cells are also the cheapest-provisioned, so resource contention bites earliest exactly where there's no headroom. An unspecified split trigger means the team discovers the contention in production under a paying design partner, not before.
  - *Fix:* Define the split trigger concretely (e.g., corpus > X docs, OR p95 Q&A breaches SLO, OR graph node count > Y, OR vector RAM > Z% of cell) and make breaching it an alert, not a postmortem. For the consolidated MVP, at minimum isolate the reconciliation/repair job and HNSW rebuilds to off-peak or a read-replica, and state the RAM budget for HNSW + AGE working set on the 'smallest viable' cell so the COGS floor isn't quietly violated by index memory.
- **[MEDIUM]** Backpressure policy says 'never drop tombstones; publish events may be shed.' This is correct in intent but creates an ordering hazard the plan doesn't address: if publishes are shed/delayed while tombstones flow, and the system uses per-record HLC versions to reject out-of-order resurrection, then a SHED-then-replayed publish carrying a version that predates a tombstone is correctly rejected — good. But a shed-then-replayed publish for a record that was NEVER tombstoned but whose newer in-cell state was also shed can leave the index serving an OLDER publishable projection indefinitely (stale-but-not-revoked). The CDC lag SLO 'bounds' this, but under sustained publish-shedding (the explicit backpressure behavior), publish lag is UNbounded by design while tombstones are prioritized. So discovery can serve arbitrarily-stale (not wrong-tier, but outdated) projections during congestion, and the 'eventual, bounded by CDC lag SLO' row in 10.4 is violated precisely when backpressure engages.
  - *Why:* The consistency table claims bounded staleness for discovery population, but the backpressure design explicitly makes publish-staleness unbounded under load. These two statements contradict. It's not a security leak (tier is preserved), but it's a correctness/freshness SLO that self-violates under the exact condition (load) where SLOs matter.
  - *Fix:* State the degraded-mode consistency honestly: under publish-shedding, discovery freshness SLO is suspended and results carry an 'as of' staleness that can exceed the nominal bound; surface this. Alternatively, shed by priority WITHIN publishes (shed re-embeddings/enrichment, keep first-publish and content-material updates) rather than all publishes uniformly, so freshness degrades gracefully. Reconcile the 10.4 table's 'bounded by CDC lag SLO' with the backpressure section's 'publish events shed.'
- **[MEDIUM]** The PSI + DP 'mandatory per-tenant-pair privacy budget' is a stateful, monotonically-depleting resource shared across a federation of mutually-distrusting parties, and the plan never says WHO holds the budget ledger or how it survives the no-centralized-confidential-data constraint. A per-pair DP budget must be tracked authoritatively somewhere both parties trust; if it lives in the control plane, it's a stateful cross-tenant consistency problem (concurrent queries from both sides racing to deplete the same budget — needs linearizable decrement, another consensus dependency); if it lives in each cell, the two cells can disagree on remaining budget and a malicious tenant can replay queries to exceed the true epsilon. Differencing attacks across the federation also span tenant PAIRS over TIME, but the budget is scoped per-pair, ignoring a colluding-coalition attacker (A and C both query B with correlated templates).
  - *Why:* DP budget is the named defense against the inference/differencing leak (Threat #13). A privacy budget with no authoritative, race-free ledger and no coalition model is a budget in name only — an attacker who can race or collude defeats it, re-opening exactly the aggregate-leak the design claims to bound.
  - *Fix:* Specify the budget ledger as a linearizable per-pair counter (the one place a small consensus dependency is justified, since PSI is already async/batch per Section 14.4 — latency-tolerant), with monotonic decrement and replay protection (query-template + nonce dedupe). Address coalition risk: cap aggregate epsilon spent against a single target tenant across ALL querying tenants, not just per-pair. Since PSI is batch/templated, this is tractable but must be designed, not assumed.
- **[LOW]** The 'continuous runtime leak assertion in production' (sample outbound records, alarm on any confidential tag at the boundary) is a SAMPLING detector on a security-critical seam. Sampling means a leak smaller than the sampling rate can pass undetected, and the alarm is post-hoc (the bytes already crossed). The plan correctly makes the PublishableProjection type the primary structural guarantee and treats the runtime assertion as defense-in-depth, which is the right framing — but it's marketed in the verdict-level invariant as if it were a hard control ('provably, via an independent egress gate').
  - *Why:* A sampling, after-the-fact detector cannot 'prove' no leak; it can only catch sustained/large leaks statistically. Overstating it risks the team trusting it as a gate rather than the type system + allowlist as the gate.
  - *Fix:* Make the egress PEP's allowlist re-validation a 100% inline blocking check (it already claims to 'strip/reject' — so it IS blocking, not sampling; reconcile the wording), and reposition the runtime sampling assertion explicitly as drift-detection/alerting, not as part of the correctness guarantee. Don't let 'continuous runtime leak assertion' carry proof weight in the headline invariant.

### Lens: Security, privacy & confidentiality: end-to-end enforcement of the public/private/confidential model, authz completeness (ABAC+ReBAC), cross-institution sharing/revocation, confidential-tier crypto/BYOK, federation confused-deputy/IDOR risks, FERPA/GDPR/export-control correctness.

*Verdict:* This is a markedly stronger security posture than a typical v1 — the federation seam is defended structurally (PublishableProjection type + independent egress PEP + runtime leak assertion), revocation has a real three-mechanism story (strongly-consistent revocation set + versioned tombstone + lease), cell-local ReBAC checks survive control-plane partition, the confused-deputy mitigation (token-bound subject resolution) is correct, ABAC is fail-closed and narrow-only, and export-control/FERPA gating is moved off SSO claims to institution attestation. Credit where due. But the plan still has real leak paths it has not closed. The most serious: (1) the "revocation set" is described as the correctness gate yet is itself an eventually-replicated artifact into cells/index — its replication failure mode is the actual leak window and is never bounded for the case where the set itself is stale or unreachable; (2) embeddings published to the central index are treated as low-risk after 256-d MRL truncation, but truncation is not a privacy mechanism and inversion of public-text embeddings still leaks corpus membership and can reconstruct text — the threat is acknowledged then dismissed with a non-mitigation; (3) the audit log, BYOK key custody, and the egress PEP all live in or depend on the control plane the operator runs, so "operator cannot see confidential data" is contradicted by who holds the KEK-unwrap path and who runs the egress PEP that sees plaintext-adjacent projections; (4) GDPR right-to-erasure via crypto-shred is structurally unsound for the central index and for federated grantee copies; (5) the prompt-injection/tier-clamp story has a hole around retrieved-content-driven tool/agent actions and the classifier being the same trust root that injection targets. These are design-level, not cosmetic. The plan is fundable but should not claim "provably so" on the seam until the revocation-set staleness bound, embedding-inversion, and operator-trust-boundary gaps are closed.

- **[CRITICAL]** The 'strongly-consistent revocation set' is the load-bearing correctness gate for the entire metadata-leak story, but it is simultaneously described as 'replicated to the index and to cells' — i.e., it is an eventually-replicated artifact. A replicated copy cannot be strongly consistent at the consumer. The plan never specifies what happens when the revocation-set REPLICA at the central index (or at a cell) is itself stale or unreachable at surface time. If the index checks its local revocation-set replica and that replica has not yet received a just-added revocation, the stale metadata IS surfaced — the exact leak the section claims to close. The '<5s leak-window SLO, P1 if exceeded' applies to purge lag, not to revocation-set replication lag, which is the real exposure.
  - *Why:* This is the platform's existential invariant ('confidential bytes / publishable projections gated on revoke'). The plan retracted v1's 'correctness independent of latency' claim as half-true, then reintroduced the same flaw one layer down: the gate is only as strongly-consistent as its slowest replica. A revoke that races a cross-region replication lag surfaces confidential-derived metadata to an unauthorized institution — a contractual and possibly regulatory breach (FERPA/GDPR/export). 'Provably so' is false while this is open.
  - *Fix:* Make the surface-time gate authoritative, not replica-based, OR bound the replica staleness as a hard fail-closed condition. Concretely: (a) the central index must treat its revocation-set replica's staleness as a security property — if the replica's last-confirmed-sync timestamp exceeds the leak-window SLO, the index must fail CLOSED (refuse to surface that tenant's projections), not serve stale; (b) for high-sensitivity discoverability scopes, require a synchronous freshness token (ZedToken-style) from the grantor cell at surface time rather than trusting the replica; (c) define and SLO revocation-set REPLICATION lag separately from purge lag, with fail-closed behavior when exceeded. State the lease/TTL as the only true backstop and size it to the worst-case replication partition, not to 5s.
- **[CRITICAL]** Embedding publication to the central index is treated as low-risk via '256-d MRL truncation' and 'only public-text embeddings,' but MRL truncation is a dimensionality/quality knob, NOT a privacy mechanism. Truncated embeddings of public text still (a) enable membership inference (which works exist in a tenant's corpus, revealing research direction), (b) support partial text reconstruction via embedding-inversion attacks, and (c) leak co-occurrence/topical structure that, combined with the expertise graph, can deanonymize confidential project participation by inference even though no confidential bytes were published. The threat is named in 11.8 then 'mitigated' by the very thing (truncation) that does not mitigate it.
  - *Why:* The central index is the aggregated honeypot across all institutions — the highest-value target in the system and operator-readable (control-plane keys, not tenant keys). 'Public-text' embeddings can still reveal that Institution A is building a corpus on a sensitive topic, and inversion research (e.g., vec2text-class attacks) reconstructs substantial text from embeddings regardless of dimension. This undermines the core marketing claim ('confidential never leaves your node, provably') because confidential RESEARCH DIRECTION and project existence leak through the public projection layer.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) do not publish raw embeddings to the central index at all — publish only opaque, salted, per-tenant-keyed locality-sensitive hashes / quantized codes usable for candidate generation but not invertible, with final scoring brokered to the owning cell; or (b) if embeddings must be published, model inversion explicitly (cite the attack class), apply per-tenant random projection/noise with a stated security parameter, and gate which fields contribute to published embeddings through the same allowlist review. Add membership-inference and inversion to the threat model (11.5) as named, tested controls, not a footnote. Stop describing MRL truncation as the privacy control.
- **[HIGH]** The operator-trust boundary is internally contradictory. The plan claims 'operator cannot see confidential data' and sells 'confidential never leaves your node,' but: (1) BYOK uses Vault that the OPERATOR runs in managed cells ('BYOK via Vault') — the operator holds or can reach the KEK-unwrap path, so 'crypto-shred' and 'university controls custody' are only true for HYOK/sovereign, which is deferred 18 months; (2) the independent egress PEP runs in the data plane the operator operates and inspects PublishableProjections (which can be derived from confidential source) before emission; (3) TEE-backed inference is the stated mitigation for plaintext-at-use but TEE is deferred to 'buyer demand,' so for the entire MVP+Phase-1 window confidential plaintext sits in operator-run cell memory with only swap/coredump disabled. The honest plaintext-at-use admission in 11.1 is good, but the GTM/marketing claims in 1.1/1.3/1.5 ('provably,' 'never leaves') are not qualified to match.
  - *Why:* For managed cells (the actual MVP and Phase-1 deployment model), the operator is in the TCB for confidential data: holds key-unwrap, runs the egress gate, runs the inference host without a TEE. A CISO doing a real threat model (the named veto-holder persona) will find that 'confidential never leaves your node' collapses to 'confidential never leaves the vendor-operated cell that the vendor can read,' which is a materially weaker and arguably misrepresented claim. This is a sales-integrity and breach-liability problem.
  - *Fix:* Separate the claim per deployment model explicitly: managed-cell = 'vendor-operated isolation + per-tenant keys + egress gate; vendor is a processor with technical+contractual controls; NOT operator-zero-trust until TEE' ; sovereign/HYOK = 'operator-zero-trust.' Move HYOK key custody and TEE-backed inference EARLIER for any tenant whose threat model is operator-zero-trust (export-controlled labs in the fallback wedge demand exactly this on day one). Do not use the word 'provably' for the managed-cell tier. Add operator-insider to the threat model with the residual-risk statement.
- **[HIGH]** GDPR right-to-erasure is reconciled solely via crypto-shred, but crypto-shred does not reach two surfaces the plan itself created: (1) the central discovery index holds PublishableProjections + embeddings encrypted under CONTROL-PLANE keys (distinct from tenant KEK, per 4.6) — revoking a tenant KEK does NOT shred control-plane-encrypted central-index data, so an erasure request for a data subject's published projection/profile is not satisfied by crypto-shred and requires an explicit index-purge path that is only described for revocation, not for per-data-subject erasure; (2) federated grantee copies in other institutions' cells are only 'contractually' obligated to shred (4.4 admits this), so a data subject's erasure right cannot be technically guaranteed across the federation. GDPR Art. 17 erasure and Art. 19 notification-to-recipients obligations are not mapped.
  - *Why:* The plan markets GDPR-processor posture and 'compliance designed in from day one' as a sales weapon. An erasure request that the architecture cannot fully honor (central index + cross-tenant copies) is a direct Art. 17/19 gap. The control-plane-key choice for the central index — made for a good reason (tenant offboarding) — actively breaks the crypto-shred erasure story for personal data in profiles/projections.
  - *Fix:* Define a per-data-subject erasure workflow distinct from tenant-KEK shred: (a) targeted hard-delete + tombstone of that subject's projections/embeddings in the central index (reuse the revocation tombstone path but keyed by data-subject, not by grant); (b) implement Art. 19 recipient-notification: when a subject is erased, propagate erasure obligations (and ideally technical revoke) to every grantee cell that received a copy, logged in the per-tenant audit; (c) state plainly which erasure guarantees are technical vs contractual, per surface. Map FERPA's analogous correction/access rights too.
- **[HIGH]** The classifier is the single trust root for the entire tier model AND is a probabilistic LLM-adjacent component that is itself a target of prompt injection — yet the two defenses are not composed. 'Fail-closed default = confidential' protects against low-confidence MISSING tiers, but does NOT protect against a confidently-WRONG downgrade: an adversarial document (or a prompt-injection payload embedded in ingested content) that manipulates the classifier into high-confidence 'public' would route confidential content to a cloud frontier model and into the publishable projection. The injection-corpus test only covers exfiltration during synthesis, not classification-time manipulation. Section 8.1 even cites the classifier→route binding as THE kernel differentiator, making this the highest-value attack surface, defended by one probabilistic component.
  - *Why:* A single high-confidence misclassification is a catastrophic leak: confidential text egresses to a cloud provider (export-control/GDPR/contract breach) and is published to the central honeypot. Fail-closed-on-uncertainty does nothing against confident-wrong, and the plan's own derived-tier MAX rule depends on the source tier being correct in the first place. The independent egress PEP is named as the 'second gate,' but it validates against the allowlist of FIELDS, not against a re-derivation of the SOURCE record's classification — so a record mis-tagged 'public' at ingest sails through the egress PEP cleanly.
  - *Fix:* Add an independent second classifier (different model/method, e.g., rules + a separate model) and require AGREEMENT for any tier-DOWN decision (public/low-risk routing); disagreement fails closed to confidential. Make the egress PEP re-derive or at least sanity-check classification on emission, not just field-allowlist. Treat ingested document content as untrusted at CLASSIFICATION time (delimiting/structured handling) the same way 8.4 treats it at synthesis time — add classification-time injection to the injection-corpus test. Require human review for any automated public-tier assignment above a corpus-sensitivity threshold for export-controlled/IRB tenants.
- **[HIGH]** PSI/DP cross-institution privacy budget is per-tenant-PAIR, but the differencing/composition attack across MULTIPLE pairs and across the discovery index is not modeled. An adversary institution can run templated overlap queries against many partners, and separately mine the central discovery index (membership inference per finding 2), then COMPOSE these signals to infer confidential collaboration structure that no single per-pair budget bounds. The k-anonymity floor and per-pair epsilon do not compose to a federation-wide guarantee, and the plan claims DP as a 'required control' without a global accounting model.
  - *Why:* DP guarantees only hold under correct composition accounting. A per-pair budget with no global ledger means a coalition (or a single well-connected institution) can exceed the intended privacy loss by spreading queries across pairs and combining with index-derived signals — reconstructing exactly the confidential cross-institution relationships the platform exists to protect. This is the classic reason naive per-query/per-pair DP fails in federated settings.
  - *Fix:* Define a GLOBAL per-subject (and per-confidential-project) privacy ledger that accounts for budget consumed across ALL pairs and across index queries, not per-pair in isolation. Rate-limit and budget at the querying-SUBJECT and target-PROJECT granularity. Explicitly model the composition of (PSI overlap signal + central-index membership signal) as a single adversary capability in the threat model. Until a global accounting model exists, scope PSI to a hard whitelist of pre-approved aggregate questions with conservative fixed thresholds and say so.
- **[HIGH]** Cross-institution sharing grants carry compliance caveats (transfer_legality_ref, export_attestation_ref, ferpa_institutional_approval) enforced by Cedar at the GRANTOR's PEP, but there is no enforcement that the GRANTEE cell honors the same constraints once data is accessed via zero-copy/brokered query or (where copies are made) replicated. The grant model enforces the egress decision and the read decision at the owner, but the grantee can re-derive, re-embed, re-share downstream, or expose the accessed data to a grantee-side user whose own ABAC attributes (export status, FERPA role) were never checked against the ORIGINATING institution's caveats. There is no notion of a sticky/propagated policy that travels with shared confidential data into the grantee's cell.
  - *Why:* Confidentiality and regulatory caveats are properties of the DATA, but enforcement is anchored only at the owner. For export-controlled material, the deemed-export rule applies to the PERSON who accesses it — a grantee-side non-US-person could access ITAR data through a valid institution-to-institution grant because the US-person determination was attested by the GRANTOR for the grant, not re-checked against the actual grantee-side accessing individual. Same for FERPA: institutional approval at the grantor does not constrain which grantee-side users see student records.
  - *Fix:* Make caveats sticky and re-evaluated at the point of grantee-side access: the brokered/zero-copy access path must carry the originating caveats and the owning PEP (or a grantee-side PEP under the grant's policy) must re-check the ACTUAL accessing individual's attributes (US-person, FERPA role) against the grant caveats — not just trust the grantor's institution-level attestation. Define downstream-reshare prohibition as a grant caveat that the egress PEP and SpiceDB enforce on the grantee side (no transitive grants without grantor consent). State which controls are technical vs DUA-contractual.
- **[MEDIUM]** The audit log is the compliance/evidentiary backbone (tamper-evident hash chain → WORM + external anchor) and is correctly access-controlled, but the cross-tenant 'record on BOTH sides' design creates an unaddressed integrity/consistency problem: if A logs 'accessed B's project' but B's side fails to log (B unreachable, replication drop, or B malicious), the two audit partitions diverge with no reconciliation, and neither side can prove the other's record. There is also no anti-repudiation binding the audit entry to the specific grant version + token + ZedToken used, so a later grant mutation cannot be tied to what was actually authorized at access time.
  - *Why:* For ITAR/FERPA/DUA disputes the audit trail is the legal artifact. A divergent or non-reconcilable two-sided log undermines its evidentiary value precisely in the cross-institution case that matters most. Without binding the decision to the exact grant/token/ZedToken, you cannot reconstruct 'was this access authorized at the time' after a revoke.
  - *Fix:* Bind each cross-tenant access audit record to (grant_id, grant_version_hlc, token_jti, ZedToken) so the authorizing state is provable post-hoc. Define a two-sided audit reconciliation job and a fail-closed rule: a cross-tenant access that cannot be durably logged on the owner side is DENIED (audit-before-serve for confidential). Consider exchanging signed audit receipts between cells so each side holds counter-signed evidence.
- **[MEDIUM]** DPoP-bound, audience-scoped short-lived tokens are specified for the brokered drill-down (good, closes confused-deputy), but token REVOCATION and replay across the short lifetime are not addressed, and the token mint (Keycloak/CILogon) is a control-plane component whose compromise yields cross-tenant impersonation. If a user's affiliation is revoked mid-session (SCIM deprovision), already-issued audience-scoped tokens remain valid until expiry — a window where a deprovisioned/insider user can still drill into another institution's confidential data. The revocation-set gate covers grants/records, not subject deprovisioning.
  - *Why:* Deprovisioning is explicitly a CISO requirement (persona) and a real insider/offboarding threat. A short-lived token still grants minutes of cross-institution confidential access after the person should have lost it. Control-plane token-mint compromise is a single point that can forge cross-tenant access; the cell-local check trusts the token's subject claim.
  - *Fix:* Add subject-level revocation to the cell-local fail-closed check: the owning PEP must consult a fast subject-revocation/deprovision signal (extend the revocation set to cover subjects, or require a freshness check on high-sensitivity confidential access) so deprovisioning takes effect within the leak-window SLO, not at token expiry. Keep confidential-tier token TTLs very short and require step-up/freshness for confidential drill-down. Document token-mint compromise in the threat model and consider per-tenant signing keys so a single mint compromise does not forge all audiences.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'tier availability derived from isolation posture, enforced in ONE place' for PLG (shared soft-isolated cells host public + private(self) only, structurally no confidential) is asserted but the multi-tenant ISOLATION of the shared PLG cell is never specified at the data layer. The plan leans on Postgres RLS (explicitly 'defense-in-depth only') and per-tenant quotas, but a shared cell co-locating many researchers' 'own materials' (which are private(self)) means a single noisy-neighbor exploit, RLS misconfiguration, or shared-embedding-index cross-contamination leaks one researcher's private drafts to another. 'Private(self)' is still confidential to that user.
  - *Why:* The plan dismisses PLG confidentiality risk by saying PLG 'has no confidential data to guard' — but private(self) draft corpora ARE sensitive (unpublished research, pre-prints, grant drafts) and the shared cell's isolation is the only thing protecting tenant-A's drafts from tenant-B. With RLS demoted to defense-in-depth and no stated primary isolation mechanism for the shared cell, a single bug is a multi-tenant private-data breach.
  - *Fix:* State the PRIMARY isolation mechanism for shared PLG cells (separate schemas/databases per tenant, per-tenant encryption keys even in PLG, separate vector collections/namespaces — not a shared index with a tenant filter column). Run the same egress/leak runtime assertion within the shared cell for cross-tenant private(self) leakage, not only at the federation seam. Treat private(self) as requiring the same per-tenant key separation as confidential, minus the local-model-routing constraint.
- **[MEDIUM]** Prompt-injection defense (8.4) hard-clamps OUTPUT tier and treats retrieved content as untrusted data — but the plan also introduces multi-agent/LangGraph and corrective-loop (CRAG) stages that can take ACTIONS (re-query, tool calls, agent steps) driven by retrieved content. A poisoned confidential document could steer an agent to issue a query whose RESULTS are surfaced to a lower-tier destination, or to invoke a tool whose side effect (e.g., creating a publishable projection, triggering a share) crosses the seam — none of which is caught by an OUTPUT-text tier-clamp. The injection-corpus test is described only against synthesis exfiltration, not against agent-action exfiltration.
  - *Why:* As the platform adds agentic stages (explicitly in scope, Phase 2/3), retrieved-content-driven actions become a confused-deputy vector that bypasses the text-output clamp entirely. An attacker who can get a document into a confidential corpus could cause the agent to take a seam-crossing action under the user's authority.
  - *Fix:* Subject every agent-initiated action (re-query target tier, tool invocation, any projection/share emission) to the same PEP + tier-clamp + egress PEP as direct user actions — agents act under a constrained, tier-pinned capability, never the user's full authority. Cap and tier-pin agent loops on the confidential path (the plan caps iterations but not action-tier). Extend the injection-corpus test to agent-action exfiltration, not just synthesis text.
- **[MEDIUM]** Export-control opt-in design is correct on US-person attestation, but the 'unknown ⇒ treated as controlled' default combined with 'export gate is OPT-IN per controlled project, never blanket' creates a gap: content that is export-controlled but NOT yet placed in an opted-in project (e.g., freshly ingested, or in a general confidential project) defaults to tier=confidential but its export_controlled flag defaults to 'unknown'⇒controlled in ABAC — yet the US-person allowlist enforcement only attaches when the export gate is opted-in per project. So genuinely ITAR material sitting outside a designated controlled project is confidential (good) but NOT subject to the US-person allowlist (bad), and could be accessed by a non-US-person who has a valid confidential grant.
  - *Why:* Deemed-export liability does not wait for an admin to opt a project into the export gate. The window between ingest of controlled material and its placement into an opted-in controlled project is a compliance gap where a non-US-person with confidential access commits a deemed export.
  - *Fix:* When export_controlled resolves to EAR/ITAR (or 'unknown' for an export-sensitive tenant), enforce the US-person allowlist check REGARDLESS of project opt-in — opt-in should govern the FRE/access-restriction tradeoff and workflow, not whether the US-person gate fires at all. Default export-sensitive tenants (the fallback wedge buyer) to gate-on for all confidential content until adjudicated, and surface a quarantine state for unadjudicated potentially-controlled ingest.
- **[LOW]** The central discovery index supports 'opt-out entirely' for export-controlled/defense tenants (good), but discoverability scope (public_web | federation | named_consortium | named_tenants) is enforced only by the Discovery PEP at query time against control-plane-stored projections — there is no stated protection against the control-plane OPERATOR or a control-plane compromise reading all federation-scoped projections directly, bypassing the Discovery PEP. The index is encrypted at rest under control-plane keys the operator holds, so 'authz-gated' is an application-layer control, not a cryptographic one against the operator.
  - *Why:* A control-plane breach or insider yields the entire cross-institution publishable corpus + embeddings (the honeypot), since the PEP is bypassable below the application layer and the keys are operator-held. For most tenants this is 'only' public/federation-scoped data, but combined with finding 2 (embedding inversion / membership inference) it leaks research direction across all institutions at once.
  - *Fix:* Document control-plane-compromise blast radius explicitly. Consider per-tenant-keyed or per-consortium-keyed encryption of named_consortium/named_tenants-scoped projections so a control-plane compromise cannot read narrowly-scoped shares, and minimize what federation-scoped projections contain. Make opt-out the default for any tenant with export-controlled or high-sensitivity posture rather than opt-in.

### Lens: Maintainability & modularity — module boundaries, decoupling, blast radius, interface/contract clarity, testability, and whether the AI-router and retrieval stay swappable

*Verdict:* This is a genuinely strong modularity story relative to v1 — the import-linter CI gate, the acyclic module DAG with cross-pillar surfaces promoted to a kernel read-model, the router/transport-policy split, and the retrieval-as-pipeline decomposition are all real, defensible decisions that resolve the worst v1 coupling. The plan also correctly identifies the single most load-bearing maintainability mechanism (machine-enforced import rules) and stakes the whole modularity claim on it.
