But the plan's confidence outruns its specification in several places where the architecture will actually rot: (1) the ClassificationCapabilityDescriptor is described as the seam that lets you add a tier without a coordinated release, but the descriptor's own schema and the fail-closed/MAX-tier/clamp semantics are pervasive cross-cutting policy that no "query, don't switch" indirection can decouple — adding a tier IS a coordinated policy-kernel change and the plan pretends otherwise; (2) the IExpertiseGraph read-model is asserted as a clean contract but never specified, and it is simultaneously a kernel-owned projection AND something mod-discovery "publishes" to AND mod-funding/mod-workspaces consume — that is a god-contract forming exactly where v1's worst coupling lived; (3) the PublishableProjection per-entity allowlist creates a hidden compile-time coupling between every internal data-model change and the central-index consumer that the plan acknowledges as a CI gate but never gives an evolution/versioning story for; (4) testability of the cross-cell seam is hand-waved — Pact covers in-process contracts but the federation seam, cell-local SpiceDB replication, and egress PEP can only be tested in paid multi-cell cloud staging, meaning the highest-blast-radius surfaces have the weakest local test loop. None of these sink the platform, but they are where maintenance cost will concentrate, and the plan's tone ("machine-verified," "provable," "pinned invariant") oversells decoupling that is really just well-organized coupling.

- **[HIGH]** The ClassificationCapabilityDescriptor is sold as the seam that makes adding a tier or export sub-regime NOT a coordinated multi-contract release ('Router/publish/authz query it rather than switch on it'). This is false decoupling. Tier semantics are not a lookup value — they are pervasive policy: fail-closed default = confidential, derived artifacts inherit MAX(input tiers), output clamped to highest tier present, BYO selectable only for public/low-risk, confidential routes local-only, PLG cells structurally cannot host confidential. Every one of those is a branch on tier identity, not a data-driven query. Adding a fourth tier (e.g. 'export-controlled' as distinct from confidential) forces edits to IClassifier, the MAX-tier lattice in derivation, the router precedence table, the egress allowlist review, the Cedar narrow-only rules, and the edition-posture derivation — i.e. the entire policy kernel plus the serving kernel's router. The descriptor indirection hides where the lattice and ordering live, making the coordinated change harder to find, not unnecessary.
  - *Why:* The plan's central maintainability claim for the most volatile dimension (data classification, which WILL change as new compliance regimes appear) is that blast radius is contained behind one descriptor. In reality the tier lattice is the most cross-cutting concept in the system. A team that believes the descriptor decouples them will add a tier as a 'config change,' miss the MAX-lattice and clamp ordering, and ship a confidentiality leak — the exact failure class the plan calls existential.
  - *Fix:* Drop the 'query not switch' framing. Instead specify the tier lattice as a single owned module (a partial order with explicit join/MAX, a declared default, and a routing/clamp policy table) that the policy kernel imports, and make 'add a tier' an explicit, checklisted, test-gated policy-kernel release with a fixture that exercises every consumer (router, derivation, egress, Cedar, edition derivation). Have the import-linter and a dedicated exhaustiveness test (e.g. a typed enum/total-match check) FAIL the build when a new tier is added without updating each consumer, turning the cross-cutting change into a compiler-enforced fan-out rather than a silently-incomplete config edit.
- **[HIGH]** IExpertiseGraph is the load-bearing decoupling mechanism for the module DAG (it is how mod-funding avoids importing mod-discovery), yet it is never specified and is structurally overloaded. Section 5.3 says mod-discovery 'publishes' to it, mod-workspaces and mod-funding 'consume' it, and it is simultaneously 'kernel-owned.' A contract that one module writes, two modules read, and the kernel owns, carrying 'scholarly graph + expertise fingerprints,' is a shared canonical model — the classic god-contract. Its schema will accrete every field any pillar needs (collaborator edges for discovery, expertise vectors for funding team-assembly, membership for workspaces), and because all four pillars couple to it, every change to it has cross-pillar blast radius. The DAG is acyclic on paper only because the cycle was relocated into an unspecified shared schema.
  - *Why:* This is precisely where v1 rotted (mod-funding -> mod-discovery). v2 renamed the edge instead of removing the coupling. As pillars evolve, IExpertiseGraph becomes the bottleneck contract: you cannot add a discovery feature without touching the schema funding/workspaces depend on, so the 'add a feature with minimal blast radius' requirement fails for any feature that touches expertise/collaboration — which is most of pillars 1, 3, and 4.
  - *Fix:* Specify IExpertiseGraph now as a versioned, additive-only, READ-only projection with a named owner (kernel, not mod-discovery — clarify that mod-discovery emits raw events the kernel projects, so mod-discovery does NOT 'publish the contract'). Split it if it carries more than one bounded concern: e.g. IExpertiseFingerprint (vectors, consumed by funding/discovery) vs ICollaborationGraph (edges/membership, consumed by workspaces/discovery). Add a consumer registry entry per field so a field can be retired when no pillar reads it. Without a concrete schema and ownership rule, this is the one contract guaranteed to rot.
- **[HIGH]** The PublishableProjection design creates a tight compile-time/CI coupling between every internal entity's data model and the central-index consumer, with no evolution story. The 'total projection function where every field defaults to non-published' plus 'any new internal-entity field is non-publishable unless added to a PublishableProjection' means the projection schemas are a second, parallel data model that must be maintained in lockstep with the internal entities AND with whatever the central index and Discovery PEP expect. The plan specifies the safety property (nothing leaks) but says nothing about how a projection schema versions, how the central index handles nodes emitting older/newer projection versions (Section 10.2 mentions schema-registry compat for events generically, but projection field-allowlist changes are a distinct, security-sensitive schema), or how adding a publishable field is reviewed and rolled out across self-hosted nodes on independent upgrade cycles.
  - *Why:* This is the federation seam — the existential surface. A maintainability gap here is also a security gap. If a new publishable field is added centrally but an older node doesn't emit it (or vice versa), you get either silent discovery degradation or, worse, a node emitting a field the central allowlist no longer recognizes and the egress PEP/consumer disagreeing about what is permitted. 'Minimal blast radius' for adding a discoverable field is unaddressed precisely where the radius is widest (every node + index + Discovery PEP).
  - *Fix:* Specify PublishableProjection as a first-class versioned contract in shared/contracts with its own additive-only evolution rule, a per-field 'introduced-in-version' and classification-review record, and a forward/backward-compat requirement that the central index accepts the intersection of fields across the version range currently deployed across nodes. Make the egress PEP validate against the node's emitted projection version, not a single global allowlist. Add a contract test that a node at version N and index at version M agree on the allowlist intersection.
- **[HIGH]** The entire modularity guarantee rests on the import-linter CI gate, but enforcement at the import-graph level does not stop the most common real decoupling failures: communication through shared data stores and through the kernel read-models. import-linter forbids module->module Python imports and concrete-store imports outside kernel adapters. It cannot detect mod-funding reading rows that mod-discovery wrote into a shared table, two modules coordinating via agreed-upon keys in the same Postgres/OpenSearch index, or a module depending on undocumented emergent fields/semantics of IExpertiseGraph. Given the MVP explicitly CONSOLIDATES everything into one Postgres (pgvector+AGE+tsvector+audit+outbox), the physical substrate actively invites cross-module data coupling that the import gate is blind to.
  - *Why:* The plan repeatedly calls the boundaries 'machine-verified' and treats the import gate as proof of modularity. Teams will trust the green check and couple through the shared database, which is the most insidious form of coupling because it survives all import checks and only surfaces when one module's write breaks another's read. Consolidating to one store for operational simplicity directly trades away the schema-level isolation that would otherwise back the import-level claim.
  - *Fix:* Add per-module schema/namespace ownership inside the consolidated Postgres (separate schemas with grants so module A's DB role cannot read module B's tables) and a CI/migration check that no module migration references another module's schema. State explicitly that cross-module data access is ONLY via kernel read-models/events, and enforce it at the DB-permission layer, not just the import layer. Otherwise narrow the modularity claim to 'import-level decoupling' and stop calling boundaries machine-verified.
- **[MEDIUM]** The router/transport-policy split keeps the router swappable but moves the swappability problem to an under-specified ITransportPolicy + IGuardrail composition that the router results must flow through, and the pinned invariant 'confidential ALWAYS routes local' lives partly in the router (selection) and partly in ITransportPolicy (egress block). Two components must agree on tier semantics for the guarantee to hold. The plan asserts the CI egress-block test enforces at ITransportPolicy 'not the router,' but the router still makes the local-vs-cloud selection; if the router mis-selects for confidential, ITransportPolicy blocks egress but there is no specified behavior for 'router picked a cloud provider for confidential and transport blocked it' (fail-closed error? silent local fallback? the BYO-ignored-audited path is specified but only for the BYO-misconfig case, not a general router bug).
  - *Why:* The locked requirement is a provider-agnostic, swappable router. Splitting selection from enforcement is correct, but a swappable component whose correctness depends on a second component sharing the same tier-precedence logic is only as swappable as the coupling between them is documented. An undocumented split-brain between router selection and transport enforcement is a confidentiality-leak risk and a maintenance trap when someone swaps the router.
  - *Fix:* Specify the router's output as a typed decision that ITransportPolicy validates against intrinsic tier independently, and define the behavior when they disagree (must be fail-closed: deny + audit, never silent cloud egress and never silent local fallback that masks a router bug). Add a contract test: a deliberately-buggy router that selects cloud for confidential must produce a hard fail at the transport layer, not a downgrade. This makes the router genuinely swappable because any replacement is validated by an independent enforcement layer with a defined disagreement contract.
- **[MEDIUM]** The intra-cell store-consolidation-then-split strategy (one Postgres for small cells -> split to Qdrant/OpenSearch at a 'scale trigger') changes the persistence topology behind kernel adapters, but the kernel contracts (IRetrieval pipeline, IExpertiseGraph) and the intra-cell consistency contract (Section 10.3: Postgres is SoR, derived indexes rebuildable) are specified for the consolidated case only. The split introduces a second consistency boundary (Postgres SoR -> Qdrant/OpenSearch via the intra-cell outbox + reconciliation job) that did not exist in MVP. The plan asserts rebuildability and a reconciliation/repair job 'that detects and heals BM25<->vector<->graph divergence' but does not specify the divergence-detection mechanism, the repair semantics, or how the IRetrieval pipeline behaves during a partial rebuild — and this only gets exercised at the moment of highest load (the scale trigger).
  - *Why:* The maintainability claim is 'swap stores behind adapters with minimal blast radius.' But moving from one transactional store to three eventually-consistent ones is a fundamental consistency-model change, not an adapter swap. Deferring its specification to 'when scale forces it' means the divergence-healing logic gets designed under production pressure, which is exactly when consistency bugs (stale vector vs fresh graph, returning passages whose source row was deleted) ship.
  - *Fix:* Specify the split-store consistency contract now, even if implemented later: per-record version (HLC) carried into every derived index, a divergence detector (compare SoR version vs index version per record on a sampling/sweep cadence), defined repair (re-project from SoR), and defined IRetrieval behavior during rebuild (serve from SoR-version-gated results, drop passages whose SoR row is tombstoned). Treat the split as a planned migration with its own contract tests, not an adapter swap.
- **[MEDIUM]** The policy-kernel / serving-kernel split with a 'narrow' ClassificationCapabilityDescriptor boundary places IAuditSink and IPublishMetadata in the policy kernel and IRetrieval/IIdentityResolution/IEventBus in the serving kernel, but several real flows cross the boundary in ways that will force the two 'independently evolving' kernels to co-version anyway. The egress PEP (IPublishMetadata, policy kernel) consumes the outbox produced via IEventBus (serving kernel); the audit sink (policy) records router decisions (serving) and retrieval provenance (serving); identity resolution (serving) feeds subject resolution used by the PEP (policy). The plan asserts the descriptor is the only coupling, but audit-of-serving-events and egress-over-eventbus are structural cross-kernel dependencies.
  - *Why:* The benefit claimed for the two-kernel split is that the serving kernel can evolve faster than the policy kernel. If audit and egress (policy) are tightly coupled to event and router shapes (serving), then a serving-kernel change to event schema or router decision shape forces a policy-kernel change — defeating the independent-evolution rationale and creating the 'distributed monolith that versions in lockstep' that v2 claims to have fixed.
  - *Fix:* Define the cross-kernel contracts explicitly: a stable AuditEvent schema (serving emits, policy consumes) and a stable Outbox record envelope (serving produces, policy's egress PEP validates) that are themselves versioned and additive-only, so serving-kernel internals can change without breaking policy consumers. List the cross-kernel contracts in the intra-kernel compatibility matrix alongside the descriptor — the matrix currently names only the descriptor as the boundary, which is incomplete.
- **[MEDIUM]** Testability of the highest-blast-radius surfaces is the weakest part of the test strategy. Section 15.5 admits the two-cell federation seam, egress-block enforcement at scale, cell-local SpiceDB replication, and multi-region replication 'cannot be validated on a 36GB Mac' and are gated behind 'budgeted cloud staging.' Pact-style consumer-driven contract tests (15.3) cover in-process kernel contracts, but the cross-cell contracts (drill-down brokering, token-audience validation, revocation-set propagation, projection allowlist agreement across node versions) have no local fast-feedback test loop. The most security-critical, highest-coupling interactions are the slowest and most expensive to test, so they will be under-tested in practice.
  - *Why:* Maintainability includes the cost and speed of catching a regression. If the only place the federation seam is exercised is a paid cloud staging environment in CI, developers will not run it on every change, regressions in the existential surface land later and cost more, and the 'minimal blast radius' promise is undermined by a slow detection loop exactly where blast radius is largest.
  - *Fix:* Build a local multi-cell harness: run two cells + a stub control plane in docker-compose (small/quantized model or a mock inference endpoint) so the federation seam, brokered drill-down, token-audience confused-deputy test, egress PEP, and revocation-set gate all run in local CI on every PR. Reserve paid cloud staging for performance/scale (vLLM batching, multi-region latency, index sharding) — not for correctness of the seam. The seam's CORRECTNESS must be locally testable or it will rot.
- **[MEDIUM]** The retrieval pipeline decomposition (IRetriever -> IFuser -> IReranker -> ICorrectiveLoop -> IGraphAugment) is a clean linear contract, but two of the deferred features break the linear assumption and the plan only hand-waves it. ICorrectiveLoop 're-queries IRetriever' (a back-edge / loop, not a stage in a pipe) and IGraphAugment (HippoRAG2 PPR) needs the graph and the query embedding and feedback, while Adaptive RAG (Section 9) is a difficulty router that must sit BEFORE the retriever and choose which downstream stages run. A fixed stage ordering cannot express 'router decides pipeline shape' + 'corrective loop re-enters' + 'graph augment interleaves with rerank.' The plan says 'worked seams are specified for the single-shot case and the corrective-loop case' but does not show how Adaptive-RAG-as-orchestrator coexists with a declared linear stage list.
  - *Why:* The locked requirement is that retrieval stays swappable/pluggable as advanced techniques are added. If the pipeline abstraction is a fixed linear list, adding Adaptive RAG (a control-flow decision over stages) or multi-agent decomposition (Section 9.2) forces a redesign of the pipeline contract — the exact 'it cannot absorb PPR/CRAG without leaking' problem v2 claims to have solved, reappearing one level up.
  - *Fix:* Define the retrieval seam as a pipeline GRAPH/plan, not a fixed stage chain: a stable IRetrievalPlanner that emits a (possibly cyclic, bounded-iteration) plan over the micro-interfaces, executed by a small orchestrator. Adaptive RAG becomes a planner strategy; CRAG's re-query is a bounded back-edge in the plan; multi-agent is another planner. Pin loop-iteration caps and per-stage tier-clamping at the orchestrator. This keeps each micro-interface swappable AND lets control flow evolve without changing the stage interfaces.
- **[LOW]** Module remove path is under-specified for shared state. Section 5.7 says removal = feature-flag off + drain consumers + tear down namespaced storage, and the consumer registry 'retains historical event schemas.' But if a removed module emitted events into IExpertiseGraph or wrote projections that other pillars now depend on (e.g. mod-discovery feeding funding's team-assembly), tearing down the module strands the read-model data those consumers still need. The blast-radius-of-removal claim ignores that read-models are populated BY modules and consumed by OTHERS.
  - *Why:* The locked NFR is add/remove with minimal blast radius. Removal blast radius is real when a producing module disappears but its projected data is still consumed — the plan treats removal as purely local teardown, which it is not for any module that feeds a shared read-model.
  - *Fix:* Specify that read-model data outlives its producing module (kernel-owned storage, not module-namespaced), and that removing a producer means the read-model goes stale-but-readable (with freshness timestamps already in the design) rather than disappearing. Add a dependency check at removal time: warn/block if a kernel read-model field has a single producer being removed and live consumers remain.
- **[LOW]** Edition packaging is asserted to be pure 'packaging toggles over one architecture' with tier availability 'derived from isolation posture, enforced in ONE place,' but the editions table (Section 2.4) couples three independent axes — isolation posture, module set, and available tiers — into five named bundles, and Section 13.2 then introduces a SEPARATE substrate (single-appliance k3s, library authz, outbox-polling) for the sovereign edition. That means the sovereign edition is NOT the same architecture with toggles — it swaps the authz implementation, the event substrate, and the orchestration. The 'one codebase, packaging toggles' claim quietly has an exception that is a different deployment substrate.
  - *Why:* If sovereign runs library-authz + outbox-polling while managed runs SpiceDB-cell-local + Kafka/Debezium, those are two materially different runtime stacks that must both be maintained and kept behaviorally identical for the confidentiality guarantees — doubling the test surface for the most security-sensitive edition. The plan's 'architecture stays constant, only build order/posture changes' is not quite true across the managed/sovereign divide.
  - *Fix:* State explicitly that there are (at least) two supported runtime profiles (full managed stack vs single-appliance) behind the same contracts, and that BOTH must pass the same confidentiality/authz/egress contract suite (the abstract conformance tests run against both substrates). Add the substrate profile as an explicit, tested axis rather than implying a single architecture; otherwise the sovereign substrate's authz/egress behavior will drift from managed and the guarantee will hold in one but not the other.

### Lens: Product, GTM & business viability

*Verdict:* v2 is dramatically more honest than v1 on business viability — it kills the inverted unit economics (no confidential GPU cell at $300/mo), retires the commodity public-lit wedge, sequences GTM motions instead of running four at once, and ties first revenue to the moat. Those are real fixes and I credit them. But the plan resolves its problems on paper by retreating into a wedge that may not exist as a buyable unit, and it consistently underweights how brutal the go-to-market is for an unproven 5-person startup selling a confidentiality-critical, multi-party, security-reviewed platform into the slowest procurement environment in the economy (research universities + consortia + export-controlled labs). The single load-bearing assumption — that a "pre-bonded 2-node consortium design partner with an executable master agreement, two members who will co-deploy, AND a $2.5–8k/mo/node security budget" exists and can be closed by one BD hire before runway expires — is asserted, not evidenced, and is relegated to Open Question #1. If that dyad doesn't close, the fallback (single export-controlled lab) is arguably a HARDER first sale (DCSA/CMMC posture, ITAR liability, on-prem expectations) for a pre-revenue vendor, not an easier one. The plan also has not honestly confronted that its differentiated value (confidential cross-institution sharing) is precisely the value that triggers the longest legal/security/IRB review, so "differentiated wedge" and "fast first revenue" are in direct tension. The one-product-four-buyers requirement is handled credibly via packaging + isolation-posture-derived tiers; that part is genuinely good. But pricing realism, willingness-to-pay, sales-cycle-vs-runway, the build-vs-incumbent-displacement reality, and the "selling against free" problem on the only tier that bootstraps the network are all under-evidenced. This is a fundable narrative, not yet a validated business.

- **[CRITICAL]** The entire revenue plan rests on a wedge buyer the plan itself cannot confirm exists. The MVP, beachhead, pricing, network-effect bootstrap, and SOC2-bridge ALL depend on landing 'a pre-bonded 2-node consortium with an executable master agreement, two members who will co-deploy at the same time, AND a security line-item budget of $2.5–8k/mo/node.' That is a four-way AND across legal readiness, technical readiness, joint scheduling, and budget — and the plan files the existence of even ONE such buyer under Open Question #1 ('spike: outreach to 5–8 candidates'). The architecture decisions are downstream of a buyer that has not been validated to exist.
  - *Why:* If this specific compound buyer cannot be found and closed within runway, the company has no first revenue and no de-risked federation seam — both of which v2 explicitly sequences FIRST. Existing master agreements (NSF/NIH center DUAs, CTSA, Big Ten Academic Alliance) almost never pre-authorize ingesting confidential data into a third-party SaaS vendor's cells; they govern data sharing between the institutions, not with a new processor. So even a 'pre-bonded' dyad likely still triggers a fresh DPA/security review for THIS vendor — collapsing the central premise that the master agreement 'short-circuits fresh DUA.'
  - *Fix:* Before committing the architecture sequence, run the buyer-discovery spike FIRST and gate the plan on it: name 3 concrete candidate dyads, confirm in writing that their existing master agreement permits onboarding a new SaaS processor without fresh full security review, and get a verbal price indication. If none clears, the wedge must change BEFORE engineering, not after. Treat 'master agreement covers a new processor' as a falsifiable assumption to test in week 1, not a stated fact in Section 2.2.
- **[CRITICAL]** Differentiated value and fast first revenue are in direct, unacknowledged tension. The plan's moat IS confidential cross-institution data sharing — which is exactly the capability that triggers the maximum procurement friction: IT security review (HECVAT 321 questions), CISO sign-off, DPA negotiation, possibly IRB, and for the fallback export lab, ITAR/DCSA. v2 correctly retired the fast-but-commodity public wedge, but in doing so it moved first revenue onto the single slowest-to-sell capability in the catalog, then claims first revenue arrives fast because the dyad shows value with 'zero confidential sharing needed' first.
  - *Why:* The 'zero confidential sharing' demo (public-tier federated discovery between two institutions) is precisely the capability that VIVO/Direct2Experts federates for FREE across 64 institutions. So the fast/early demonstrable slice has no paid differentiation, and the paid differentiation (confidential workspace + revocable grant) is gated behind the full security/legal review the plan elsewhere admits takes 12–18 months. The revenue-bearing increment and the fast-to-show increment are different increments — the plan conflates them to manufacture a 'fast differentiated wedge' that doesn't exist as a single sellable motion.
  - *Fix:* Be explicit about which increment the design partner PAYS for at first dollar and when that payment clears the buyer's review gate. If the paid trigger is the confidential workspace, model the real first-dollar date as post-security-review (likely 6–12 months from first contact) and size runway to it. If you want genuinely fast first revenue, you may need a paid pilot/POC contract (services-style, not SaaS subscription) as the actual Phase-0 revenue instrument — say so, because that changes the revenue quality and the funding story.
- **[HIGH]** The fallback wedge (single export-controlled / defense lab) is presented as the lower-risk safety net but is almost certainly a HARDER first sale than the primary wedge, and the plan under-prices that. An export-controlled lab buying a SaaS vendor for ITAR/EAR-relevant data implies CUI handling, likely CMMC 2.0 / NIST 800-171 compliance, DCSA scrutiny, US-person personnel controls on the VENDOR side, and a strong on-prem/air-gap expectation — yet the plan defers sovereign/on-prem out of the first 18 months and offers a 'managed cell.' A defense lab will not put ITAR data in a pre-revenue startup's managed cloud cell.
  - *Why:* The fallback is supposed to de-risk the case where the dyad doesn't close. But it swaps a hard sale for a harder one with a longer compliance moat (CMMC alone is a multi-quarter, capital-intensive certification) and a deployment posture (sovereign/on-prem) the plan explicitly defers. So the 'fallback' is not actually available within the MVP architecture and timeline — it's a different, more expensive company.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) drop the export-lab fallback as a Phase-0 option and find a genuinely easier fallback (e.g., a single well-funded R1 center buying the confidential single-tenant product for internal-only use, no export controls, no federation, priced on the security guarantee), or (b) if you keep the export-lab path, honestly state it requires the sovereign/on-prem appliance + CMMC posture and therefore is NOT a Phase-0 fallback but a Phase-2+ vertical with its own capital requirement.
- **[HIGH]** Willingness-to-pay at $2.5–8k/mo/node is asserted as 'defensible' but is anchored only to internal COGS, not to any external evidence of what a research office or center will actually pay — and the plan's own competitive table shows the comparable confidential-collaboration value (VIVO federation) is free and the AI-grant fast-follow (Atom) is $179/mo across 50+ institutions. The plan derives price bottom-up from confidential-GPU COGS ('price the security guarantee'), but buyers don't pay your COGS; they pay their perceived value ceiling, which the same plan shows is depressed by free/cheap incumbents.
  - *Why:* Pricing on COGS in a market with free/cheap adjacent substitutes is how startups discover their floor price exceeds the market's ceiling price — i.e., the unit economics are still inverted, just at a different price point. A research center on a fixed indirect-cost recovery budget paying $30–100k/yr/node for a security guarantee they currently get via DUA + their own infra for ~zero marginal cost is a hard, unevidenced sell. The 'security line-item budget' is assumed to exist and absorb this; that is the actual risk and it's listed as Open Question #2, downstream of the architecture commitment.
  - *Fix:* Pressure-test value-based (not cost-based) WTP before building: run 8–10 pricing interviews with RD officers/CISOs framed as 'what would you pay to share confidential project data with a partner institution without it leaving your boundary, replacing your current DUA+manual process?' If the answer clusters below the COGS floor, the confidential-managed-cell model is dead on arrival and you must shift COGS to the buyer (BYO-compute) as the DEFAULT, not the PLG-only afterthought it currently is. Also model gross margin at the floor price including the GPU — a 'few hundred to ~$1k+/mo GPU alone' against a $2.5k floor is a thin-to-negative margin once you add support, SOC2 amortization, and CILogon.
- **[HIGH]** The network-effect cold-start is not actually solved — it's restated. The moat is 'value compounds per connected node; worthless single-tenant, defensible at N≥2.' The bootstrap mechanism is 'seed a pre-bonded dyad.' But a dyad is N=2 with exactly ONE possible cross-institution edge, and the value of that edge to the two members is bounded by what they already get from co-authoring directly. There is no mechanism described for getting from N=2 (one design-partner dyad) to N≥10 where cross-institution discovery has real combinatorial value — and each new node requires its own institutional security review + DPA, so node acquisition does not get cheaper as N grows (no supply-side or demand-side economy of scale in onboarding).
  - *Why:* Classic two-sided/network products bootstrap by making single-side value standalone-useful (so the first nodes join without the network) and then letting cross-side value compound. Here the plan admits single-tenant is 'worthless' for the moat, and cross-node value only exists at N≥2 within an ALREADY-bonded relationship — meaning the network never bootstraps beyond pre-existing collaboration clusters the platform didn't create. The platform captures value from collaborations that already exist; it doesn't manufacture new cross-institution value, which is what the funding narrative ('federation network effect') promises.
  - *Fix:* Specify the concrete path from the first dyad to a self-reinforcing network: which side is standalone-useful enough to acquire nodes without the network (likely the intra-institution research-intelligence product, which must be independently worth buying), and what specifically makes node #11's join attractive to nodes #1–10. If the honest answer is 'we ride existing consortia (CTSA, Big Ten),' then your TAM is bounded by existing consortium membership and your GTM is consortium-BD, not PLG-bootstrapped network growth — which contradicts the 'PLG funnel as brand surface / expansion' story and should be reconciled.
- **[HIGH]** The PLG tier — the only motion that could cheaply bootstrap bottom-up adoption and brand — has been scoped down to 'public literature + the researcher's own draft materials, AI Q&A with no training on your data,' which the plan itself calls a 'modest' increment 'priced accordingly.' Against free/generous-tier Elicit, Consensus, SciSpace, NotebookLM, and ChatGPT-with-files, this PLG product has no defensible differentiation and no obvious reason a researcher pays for it.
  - *Why:* v2 correctly de-fanged the CISO-shadow-IT risk by removing confidential data from PLG. But in doing so it gutted PLG's value prop to something the market already gives away. So PLG cannot serve its stated Phase-2 role as an 'expansion funnel and brand surface' — a funnel that no one enters because the free product is as good is not a funnel. The locked requirement that one product serve the bottom-up PLG buyer is technically satisfied (packaging exists) but commercially hollow.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) accept PLG is a loss-leader / brand-awareness play and stop modeling it as revenue or a meaningful funnel, and reallocate that GTM energy to the consortium/institution motion; or (b) find a PLG increment that is genuinely differentiated AND safe — e.g., grounded Q&A over the researcher's OWN institutional library/subscription corpus via a sanctioned library connector (a real gap vs Elicit), which requires institutional buy-in and thus is really a land-and-expand into the top-down motion, not true self-serve PLG. Be honest about which it is.
- **[HIGH]** Sales-cycle-vs-runway is never quantified, and the plan's own facts imply it is fatal. SOC2 Type II gates the first institutional contract and takes a 6–12 month observation window 'once controls are stable' (i.e., not from day 1). HECVAT, DPA negotiation, and multi-institution legal review for confidential federation add months. The plan acknowledges a '12–18 month institutional cycle' and says the consortium master-agreement wedge 'bridges' it — but the wedge revenue is itself gated behind security review (Finding #2), and a 5-person team with a fractional GTM hire running ONE motion cannot parallelize. There is no stated runway, burn, or revenue-by-month model anywhere in Section 16/18.
  - *Why:* A from-scratch startup selling confidential, multi-party, security-reviewed software into universities and consortia is signing up for the longest enterprise sales cycles that exist, with the smallest possible sales org (one fractional hire). If first real (SaaS, post-review) revenue is 12–18 months out and Phase-0 'first paying design-partner node' is really a paid pilot, the company needs 24–30 months of runway and a funding story that explicitly accounts for a long pre-revenue valley. The cost section lists COGS lines but no burn, no runway, no break-even customer count realized against a timeline.
  - *Fix:* Add a burn/runway/revenue-by-quarter model. State the assumed seed raise, monthly burn for 5 FTE + cloud staging + SOC2 (~$40–70k/yr) + GPU + CILogon, and overlay the realistic first-dollar and first-recurring-revenue dates given the security/legal gates. If the model shows recurring revenue arriving after month 15+, either the team must shrink, the wedge must get faster (paid pilots/services), or the raise must be larger — make that explicit rather than implying revenue 'first.'
- **[MEDIUM]** The competitive moat against the named fast-follow (Atom Grants) is overstated as structural. The plan asserts confidentiality+federation is 'a structural moat they cannot bolt on cheaply.' But Atom already has 50+ institutional relationships and RD-office distribution; adding a per-institution isolated cell + BYO-key + local-model routing is an engineering project, not a structural impossibility — and they'd be adding it to an installed base, whereas you'd be adding distribution to your tech. Distribution is usually the harder thing to bolt on, and they have it.
  - *Why:* The plan frames the moat as defensible because incumbents are 'public-only and single-tenant,' but a funded fast-follow with 50+ institutional logos and an existing grant-intelligence wedge could reach the confidential-federation feature faster than you reach their distribution. 'They can't bolt it on cheaply' is an assumption about their engineering will, not a structural barrier.
  - *Fix:* Reframe the moat honestly as a race between your tech-to-distribution and their distribution-to-tech, and identify what actually compounds for you that they can't copy: accumulated cross-institution sharing-grant graph + per-tenant trust relationships + compliance certifications already passed. State the time window you believe you have and what you must achieve within it (e.g., 'lock N consortia under multi-year contracts before a funded incumbent ships isolation'). 'Structural moat' is doing too much work.
- **[MEDIUM]** The pricing architecture for the sales-assisted tiers is multi-axis (per-node base + per-active-user pillar modules + per-connected-institution exchange add-on + BYO-key premium). For a 5-person team with one fractional GTM hire and zero sales engineers in Phase 0, this is unquotable in practice. The plan waves this away with 'there is a sales engineer to quote it' — but there isn't one in the founding team table.
  - *Why:* Complex multi-axis pricing with no sales-engineering capacity produces stalled deals, inconsistent quotes, and long negotiation cycles — the opposite of what a runway-constrained startup needs for its first handful of deals. Early enterprise deals should be radically simple to quote and sign, even if money is left on the table, to compress cycle time and learn.
  - *Fix:* For Phase 0–1, collapse pricing to a single annual per-node platform fee with everything included (land), and defer per-user/per-pillar/exchange-add-on unbundling to Phase 2 once there's a sales engineer and pricing data. Make the first 3–5 deals trivially quotable. Add the sales-engineering capability to the team plan before introducing multi-axis pricing as a real motion.
- **[MEDIUM]** Data governance friction beyond security review is under-addressed for the confidential-federation use case. The plan covers FERPA/GDPR/ITAR mechanisms well at the attribute level, but the GTM/procurement reality is that cross-institution confidential sharing often requires institution-specific IRB review, office-of-general-counsel sign-off on a new data flow, and library/scholarly-comms involvement — each a separate stakeholder with veto power, none of which is in the buyer/persona sequencing as a deal-stage gate.
  - *Why:* The plan identifies CISO as the 'veto holder' but treats the sale as essentially one security review. In reality, confidential cross-institution data movement at a university routes through 4–6 offices (IT security, OGC, IRB/HRPP, sponsored programs, library, data governance), and ANY can stall the deal indefinitely. Underestimating the number of veto points is a classic reason university SaaS deals die after a promising champion.
  - *Fix:* Map the full procurement stakeholder graph per buyer motion as a deal-stage checklist (who must approve, in what order, typical duration), and design the product/collateral to pre-empt each (e.g., a ready IRB-data-flow description, an OGC-ready DPA+SCC package, a HECVAT pre-fill). Treat 'number and sequence of veto points' as a primary GTM risk, not a footnote under CISO.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'integrate, don't displace' strategy with incumbents (connectors to Pure/Esploro/ORCID/OpenAlex in; push out to Cayuse/Kuali) is asserted as a friction-reducer but creates a partnership/integration dependency that is itself a GTM cost and risk, and several of these incumbents (Elsevier/Pure, Clarivate) are competitors who control the data layer you depend on.
  - *Why:* Building connectors into Pure (Elsevier) and pushing to Cayuse/Kuali means depending on the cooperation, API stability, and goodwill of companies that may view a confidential-federation AI layer as a threat to their own roadmap. 'Integrate don't displace' works until the incumbent decides the integration partner is a competitor — and the data-layer incumbents have every incentive to build the AI layer themselves. This is a strategic dependency, not just an engineering connector list.
  - *Fix:* Identify which integrations are genuinely partner-friendly (open standards: ORCID, OpenAlex, ROR — fine) vs which depend on a competitor's proprietary API (Pure, Symplectic — risky). Reduce critical-path dependence on competitor-controlled APIs, and have a fallback for each (e.g., institution-provided exports). State the strategic risk that data-layer incumbents are the most likely well-resourced entrant into your exact white space.
- **[LOW]** The positioning name 'TigerExchange' (and the inherited Tiger* branding) is RIT/Princeton-mascot-coded and single-institution-flavored for a product whose entire pitch is neutral, multi-institution federation where no member is privileged. A consortium of competing R1s is unlikely to adopt infrastructure branded after one member's mascot.
  - *Why:* Branding signals neutrality (or lack of it) in a multi-party trust product. A name that reads as belonging to one institution undercuts the 'neutral exchange layer no one centralizes' positioning at the exact moment trust is being established with rival institutions.
  - *Fix:* Drop the Tiger* lineage for the commercial product; choose an institution-neutral name that connotes neutral federation/exchange. Minor, but trust-product branding matters in multi-party BD.

### Lens: Cost, ops & founder feasibility — buildability by a small team, infra cost shape, self-host burden per node, dev complexity vs MVP, over-engineering, licensing traps, M4 Max dev-vs-prod gap

*Verdict:* This is a markedly more honest plan than a typical architecture deck — the v1→v2 decision log explicitly retires the inverted-unit-economics fantasy (no $300/mo confidential GPU cell), shrinks the MVP kernel to thin swappable implementations, sequences Dagster-only / outbox-polling instead of five distributed systems, defers TEE and sovereign on-prem, and names a budgeted cloud-staging opex line to cover the M4 Max parity gap. Those are exactly the right founder-feasibility moves and they materially de-risk the build. BUT the plan still over-scopes the Phase-0 MVP relative to what it claims three engineers can ship: even the "reduced" kernel bundles classification, a pure router with separated transport-policy enforcement, an independent egress PEP with a structural PublishableProjection type + continuous runtime leak assertion, cell-local grant replication design, a full composable retrieval pipeline, RAGAS-in-CI, two-cell live federation, AND a SOC2 Type I sprint — for a pre-bonded two-institution design partner that has to be found before any revenue exists. The hidden-COGS picture is also rosier than reality: Vault namespaces (the per-tenant key-isolation backbone) are Enterprise-only at low-six-figures/yr, CILogon commercial is quote-only and unbudgeted, always-on cloud GPU per cell is ~$735–1,450/mo for the instance alone before redundancy/storage/overhead, and the central sharded OpenSearch index is a real growing cost the plan correctly reclassifies but never sizes in dollars. The strongest feasibility risk is not technical correctness — it's that the entire revenue model is gated on landing a specific, rare two-node consortium design partner that may not exist on the timeline, with a fallback (single export-controlled lab) that demands the hardest compliance machinery earliest. Good instincts, still too much for first-build.

- **[CRITICAL]** Phase-0 MVP is still too large for 3 engineers despite the 'reduced kernel' framing. The Phase-0 build list contains ~12 distinct hard subsystems: fail-closed classification, pure model router, ITransportPolicy egress block, IPolicyEnforcement-over-RBAC, hash-chained audit, PublishableProjection structural type + total projection function, independent egress PEP, continuous runtime leak assertion, deterministic identity resolution, a full composable retrieval pipeline (retriever/fuser/reranker) with RAGAS-in-CI, a functional confidential tier on vLLM, AND live 2-node public-tier federation with one revocable cell-local-SpiceDB grant — plus standing up cloud staging and a SOC2 Type I sprint. Each of the security-critical pieces (egress PEP correctness, projection type, cell-local grant semantics, runtime leak assertion) is a multi-week hardening effort on its own because a single bug is the existential leak the whole product sells against.
  - *Why:* This is the exact failure mode the plan claims to have fixed in v1 ('16-month death march for 3 engineers'). Two-cell live federation + a confidential egress gate that must be provably correct is not an MVP — it is the full moat. Building it before a single paying customer is validated means burning the entire runway on infrastructure for a design partner that section 19 admits has not been identified yet.
  - *Fix:* Cut Phase-0 to a single-cell functional confidential product (no live second cell, no central index, no federation seam) for the FALLBACK buyer (one export-controlled or security-sensitive lab). Ship: classification + router + transport-policy egress block + confidential local inference + hybrid retrieval + audit. Defer the egress PEP / PublishableProjection / runtime leak assertion / 2-node seam to Phase-1, because they only earn their cost once a SECOND node exists. Make the consortium dyad Phase-1, not Phase-0. This halves the build and lets first revenue land on N=1 differentiated security value while the dyad is being sourced.
- **[CRITICAL]** The entire revenue plan is gated on finding a rare, specific artifact: a pre-bonded 2-node consortium with an EXECUTABLE master agreement AND two members willing to co-deploy a brand-new vendor's GPU cells. Section 19 Q1 admits this partner is not yet identified and the discovery method is 'outreach to 5-8 candidates.' The fallback (single export-controlled lab) front-loads ITAR/EAR US-person attestation, FRE-loss reasoning, and the highest-ACV/highest-friction procurement — the opposite of an easy first sale.
  - *Why:* A founder-feasibility plan cannot have its first dollar depend on an external precondition with an unknown hit rate and a months-long sales cycle. If no dyad closes in the first 2 quarters, the company has built two-cell federation infrastructure with zero revenue and a fallback buyer whose compliance bar is the most expensive in the catalog. This is a single point of go-to-market failure dressed up as 'resolved.'
  - *Fix:* De-risk by making the first sellable unit independent of the dyad: a single-tenant confidential research-intelligence cell (the fallback's product, minus the ITAR gate) sold to ANY security-sensitive lab or funded center — not requiring export controls, not requiring a second institution. Validate willingness-to-pay at the $2.5-8k/mo band against 5-8 such single buyers BEFORE committing engineering to the federation seam. Treat the consortium dyad as the expansion proof, not the survival-critical first close.
- **[HIGH]** Per-tenant key isolation depends on Vault namespaces, which are Enterprise-only (low-six-figures/yr, unpublished pricing, +25-60% hidden cost per vendor reports). The plan lists this as Open Question #6 ('confirm OSS suffices... else budget Enterprise') but the answer is already knowable: Vault Community lacks namespaces. The per-tenant KMS/namespace isolation story — load-bearing for BYOK/HYOK and crypto-shred — likely forces Vault Enterprise or a re-architecture onto per-cell separate Vault instances / cloud KMS.
  - *Why:* This is a hard COGS line the cost model (Section 16) does not include. A low-six-figure annual licensing floor materially changes breakeven customer count, and the M4 Max / OSS-first ethos in the project's own constraints (cloud APIs opt-in, default to local/free) is violated silently. Discovering this post-commit is a budget shock.
  - *Fix:* Resolve now, not as an open question: design per-tenant key isolation on per-cell isolated Vault Community instances (one Vault per cell, naturally tenant-isolated, no namespaces needed) OR cloud KMS per-tenant keys. Both avoid the Enterprise license. State explicitly that Vault namespaces are NOT used, and add whichever path you pick to the COGS table with a dollar figure.
- **[HIGH]** Cloud-GPU COGS is understated. The plan's 'realistic floor ≈ a few hundred to ~$1k+/mo per dedicated cell in cloud GPU alone' covers only one always-on small GPU. Verified pricing: a single always-on A10G (g5) or L4 (g6) is ~$735-1,454/mo for the instance ALONE. A production confidential cell also needs the vLLM host headroom, the consolidated Postgres (pgvector+AGE+tsvector) compute/RAM, object storage, and any redundancy. There is no HA/failover GPU budgeted, yet Section 14.4 promises 99.9% cell availability — a single non-redundant GPU instance cannot hit 99.9% through a host failure or AZ event.
  - *Why:* Realistic all-in confidential-cell COGS is likely $1.5-3k/mo, not 'a few hundred,' which compresses margin at the $2.5-8k/mo price band far more than implied — especially for the smaller end of that range. And the 99.9% SLO is unfundable on a single GPU without a standby, which is another unbudgeted GPU.
  - *Fix:* Rebuild the per-cell COGS as a line-item stack (GPU instance + standby-or-degraded-mode policy + Postgres node + storage + egress + share of central index + monitoring) and state the all-in monthly figure. Either budget a warm-standby GPU (doubling GPU cost) or explicitly downgrade the confidential-cell availability SLO to single-instance reality (e.g. 99.5% with a documented recovery procedure). Do not promise 99.9% on non-redundant GPU.
- **[HIGH]** The central sharded OpenSearch discovery index is correctly reclassified as first-class stateful (good), and sized in GB (30-150GB), but never sized in DOLLARS or assigned an owner/ops model. A continuously-CDC-fed, multi-region, encrypted, authz-gated, scatter-gather OpenSearch cluster with separate write/query node pools and per-tenant quotas is a meaningful standing cost AND a meaningful ops burden (cluster upgrades, shard rebalancing, hot-tenant tuning) that lands on the same 3-person team.
  - *Why:* This is shared infrastructure whose cost is NOT covered by any single tenant's per-node fee, so it is pure overhead until enough nodes amortize it — and it arrives in Phase 2 right when the team is also running multiple live cells. An unsized shared-overhead component plus an OpenSearch cluster to operate is a classic small-team ops sink.
  - *Fix:* Add a dollar line for the central index at N=10/50/200 to the cost model, and decide explicitly between self-managed OpenSearch vs a managed service (OpenSearch Serverless / managed Qdrant Cloud) — managed shifts ops burden off the 3 engineers at higher unit cost, which is the right trade pre-scale. Tie central-index cost recovery to the consortium/exchange add-on pricing so it is not silent overhead.
- **[HIGH]** The four-buyer 'one product, sequenced motions' framing still implies the product must SUPPORT all four packaging editions (Lab/Department/Institution/Consortium/Sovereign) from a constant architecture — including a shared soft-isolated PLG cell with per-tenant GPU/RAM quotas and fair scheduling (Section 14.5), edition-config tier-availability derivation, and a sovereign single-appliance image. Even if only one motion is SOLD at a time, the architectural surface for all editions is asserted as constant and therefore must be designed (and largely built) up front to avoid rework.
  - *Why:* Designing five isolation postures, a multi-tenant fair-scheduled PLG substrate, AND a self-contained sovereign appliance image into a from-scratch platform is a large design tax for a team that will only sell one edition for 18+ months. The locked requirement is 'one product via packaging,' but nothing forces all packaging variants to be built early — the plan blurs 'architecture stays constant' into 'build the variation points now.'
  - *Fix:* Explicitly state which edition variation points are DESIGNED-only (interfaces reserved, not implemented) vs BUILT in each phase. The PLG shared-cell scheduler, sovereign appliance image, and BYO-compute path should be design-reserved seams with zero implementation until their phase. Confirm in writing that the single-cell managed edition is the only fully-built deployment topology through Phase 1.
- **[MEDIUM]** Cloud staging is named as a budgeted opex line (good) but its scope is large and recurring: validating vLLM batching, the two-cell federation seam, egress-block-at-scale, and multi-region SpiceDB replication 'gated behind CI.' A CI-gated multi-cell, multi-region cloud staging environment with GPUs is itself a non-trivial always-on (or frequently-on) cost and a maintenance surface — effectively a second production-shaped environment the 3-person team must keep green.
  - *Why:* The M4 Max dev box genuinely cannot validate the moat (correctly acknowledged), so this staging env is mandatory, not optional. But a multi-region GPU-bearing staging cluster run as a CI gate can rival a meaningful fraction of production COGS and consumes ops time that the plan budgets to feature work.
  - *Fix:* Scope staging to ephemeral, on-demand spin-up for the federation/egress CI gates (tear down between runs) rather than always-on, and keep multi-region SpiceDB validation as a one-time spike (Section 19 Q3) rather than a standing CI gate. Put a dollar estimate on staging in Section 16 and confirm it is on-demand, not 24/7.
- **[MEDIUM]** SOC2 Type I is slotted into Phase 0 ('weeks') concurrent with the heaviest engineering build. SOC2 Type I still requires written policies, an access-control/onboarding-offboarding process, a risk assessment, vendor management, and evidence collection — work that competes directly with three engineers shipping the kernel, and typically needs founder/ops time the plan does not staff until the 'fractional GRC' role appears later.
  - *Why:* Doing SOC2 Type I in parallel with Phase-0 engineering either slows the build or produces a thin paper Type I that the design-partner CISO discounts. The plan treats it as a quick unblock, but for a 3-engineer team with no dedicated GRC owner in Phase 0, it is a real time tax.
  - *Fix:* Either staff the fractional GRC/compliance role into Phase 0 explicitly (it currently appears as scale-up), or move SOC2 Type I to the boundary of Phase 0→1 once the first verbal design-partner commit exists (many design partners will start on a security questionnaire + roadmap, not a completed Type I). Do not assume 'weeks' of zero engineering cost.
- **[MEDIUM]** CILogon commercial subscription is correctly flagged as a COGS line, but its price is quote-only/unpublished (verified: NCSA sets it case-by-case) and the named fallback is 'direct Keycloak SAML/OIDC brokering' against 5,000+ InCommon/eduGAIN IdPs. That fallback is not a drop-in cost swap — brokering eduGAIN directly means owning metadata aggregation, certificate rotation, and per-IdP attribute-release quirks that CILogon exists to abstract. The fallback transfers significant ops burden, not just cost.
  - *Why:* If CILogon's commercial quote is high, the 'fallback' the plan leans on for price-sensitive tiers is actually a substantial federation-engineering project landing on the same 3 engineers. The cost is hidden as ops, not licensing.
  - *Fix:* Get the CILogon commercial quote before committing it as the default front-door (Section 19 Q7 is right to ask, but elevate it to a pre-build gate). If the fallback is the real plan for cheaper tiers, scope the eduGAIN-brokering effort honestly as engineering weeks, and consider that for the single-lab/single-institution first buyer you may not need broad federation at all — that buyer has ONE IdP, so direct OIDC/SAML to their Keycloak is trivial and CILogon can be deferred entirely until federation breadth is needed.
- **[MEDIUM]** Apache AGE inside the consolidated Postgres is the MVP graph store, with a 'switch trigger to Neo4j/Memgraph if it fails the multi-hop/PPR benchmark' (Section 6.2). The benchmark is deferred and the switch is a different database engine, different query language (openCypher vs AGE's Cypher dialect), and a different operational footprint. If AGE fails at R1 scale, this is not a config flip — it is a graph-layer rewrite, and Neo4j Community is GPLv3 (license-isolation burden) while Memgraph is BSL (commercial-use restriction to verify).
  - *Why:* A 'switch trigger' that is actually a database migration with a license change is a deferred-but-large risk parked on a small team. The plan correctly gates it behind a benchmark but treats the alternatives as interchangeable fallbacks when their licenses and query semantics differ materially.
  - *Fix:* Run the AGE-at-scale benchmark in Phase 0/1 (it needs only synthetic OpenAlex-subset data, no customer), not Phase 2, so the graph-engine decision is made before any cell scales. Pre-verify the Neo4j GPLv3 isolation story (separate process/service boundary) and Memgraph BSL commercial terms now, and pick the fallback engine before you need it so the switch is a planned port, not a fire drill.
- **[LOW]** The dev-vs-prod model split (Ollama on M4 Max dev / vLLM TEE-backed prod) is sound, but the plan assumes confidential-tier eval (judge = local model on the confidential tier) runs in CI. The judge model at prod fidelity (a real local LLM under vLLM) cannot run on the M4 Max dev box at the same size used in prod, so RAGAS-in-CI either runs against a smaller dev model (eval drift between dev and prod judgments) or must run in the GPU cloud-staging env (more staging cost/time).
  - *Why:* If the contractually-promised quality bar (RRF+rerank baseline, Section 9.1) is gated by a CI eval whose judge model differs between dev and prod, the quality gate is measuring something slightly different from what ships — a subtle correctness/parity gap, not catastrophic but real.
  - *Fix:* Pin which model size runs the RAGAS judge in CI and confirm it matches prod, or explicitly accept a dev-tier judge with a documented periodic prod-tier re-validation. Fold the judge's GPU cost into the staging budget rather than assuming it runs free on the Mac.

## Review of plan3  (9 critical / 22 high)


### Lens: Distributed-systems & scalability: federation topology, control/data-plane split, consistency model, sync mechanics (CDC/events), bottlenecks, failure domains, hot tenants, knowledge-graph + vector-index growth, cross-node latency.

*Verdict:* This is a markedly stronger plan than a typical "we'll replicate the revocation set, checked locally, <5s" hand-wave — the v3 author has clearly already absorbed a hard adversarial round. The authority-anchored monotonic version for tombstone ordering is correct and is the single most important DS decision in the document; the fail-closed freshness watermark with an explicitly asymmetric partition tradeoff (deny, never stale-allow) is the right CAP call and is stated honestly; pushing the revocation gate into a per-shard materialized bitmap instead of N remote lookups is the right shape; decoupling lease-bumps from hybrid-index re-writes kills a real write-amp; and two-tier topical sharding correctly matches the dominant cross-tenant read pattern. Credit where due. BUT the plan repeatedly asserts mechanisms as if naming them constitutes designing them. The freshness watermark is the load-bearing correctness primitive of the entire confidentiality guarantee, yet its propagation mechanics, monotonicity-under-failover semantics, and the actual feasibility of a <5s end-to-end window across a single-region linearizable authority → CDC → per-shard bitmap refresh → query path are never costed — they are deferred to "Open Question #4." The single-region linearizable Revocation Authority is a global synchronous dependency on the security-critical path that is asserted to be highly available without any HA/failover story, and its own partition behavior is undefined. The two-tier topical index has an unexamined re-clustering/drift problem and an under-specified fan-out claim. Cross-region generated-answer latency is summed honestly for the happy path but ignores tail amplification and the freshness-check round-trip. The brokered drill-down availability math (~98.5%) is stated but its consequences for the product (a meaningful fraction of cross-institution detail requests fail) are not confronted. These are not nitpicks: the freshness watermark and the revocation authority are the spine of the whole "confidential, revocable, federated" value proposition, and the spine is drawn but not engineered.

- **[CRITICAL]** The freshness watermark — the single load-bearing correctness primitive — has no specified propagation mechanism, no monotonicity-under-failover semantics, and no end-to-end latency budget; its feasibility is punted to Open Question #4. The plan repeatedly says 'consumer checks its revocation freshness watermark' and 'fail closed if older than (now − leak-window)' but never specifies HOW a watermark gets from the single-region linearizable authority to (a) every cell PEP and (b) every shard of the managed central index within the <5s SLO. The path is: authority assigns version → emits 'monotonic version + freshness watermark feed' → cells AND index consume → per-shard bitmap materializes → query filters on it. That is a multi-hop pipeline (authority → CDC/feed → per-shard refresh) whose total latency must be < 5s at p99 under load, across regions, with publish-shedding active. Nowhere is this budget summed the way 14.4.1 sums the query path. Worse: 'now' in '(now − leak-window)' is a clock comparison, and the trust model explicitly says no node's self-asserted clock orders security decisions — but the freshness gate compares the watermark's authority-version-or-timestamp against the CONSUMER'S local 'now'. If the consumer's clock is skewed forward, it fails closed prematurely (availability hit); if skewed backward, it serves stale data past the leak-window (the exact leak the gate exists to prevent). The watermark must be a signed authority-timestamp compared against a bounded-skew local clock, and that bound must be inside the leak-window — none of which is specified.
  - *Why:* Every confidentiality, compliance (GDPR erasure within the window, deemed-export revocation, subject deprovisioning), and revocation claim in the plan reduces to 'the freshness gate works within the leak-window.' If the propagation pipeline cannot actually hit <5s p99 — or if the clock-comparison is unsound — then the headline invariant ('fail-closed when the freshness watermark is older than the leak-window') is either an availability disaster (over-firing) or a silent leak (under-firing). This is the spine of the product and it is asserted, not engineered.
  - *Fix:* Specify the watermark as a signed (authority_monotonic_version, authority_wallclock) tuple. Define the freshness check as: max accepted authority_version seen by this consumer, AND authority_wallclock newer than (consumer_clock − leak_window − max_clock_skew_bound), where max_clock_skew_bound is enforced (NTP/PTP discipline with alarms; consumers whose skew exceeds the bound fail closed). Sum the propagation budget explicitly the way 14.4.1 sums the query path: authority commit → feed publish → per-shard/per-cell apply → queryable, at p99, under publish-shedding. State the dependency: the leak-window SLO is achievable ONLY if propagation p99 + clock-skew-bound < leak-window; if it isn't, set the leak-window honestly (e.g., 30s) rather than asserting 5s. Move this from Open Question #4 to a designed mechanism with a contract test.
- **[CRITICAL]** The single-region linearizable Revocation Authority is a global synchronous dependency on the security-critical path with no HA, failover, or self-partition story — and its own unavailability is conflated with 'control-plane partition' without analyzing the difference. Section 12 lists it as 'Single-region linearizable store (e.g. etcd/Spanner-class).' Single-region etcd is a 3-5 node Raft group in ONE region: a region outage takes it down entirely. When the authority is DOWN (not partitioned-from — actually down), it cannot advance the watermark, so EVERY cell's watermark ages past the leak-window and EVERY confidential cross-tenant drill-down across the ENTIRE platform fails closed simultaneously. That is a global blast radius from a single-region component, which directly contradicts the 'failure domains are cell-isolated' claim (14.3) and the 99.95% control-plane SLO (a single-region Raft store does not get 99.95% through region failures). The plan also never specifies: how a new version is assigned during authority leader election (gap in monotonicity?), what happens to in-flight revokes during failover, or whether the authority itself can be partitioned from the CDC feed it must drive.
  - *Why:* A 'single-region linearizable authority' that orders every security decision is the textbook single point of failure the rest of the architecture works hard to avoid. Its downtime degrades the entire federated product to deny — turning a routine cloud-region blip into a platform-wide confidential-access outage. For a product whose pitch is 'sovereign, resilient, your data stays available in your cell,' a global synchronous control dependency that can dark the cross-institution surface platform-wide is a contradiction of the deployment promise.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) make the authority multi-region with a defined consensus (Spanner/CockroachDB with explicit replica placement and a stated commit-latency cost on the revoke write path — note PSI's ledger already accepts one such dependency, so reuse the analysis), accepting higher revoke-commit latency, OR (b) explicitly bound the blast radius: define authority-unavailability as distinct from partition, give cells a 'last-known-good watermark + grace policy' that is itself a security decision (e.g., revoke writes are buffered durably and the leak-window clock is measured from authority recovery, with the outage surfaced as degraded), and state the worst-case global-deny exposure. Specify version monotonicity across leader elections (fenced/lease-based version allocation, no gaps, no reuse). Add a failure-injection test: kill the authority and assert the platform degrades to deny without violating monotonicity on recovery.
- **[HIGH]** The two-tier topical index's 'few shards per query' fan-out claim is unverified hand-waving, and the topical (embedding-cluster) shard key creates a re-clustering / drift problem the plan never addresses. The plan asserts that sharding by embedding cluster means 'the dominant query hits FEW shards' and prevents a mega-shard from dominating p95. But: (1) research topics are power-law distributed — a few hot topics (ML, genomics, cancer) will concentrate a huge fraction of corpus AND queries into a few topical shards, recreating the exact hot-shard problem 'topic-subsharding mega-R1s' was supposed to solve, except now the hotspot is cross-tenant and unavoidable. (2) Embedding clusters are not static: as the corpus grows and as embedding models are upgraded (the plan explicitly upgrades nomic→Qwen3 and contemplates model evolution), cluster boundaries drift, forcing re-sharding/re-clustering of a multi-tenant managed index — a massive, ongoing, hot-path-affecting operation that is never costed. (3) A genuinely cross-cutting query ('who works on X across the consortium') where X spans multiple clusters still scatters; the 'few shards' claim holds only for tightly-clustered queries. (4) The per-shard revocation bitmap must be replicated to EVERY shard a tenant's records land in — with topical sharding, a single tenant's records are smeared across many topical shards, so the bitmap is not one-per-tenant-cell but fanned across shards, multiplying the watermark-refresh write load the plan claims to have minimized.
  - *Why:* Sharding strategy is the core scalability decision for the central index, and topical sharding was chosen specifically to fix tenant-sharding's fan-out. But it trades a known, bounded problem (tenant skew, fixable by subsharding) for an unbounded, dynamic one (topical hotspots + embedding drift + smeared revocation bitmaps). If hot topical shards dominate p95 or re-clustering becomes a recurring fire, the 800ms discovery SLO and the managed-ops-off-3-engineers premise both break.
  - *Fix:* Cost the topical-skew distribution explicitly on synthetic OpenAlex data (Open Q #7 mentions fan-out but not skew or drift): measure query and corpus concentration across clusters, not just average fan-out. Specify the re-clustering/re-embedding migration as a first-class operation (versioned embedding space, dual-index during cutover, who pays, downtime), the same way 6.3 specifies the intra-cell split. Quantify the per-shard revocation-bitmap write amplification given that a tenant's records smear across N topical shards — reconcile this against the DS-high-lease-write-amp resolution which assumed bitmap refresh was cheap. Consider a hybrid: topical macro-shard + per-tenant sub-partition so a tenant's revocation bitmap stays local to a bounded shard set.
- **[HIGH]** The CDC / event-sync mechanics carry the tombstone-ordering and freshness-propagation guarantees, but the plan's sync stack is internally inconsistent across phases and the exactly-once-effect claim is overstated. Phase 0/1 use 'transactional-outbox polling'; Debezium+Kafka CDC arrives only at Phase 2 'when the central index first exists.' But the central index is ALSO Phase 2, and the freshness watermark + tombstone feed must reach BOTH cells AND the index. So in Phase 1 (first federation, first revocable grant, first freshness gate — per 3.5), there is NO Kafka and NO central index, yet the plan claims the authority 'emits a CDC tombstone' and feeds a watermark to consumers. What is the Phase-1 transport for the watermark/tombstone feed across cells if CDC doesn't exist until Phase 2? Outbox-polling has a polling interval that directly bounds the achievable leak-window — a 1s poll interval alone eats 20% of a 5s budget, and polling does not naturally fan out a single authority watermark to many consumers. Separately, 'exactly-once effect on at-least-once delivery via authority-version + idempotency keys' is correct for idempotent upserts/deletes but the plan also runs side-effecting consumers (notifications, key-rotation via Temporal, audit-receipt exchange) where idempotency keys do not make the EFFECT exactly-once for free — that requires per-consumer dedup state that is asserted but not designed.
  - *Why:* The revocation correctness story assumes a tombstone-and-watermark distribution substrate. If that substrate is outbox-polling in the exact phase the freshness gate first ships (Phase 1), the polling interval is an unacknowledged additive term in the leak-window budget, and the fan-out of one authority watermark to many cells over polling is unspecified. A guarantee that depends on a transport that doesn't exist yet in the phase it's promised is a phase-sequencing hole in the most important mechanism.
  - *Fix:* Specify the Phase-1 watermark/tombstone transport explicitly (a dedicated authority→cell push/long-poll feed independent of the Phase-2 Kafka CDC, since the central index isn't the only consumer — cells need it too). Add the polling/feed interval as an explicit line item in the leak-window budget (per the crit-1 recommendation). Distinguish idempotent-effect consumers (index upsert/delete — fine) from genuinely side-effecting consumers (notifications, key-rotation) and specify the dedup/once-effect mechanism for the latter, or state they are at-least-once with idempotent side effects designed in. Reconcile the Phase-1-no-Kafka / Phase-1-needs-watermark-feed contradiction in the 3.5 table.
- **[HIGH]** Brokered drill-down availability is computed as ~98.5% (product of A-PEP × token-mint × B-cell), the consequence is acknowledged in one line ('degrade to publishable-metadata-only'), but the product/scale implication is never confronted: at the platform's core value action, ~1.5% of cross-institution detail requests fail, and this compounds with the freshness-gate deny and the authority-availability deny into a much worse effective number. The plan treats degradation as benign UX ('detail unavailable'). But the entire premise of the federated tier is rich cross-institution discovery + drill-down. Three independent deny sources stack on the SAME request: (1) brokered-path component product ~98.5%, (2) freshness watermark stale-beyond-leak-window → deny, (3) authority unavailable → deny (crit-2). These are not independent in a correlated outage (a control-plane region blip hits token mint AND the authority AND the watermark feed simultaneously), so the real-world failure of cross-institution drill-down during any control-plane degradation approaches 100%, not 1.5%. The cross-region path (14.4.1) also omits the freshness-check round-trip latency entirely from its sum, and ignores tail amplification across the 6 serial stages (p95 of a 6-stage serial pipeline is NOT the sum of per-stage p95s — it's worse).
  - *Why:* The cross-institution drill-down is THE differentiated action the whole architecture exists to enable. If it is the least-available, most-deny-prone path in the system — and degrades to 'metadata only' precisely when a partner institution most wants the detail — the product's headline capability is its flakiest. Underselling this as a one-line UX note hides a real reliability ceiling that buyers' CISOs and end users will hit.
  - *Fix:* Model the COMPOUND availability of confidential cross-institution drill-down with correlated control-plane failure (token mint + authority + watermark feed share a failure domain). State the honest effective availability during a control-plane degradation (likely near-total deny, not 98.5%). Add the freshness-check latency to the 14.4.1 cross-region sum, and replace the additive p95 with a proper serial-pipeline tail estimate (or a measured end-to-end SLO). Decide whether 'metadata-only during degradation' is acceptable for the beachhead use case or whether some confidential reads need a cell-local cached-grant + last-good-watermark grace path (which reopens the partition tradeoff — make the call).
- **[MEDIUM]** Cell-local SpiceDB replication is asserted to provide 'ZedToken-monotonic read-after-write' with 'home-minted ZedToken carried along,' but cross-region replication of a Zanzibar-style tuple store with carried ZedTokens is non-trivial and the plan defers the entire mechanism to Open Question #5 while already committing to its SLO (p99 <50ms cell-local Check). SpiceDB's consistency model (ZedTokens / zookies) guarantees monotonicity relative to a revision in the cluster that minted them. Carrying a home-region-minted ZedToken into a remote cell-local replica and using it for a Check assumes the replica's revision is comparable to the home revision — which requires either a shared revision space (i.e., it's the same logical cluster, contradicting 'regional clusters + cross-region replication') or a translation layer that maps home-revisions to replica-revisions. The plan does not specify which. If grant tuples are replicated asynchronously into the cell, then a freshly-minted grant's ZedToken presented at the cell may reference a revision the replica hasn't received yet → the Check either blocks (latency hit, violating the 50ms SLO) or fails (grant appears not to exist). This is the dual of the revocation problem and is hand-waved.
  - *Why:* The grant-CHECK path is claimed as the 'strong, cell-local, 0-staleness' leg of the consistency model (10.4) and the basis for the 50ms SLO and partition-survivability. If the replication/ZedToken-carry mechanism isn't actually monotonic across regional clusters, the 'strong cell-local' claim is false and grant-creation-visibility races (already flagged as a UX issue) become correctness issues at the grant-Check itself.
  - *Fix:* Specify the replication model concretely: is it SpiceDB's own cross-cluster replication, a CDC-driven tuple feed, or watch-API mirroring? Define how a home-minted ZedToken is interpreted at a replica (revision translation table, or 'wait-for-replica-to-catch-up-to-carried-revision with a bounded timeout then fail-closed'). State the SLO honestly: cell-local Check is <50ms ONLY for grants whose tuples have already replicated; freshly-minted-elsewhere grants are subject to replication lag (bound it). Benchmark replication lag in the Phase-0/1 spike (Open Q #5 already lists this — but the SLO is committed in 14.4 before the spike, which is backwards).
- **[MEDIUM]** Publish-shedding under backpressure suspends the discovery freshness SLO and surfaces 'as-of staleness,' and tombstones are 'never shed' — but the interaction between shedding and the freshness WATERMARK (a security control) versus discovery POPULATION (a quality concern) is muddled, and 'tombstones never shed' has no admission-control backpressure story of its own. The plan correctly separates 'incomplete = security state (fail-closed)' from 'as-of staleness = quality flag.' But under sustained load, if publishes are shed while tombstones and watermark feeds are prioritized, the watermark/tombstone stream itself can saturate its own capacity (a mass-revocation event — e.g., a tenant offboarding or a bulk reclassification — produces a tombstone flood that 'never sheds'). There is no admission control or capacity model for the tombstone/watermark path when tombstones spike. If the tombstone stream backs up, watermark advancement stalls → consumers age past the leak-window → platform-wide fail-closed (the crit-2 blast radius, triggered by load rather than outage). The plan optimizes the common case (shed enrichment, keep tombstones) but doesn't model the adversarial/bulk case where the un-sheddable stream is itself the bottleneck.
  - *Why:* Backpressure design that protects the security stream by making it un-sheddable only works if the security stream has bounded volume. Bulk reclassification, tenant offboarding, or a compromised-tenant mass-revoke can produce tombstone bursts that saturate the prioritized lane, stalling watermark advancement and tripping global fail-closed under load — exactly when the system is busiest.
