  - *Fix:* Add a capacity/admission-control model for the tombstone+watermark stream specifically: bound the tombstone burst rate, define batching/coalescing for bulk revocations (a single 'tenant-version revoked' supersedes N per-record tombstones), and specify what happens when even the prioritized lane saturates (graceful watermark-advance via coarser-grained bulk tombstones vs fail-closed). Model the worst case (tenant offboarding = mass tombstone) explicitly in the 10.2 backpressure section, not just the steady-state shed-enrichment case.
- **[MEDIUM]** The knowledge-graph scaling gate (AGE multi-hop + PPR) is well-flagged for benchmarking, but the cross-institution / federated graph dimension is entirely absent from the scaling analysis — the graph story is per-cell only, while features 1 and 4 (cross-institution expert discovery, cross-institution team assembly) require traversing a graph that spans cells. Section 6.2 benchmarks AGE at 'a realistic R1 graph size (~0.5–5M nodes per cell).' Section 9.3 says cross-institution team assembly runs 'over the federated public-tier graph.' But there is no design for how a multi-hop graph query (PPR, collaborator-of-collaborator, team assembly) executes ACROSS cells when the edges/nodes live in different tenants' cells and only public-tier projections reach the central index. PPR is inherently a global-graph algorithm (it needs the whole adjacency structure to compute personalized rank); you cannot run it correctly over a sharded-by-cell graph without either (a) materializing the federated public-tier graph centrally (a second large stateful central store nobody costed) or (b) a distributed graph-traversal protocol across cells (cross-cell latency per hop — multi-hop = multi-RTT, blowing any latency budget). The plan picks neither.
  - *Why:* Two of the four locked features are cross-institution graph features, and graph algorithms don't decompose cleanly across a federation. 'Federated public-tier graph' is named as if it's a place, but it's actually either a third big central index (uncosted, another ops sink, another honeypot) or a distributed multi-RTT traversal (latency-fatal for interactive discovery). This is the same 'naming a mechanism isn't designing it' pattern as the watermark, applied to the graph.
  - *Fix:* Decide and specify: is the federated public-tier graph (a) materialized as a central graph store (then cost it at N=10/50/200, give it a sharding/ops/honeypot story like the vector index got, and note PPR compute cost at federated scale), or (b) computed by distributed traversal (then state the per-hop cross-cell RTT and cap hop count for interactive use, accepting approximate/local PPR). For team assembly, consider precomputing expertise fingerprints centrally (already proposed via IExpertiseFingerprint) and doing graph augmentation only within a bounded candidate set, avoiding global PPR across the federation. Add this as a first-class scaling section, not a one-liner.
- **[LOW]** Hot-tenant fairness relies on 'per-tenant write/query quotas + fair scheduling' on the managed central index, but with topical (not tenant) sharding, per-tenant quotas don't map cleanly onto shards — a mega-R1's queries and writes smear across topical shards, so a per-tenant quota cannot protect a specific shard from a hot topic shared by many tenants. The noisy-neighbor model in 14.5 assumes tenant-aligned resources (true for cells, true for tenant-sharded stores) but the central index is deliberately topic-sharded. A hot topical shard is hot because MANY tenants query/write that topic, so no single per-tenant quota throttles it; you need per-SHARD admission control and per-(tenant×topic) accounting, which the plan doesn't mention.
  - *Why:* The fairness mechanism is specified against the wrong partitioning axis. Per-tenant quotas protect against one greedy tenant but not against an organically hot topical shard, which is the actual p95 risk the topical sharding introduced (see the high-severity topical-skew finding). Mismatched isolation primitives leave the real hotspot ungoverned.
  - *Fix:* Add per-shard admission control / load-shedding on the central index in addition to per-tenant quotas, and account hot-topic cost at the (tenant×topic) granularity for billing and throttling. Reconcile 14.5's tenant-aligned fairness language with 4.6's topic-aligned sharding so the isolation primitive matches the partition key.

### Lens: Security, privacy & confidentiality — end-to-end enforcement of public/private/confidential, authz completeness (ABAC+ReBAC), cross-institution sharing/revocation, confidential-tier crypto/BYOK, federation confused-deputy/IDOR, FERPA/GDPR/export-control correctness

*Verdict:* This is a genuinely strong v3 from a security standpoint — markedly better than most architecture plans I review. It correctly retracts the "read-time re-check makes correctness latency-independent" fantasy, anchors revocation ordering to a central monotonic authority instead of trusting node clocks, refuses to publish raw invertible embeddings, splits the operator-trust claim honestly per deployment tier, makes the egress gate a 100%-blocking structural type rather than a sampling detector, and re-evaluates sticky caveats at the accessing individual for deemed-export/FERPA. Those are the hard things and they are largely right. BUT the plan repeatedly conflates "decided and named" with "secure," and several named controls have unclosed leak paths hiding inside the very mechanisms it claims resolve them. The freshness-watermark model has a fail-open gap during the leak-window before the watermark goes stale; the LSH-codes-are-non-invertible claim is asserted, not proven, and the index aggregates a cross-tenant honeypot of them; the per-shard revocation bitmap creates a window-of-staleness AND a covert membership channel; subject-deprovisioning still loses the already-minted token within the leak-window for non-confidential tiers; the audit-before-serve and counter-signed receipts have no defined behavior when the two sides disagree; and several compliance gates (FERPA derived-data, GDPR processor-vs-controller, export-control quarantine-at-ingest correctness, BYO-endpoint attestation) have softer enforcement than the prose implies. None of these sink the platform, but #1–#6 below are serious design gaps that must be closed before the confidentiality guarantee is real rather than rhetorical.

- **[CRITICAL]** The freshness-watermark gate is fail-OPEN inside the leak-window, and the leak-window applies to the WRONG event. The plan's invariant is: surface/drill-down is denied only when the watermark is older than (now − leak-window). But a revocation that was committed to the authority 1 second ago is, by construction, INSIDE the leak-window — so a cell whose watermark is fresh (e.g. 2s old) will happily serve data that was revoked 1s ago, because the watermark says 'I am fresh' while not yet carrying the new tombstone. Freshness of the watermark is not the same as having-seen-the-revocation. The gate proves 'my replication channel is alive,' NOT 'this specific grant is not revoked.' The plan conflates liveness-of-channel with currency-of-decision. So the actual exposure window for any single revocation is the full propagation latency (commit-at-authority → tombstone reaches this cell/shard), which is exactly the leak-window the plan claims to bound — but it is served as ALLOW, not deny, the whole time. The fail-closed only triggers on TOTAL channel staleness (partition), not on per-record propagation delay.
  - *Why:* This is the central confidentiality claim of the entire platform. The headline invariant says revocation is 'genuinely correct under partition' and 'fail-closed when the freshness watermark is older than the leak-window.' But the dangerous case is not partition — it is the normal-operation propagation delay of a single fresh revocation, during which the gate is fail-OPEN. An attacker (or a deprovisioned insider, or a partner whose grant was just pulled because of an IP dispute) retains access for the full propagation window on a channel that reports itself healthy. The plan markets a deny-by-default revocation gate that is actually allow-by-default for the first leak-window seconds after every revocation. For confidential research IP and ITAR-adjacent data, 'we leak for up to 5 seconds after every revoke, by design, on a healthy channel' is a materially different (and weaker) guarantee than what every customer-facing sentence in the doc states.
  - *Fix:* Separate the two properties explicitly. (a) For confidential drill-down, do NOT rely on watermark-freshness alone; require a SYNCHRONOUS authoritative revocation check (or a linearizable read of the authority's tombstone-version for the specific grant_id/record/subject) on the hot path for confidential tier, accepting the latency, OR (b) state the guarantee honestly as 'eventual revocation with a bounded propagation window during which already-authorized confidential access continues to be served — this is an ALLOW window, not a deny window' and make the leak-window a contractual exposure bound, not a correctness claim. The current text lets a reader believe revocation is effectively immediate-and-fail-closed; it is bounded-delay-and-fail-open-within-the-bound. Pick the synchronous check for the highest-sensitivity tier (export/ITAR-adjacent) and document the async window for the rest. Also add a 'revocation acknowledgment' protocol: the authority does not consider a revoke fully effective until all subscribed cells/shards ACK the tombstone version, and high-sensitivity grants can be configured to block reads until ACK.
- **[CRITICAL]** The central discovery index is a cross-tenant aggregation of per-tenant LSH codes whose non-invertibility is ASSERTED, not established, and the plan's own composition threat undermines it. LSH codes are designed to preserve neighborhood structure — that is precisely what makes them useful for candidate generation AND what makes them leak. Per-tenant salting/keying defends against cross-tenant linkage of identical inputs, but does NOT prevent (i) within-tenant membership inference (does institution B have a paper near query vector q?), (ii) reconstruction-by-triangulation when an adversary controls or colludes with one tenant and can issue crafted queries to map another tenant's code space, or (iii) the plan's own named composition channel: PSI overlap signal + index membership signal. The index ALSO carries discoverability scope, authority_version, and lease metadata per record — so even if the code itself were perfectly opaque, the EXISTENCE, COUNT, TOPICAL CLUSTER, and CHURN (publish/tombstone events) of a tenant's confidential-adjacent projections are observable to anyone with index query access and to the control-plane operator. 'Federation-scoped projection content minimized' is hand-waving; the metadata IS the leak.
  - *Why:* The plan correctly identifies this as the existential surface (Risk #1) and correctly removes raw embeddings — but then re-creates a softer version of the same honeypot and declares it safe by fiat ('LSH codes only,' 'non-invertible,' 'per-tenant-keyed salted'). A reviewer at a target CISO's office will ask for the proof that these codes resist a vec2text-class or membership-inference attack under the platform's OWN adversary model (a colluding tenant + control-plane visibility), and the plan offers a Phase-2 red-team (Open Q #8) AFTER the index ships in Phase 2. That means the honeypot goes live before the privacy property is validated. For a confidentiality-first platform whose entire moat is 'confidential never centralizes,' shipping an unvalidated cross-tenant statistical honeypot is a contradiction of the core promise.
  - *Fix:* Move the LSH/membership-inference red-team to a GATE that blocks central-index GA, not a Phase-2 open question. Specifically: (1) Default confidential-adjacent tenants to brokered-only (no index presence at all) — the plan says opt-out is the default for 'export-controlled/high-sensitivity' but should extend this to ALL confidential-derived projections; the index should hold PUBLIC-tier and explicitly-shared-federation-tier projections only, never anything derived from confidential corpora even after projection. (2) Treat index PRESENCE/COUNT/CHURN metadata as classified — add per-tenant differential-privacy noise or k-anonymity floors to count/existence queries, and encrypt scope+version metadata under per-consortium keys (the plan does this only for named-scope content, not for the existence signal). (3) Specify the concrete LSH scheme + a quantified bound (e.g. (ε,δ) membership-inference resistance) in shared/contracts, and make the red-team a release gate. Until then, do not claim the index is privacy-safe; claim only that it holds non-confidential-derived projections.
- **[HIGH]** Subject deprovisioning is only enforced for CONFIDENTIAL drill-down, leaving private/own-materials and any non-drill-down surface exposed for the full token TTL. Section 7.7 / 11.5 say 'Confidential drill-down requires a fresh subject-not-deprovisioned watermark within the leak-window' and 'very short confidential-tier token TTLs.' But a deprovisioned insider (terminated employee, revoked collaborator) retains: (a) access to PRIVATE(institution-internal) and private(self) tiers until their token expires — no subject-freshness check is specified there; (b) any cached/streamed session in progress; (c) discovery-index query access (the discovery PEP checks 'authz + discoverability scope' but the plan does not state it re-checks subject-not-deprovisioned freshness on every discovery query). The plan scopes the strongest control to the narrowest surface and leaves the broad surface on bearer-token-expiry semantics.
  - *Why:* Insider deprovisioning is one of the most common real breach vectors in university environments (grad students leaving, visiting researchers, terminated staff with grievances). The threat model entry (SEC-med-deprovision) is treated as a confidential-tier-only problem, but the bulk of sensitive material a deprovisioned insider would exfiltrate is institution-private and own-materials — exactly the tiers with no subject-freshness gate. A breach here is a FERPA/contractual-confidentiality incident even if no cross-institution confidential record is touched.
  - *Fix:* Extend the subject-not-deprovisioned freshness check to ALL non-public tiers and to the discovery PEP, not just confidential drill-down. Make subject-revocation a first-class check in the standard PEP decision flow (7.4) alongside grant/record freshness, evaluated on every request that touches private or confidential data. Keep token TTLs short across the board for non-public access, and add server-side session-kill that is triggered by the SCIM deprovision event (push, not pull) for in-flight sessions. State the residual exposure window explicitly per tier.
- **[HIGH]** Audit-before-serve and counter-signed reconciliation have no defined behavior when the two sides DISAGREE — the plan detects divergence but never says what divergence MEANS or triggers. Section 11.2 says 'Cells exchange counter-signed audit receipts; a two-sided reconciliation job detects divergence.' Detection without a defined response is not a control. What happens when grantor's log shows an access the grantee's log denies (or vice versa)? Is the grant frozen? Is it a P1? Is access suspended pending investigation? Is there a non-repudiation tie-break (whose signed record wins)? Additionally, 'an access that cannot be durably logged on the OWNER side is DENIED' protects the owner but says nothing about the requester-side record — a malicious owner could serve data and then claim it never logged the access (repudiation of disclosure), or log a DIFFERENT scope than it served. The receipts are counter-signed but the protocol for what the requester must counter-sign BEFORE receiving data (vs. after) is unspecified, which is the difference between a real non-repudiation protocol and two independent logs that happen to be signed.
  - *Why:* For cross-institution confidential sharing, the audit log IS the legal artifact in a DUA/IP dispute. 'We detect divergence' with no defined consequence means the divergence is discovered post-hoc and resolved by lawyers, not by the system. The plan explicitly criticized v2 for 'a divergent/unbindable two-sided log is worthless as a legal artifact' and then ships a log whose divergence handling is undefined — re-creating the same flaw at a deeper level.
  - *Fix:* Specify the protocol: (1) Requester must obtain a signed access-receipt FROM the owner that binds (grant_id, grant_version, scope, token_jti, watermark, timestamp) and the owner must obtain a signed request-receipt from the requester BEFORE bytes are served (fair-exchange / commit then serve). (2) Define divergence consequences: any reconciliation mismatch on a confidential grant auto-suspends that grant pending manual adjudication and raises a P1, with both signed records preserved. (3) Define the tie-break/non-repudiation rule (e.g., the owner-side WORM-anchored record is authoritative for disclosure; the requester-side record is authoritative for receipt). (4) Anchor both sides to the same external timestamp authority so 'who logged what when' is provable.
- **[HIGH]** Derived-data classification (MAX-tier join) is enforced at derivative CREATION but the embedding/LSH publication path for derivatives of confidential data is not blocked by the same rule — and the egress PEP re-derives classification of the SOURCE record, not of the projection's provenance. The plan says every derivative inherits MAX(input tiers) and that the egress PEP 're-derives/sanity-checks the source record's classification.' But an LSH code or expertise-fingerprint vector derived from a confidential corpus is itself a derivative whose tier should be confidential by the MAX rule — yet the index is explicitly designed to RECEIVE such codes ('per-tenant-keyed salted LSH codes for candidate generation' from confidential records, with brokered final scoring). So the system intentionally publishes a confidential-derived artifact to the shared plane. The egress PEP re-deriving the SOURCE classification would flag this as confidential and block it — UNLESS the projection is treated as a special-cased exception that bypasses the MAX rule. That exception is the leak path, and it is not reconciled: either the MAX rule holds (and LSH-from-confidential cannot be published, contradicting the index design) or it has a carve-out (which is exactly the kind of silent downgrade the dual-classifier was supposed to prevent).
  - *Why:* This is an internal contradiction between two load-bearing controls (the MAX-tier derived-data rule + egress-PEP source-class re-derivation) and the central-index design. If a reviewer follows the type system honestly, publishing an LSH code derived from a confidential document is a tier violation; the plan resolves this implicitly by declaring the projection 'publishable,' which means the publishability decision OVERRIDES the classification — re-introducing trust in the projection-author's judgment that the dual-classifier and MAX-rule were meant to remove.
  - *Fix:* Make the provenance explicit in the type system: a PublishableProjection carries the tier(s) of its source(s), and the egress PEP enforces that NO field (including LSH codes) derived from a confidential source may be published unless there is an explicit, audited, dual-classifier-agreed, grantor-consented declassification of THAT derived artifact — not just a per-field allowlist. Better: forbid confidential-derived codes in the index entirely (per finding #2's recommendation) and require brokered candidate generation for confidential corpora, so the MAX rule and the index design stop contradicting each other.
- **[HIGH]** Managed-cell operator is in the TCB AND holds the key-unwrap path AND runs the egress PEP — so for the default (Phase 0–1) deployment, the entire confidentiality guarantee reduces to trusting the vendor, yet the same deployment is sold to 'security-sensitive funded R1 centers' as the confidential-tier product. The plan is commendably honest that managed = bounded processor, not zero-trust, and reserves 'provably' for sovereign+TEE. But it does not reconcile that the Phase-0 beachhead (the FIRST revenue, the security-line-item buyer) is sold the MANAGED cell where the vendor can, by construction, read plaintext (key-unwrap held, no TEE, vendor runs the inference host and the egress gate). A 'security-sensitive' center's CISO doing real diligence will find that the vendor's own engineers and a compromised vendor control plane can read confidential research IP. The plan's mitigations (disabled swap, disabled core dumps, ephemeral DEK caching) are insider-deterrents, not insider-PREVENTION, and do nothing against a malicious-vendor or vendor-compromise scenario for the exact buyer who is paying for confidentiality.
  - *Why:* The go-to-market sequence sells the WEAKEST trust tier to the buyer most likely to scrutinize trust. There is a real risk of a failed security review killing the beachhead deal precisely because the confidential-tier guarantee at N=1 is 'trust us' while the marketing says 'confidential never leaves your boundary.' This is both a security gap (vendor in TCB for confidential data) and a sales-integrity / sustainability gap.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) bring TEE-backed inference into Phase-0 for the confidential tier of the managed beachhead cell (the plan already brings TEE early for export-class buyers — extend it to the security-sensitive R1 center, since that buyer's whole value prop is confidentiality), accepting the COGS hit; or (b) offer the beachhead buyer HYOK from day one (university holds the KEK, vendor cannot unwrap without university-held key) even on managed infra, so vendor-compromise cannot read plaintext-at-rest; or (c) be explicit in the sales motion and contract that the Phase-0 managed confidential tier is 'vendor-as-bounded-processor with contractual + technical controls, NOT cryptographically vendor-blind' and confirm in the Week-1 buyer-discovery spike that the beachhead CISO accepts that trust model. Do not let the gap between the marketed invariant and the managed-cell reality surface for the first time inside a paid security review.
- **[HIGH]** The per-shard revocation bitmap creates BOTH a fail-open staleness window per shard AND a covert membership/inference channel via the 'incomplete' security state. (a) Each shard refreshes its bitmap 'within the leak-window'; a revocation committed to the authority is not in a given shard's bitmap until that shard refreshes — so the same fail-open-within-the-window problem as finding #1 applies per shard, and a shard reporting itself fresh can still surface a just-revoked hit. (b) The 'incomplete' flag is returned to the QUERYING user. Because incomplete fires when a shard's bitmap is stale, and bitmap staleness correlates with revocation churn on specific topical clusters (shards are sharded BY TOPIC), an adversary issuing topical queries can use the incomplete signal as a side channel to infer WHICH topical areas are experiencing revocation activity across the federation — i.e., which research areas have confidential grants being pulled. Topical-cluster sharding + per-shard completeness leakage = a cross-institution research-activity inference channel.
  - *Why:* The plan elevates 'incomplete' from a quality flag to a security state (good) but does not consider that exposing a security state to the querier is itself an information disclosure. Combined with topic-based sharding, the completeness signal leaks the rhythm of confidential collaboration changes across institutions — exactly the kind of competitive/strategic intelligence the confidentiality tier exists to protect.
  - *Fix:* (1) Apply finding #1's synchronous-check or ACK-protocol logic to the bitmap: a shard should not surface a hit whose tombstone-version it cannot prove it has seen; consider serving the candidate but gating final reveal through the owning cell's synchronous check (the plan already brokers final scoring — push the authoritative revocation check there too, making the index bitmap a performance optimization, not the authority). (2) Do NOT expose raw 'incomplete' per-shard to the user; aggregate completeness coarsely, add noise, or fail the WHOLE query closed rather than leaking per-shard/per-topic completeness. (3) Decouple shard topology from topic for the completeness signal, or add cover traffic, so incomplete cannot be correlated to a topical cluster's revocation activity.
- **[HIGH]** BYO-endpoint for confidential routing trusts an unverified institutional ATTESTATION that the endpoint is in-boundary — an attestation-spoofing / misconfiguration leak path straight through the router's strongest invariant. Section 8.2: 'BYO may point at a self-hosted endpoint for confidential ONLY if the institution attests it is in-boundary.' This is a self-asserted claim by the tenant admin, with no technical verification that the attested endpoint is actually (a) in the data-residency region, (b) not a proxy to a cloud frontier model, (c) not logging/retaining prompts, (d) actually the model it claims. The entire 'confidential routes local only' invariant — which the plan pins, tests, and hard-fails on — has a configuration-time bypass: an admin (compromised, mistaken, or malicious) attests a cloud-proxying endpoint as in-boundary, and confidential prompts flow to a frontier provider while the system believes it is compliant. The transport-policy hard-fail checks the router's DECISION against intrinsic tier, but if the configured 'local' endpoint is secretly remote, transport policy sees a 'local' selection and passes it.
  - *Why:* This is a direct end-run around the confidential-never-leaves-boundary guarantee, gated only by a checkbox attestation. For a platform whose moat is structural confidentiality, allowing confidential inference to a BYO endpoint validated by trust-me is a contradiction. It is also a deemed-export and GDPR-transfer hole: confidential ITAR-adjacent or EU-personal-data prompts could egress to a US/foreign cloud through a mis-attested 'in-boundary' endpoint.
  - *Fix:* For confidential-tier BYO endpoints, require TECHNICAL verification, not attestation: (1) network-level proof the endpoint resolves and routes within the tenant's declared boundary/region (egress-policy-enforced, not attested); (2) endpoint attestation via mTLS + a challenge that confirms the model identity and a no-retention contract enforced where possible (TEE attestation of the BYO host for the strongest tier); (3) for export-controlled/EU-personal data, FORBID BYO confidential endpoints unless they pass the sovereign-tier verification. Treat the in-boundary claim as an assertion that must be technically validated before any confidential prompt is routed there, and audit every confidential inference with the verified endpoint identity.
- **[MEDIUM]** FERPA handling encodes institutional approval and grantee-side role re-check but does not address FERPA's flow-down to DERIVED data, aggregates, and the audit log itself. A confidential/private answer synthesized from a corpus that includes FERPA-protected student records (e.g., a grounded Q&A over internal docs that reference advisees, or an expertise fingerprint built partly from student-coauthored work) is a derivative that may carry student-record character, but the FERPA gate is specified only on records flagged contains_FERPA_student_record and on cross-institution grants of those records. The MAX-tier rule handles confidentiality TIER but not the FERPA disclosure-authorization ATTRIBUTE — a derivative could be tier-correct (confidential) yet still be a FERPA disclosure when surfaced to someone without the institutional authorization. Also, the audit log explicitly 'can reveal confidential relationships' and is access-controlled, but audit content over FERPA-flagged access is itself an education-record disclosure that needs FERPA-grade authz, which the plan does not state.
  - *Why:* FERPA attaches to the personally identifiable information from education records wherever it flows, including derivatives and disclosures-about-disclosures. A tier-correct but FERPA-uncleared synthesis or audit view is a compliance violation even though the confidentiality machinery worked as designed. FERPA is a locked requirement.
  - *Fix:* Propagate a FERPA attribute (and IRB/export attributes) as STICKY metadata on derivatives via the same join logic as tier (a derivative inherits the union of source compliance flags, not just MAX tier), and gate surfacing of FERPA-tainted derivatives on the same institutional-authorization + grantee-role check. Apply FERPA-grade authz to audit content that records access to education records. Add a contract test: a synthesis drawing on a FERPA-flagged source carries the FERPA flag to the output and is gated accordingly.
- **[MEDIUM]** Export-control quarantine-at-ingest and 'unknown ⇒ controlled' is correct in spirit but the QUARANTINE state's enforcement surface is underspecified — quarantined data is still in the cell, indexed, and potentially retrievable before adjudication. The plan says unadjudicated potentially-controlled ingest 'enters a QUARANTINE state' and quarantined=true 'until export adjudication.' But the pipeline (Section 10.1) runs classify AFTER embed/index ('embed → index → graph → classify'), meaning the document is embedded and indexed into the cell's retrieval stores BEFORE the dual classifier and quarantine decision run. A query during that window could retrieve a quarantined/unadjudicated potentially-ITAR passage. The quarantine flag gates the egress PEP and (presumably) surfacing, but the ordering of pipeline stages means the data is searchable in-cell before it is classified, and the plan does not state that quarantined records are excluded from retrieval results.
  - *Why:* Deemed-export liability can attach to in-cell access by a non-US-person to controlled technical data, not only to cross-institution egress. If quarantined-but-indexed data is retrievable by any cell user before adjudication, the export gate (which the plan correctly fires on classification) is bypassed by the ingest-ordering for the in-cell case.
  - *Fix:* Reorder so classification (at least the fail-closed default-to-controlled/quarantine decision) gates INDEXING, not just egress — or make the retrieval layer hard-exclude quarantined and unadjudicated-export records from ALL results until adjudication, enforced at IRetrieval with a contract test. State explicitly that quarantine suppresses in-cell retrievability for export-sensitive tenants, not merely cross-institution publication.
- **[MEDIUM]** GDPR processor-vs-controller posture is asserted ('We = processor') but the central index and cross-tenant federated discovery look like CONTROLLER or joint-controller activities, which changes the legal obligations the plan is designed around. As a pure processor, the vendor only processes on documented instructions of each tenant-controller. But the central discovery index aggregates projections across tenants and serves cross-institution discovery — the vendor is determining means/purposes of a cross-tenant processing operation that no single tenant-controller instructed. That is at least joint-controllership for the exchange layer, triggering Art. 26 joint-controller arrangements, transparency obligations, and a lawful-basis determination for the cross-tenant processing — none of which the plan accounts for (it assumes a simple DPA + SCC processor model).
  - *Why:* Mis-characterizing the controllership of the exchange layer means the wrong legal instruments are prepared (DPA where a joint-controller agreement is needed), the wrong party bears certain obligations, and the lawful basis for cross-tenant processing of personal data (researcher PII in profiles, even if 'public-tier') is unestablished. GDPR is a locked requirement and EU consortia are an explicit target.
  - *Fix:* Get a GDPR controllership determination for EACH plane (cell = processor on tenant instruction; exchange/central-index = likely joint-controller or independent controller for the cross-tenant discovery purpose) BEFORE the index ships, and prepare Art. 26 joint-controller arrangements plus a documented lawful basis (likely legitimate-interest balancing for researcher discovery, with opt-out) for the exchange-layer processing. Add this to Open Q #13 as a release gate for the EU index, not a post-hoc DPO chat.
- **[MEDIUM]** Sticky-caveat re-evaluation depends on grantee-side attributes (US-person, FERPA role) that originate from the GRANTEE institution's IdP — a confused-deputy / attribute-spoofing vector at the attribute source, not just at the token. The plan correctly resolves the subject from the control-plane-minted token (not the requesting cell's assertion) and re-evaluates caveats against the accessing individual. But the US-person/FERPA-role ATTRIBUTES used in that re-evaluation come from the grantee institution's identity/attribute provider. The grantor cell is now making an export-control / FERPA decision based on attributes asserted by the OTHER institution. A misconfigured or malicious grantee IdP that over-asserts 'US-person=true' or 'FERPA-authorized=true' causes the grantor's PEP to release controlled/education-record data to an ineligible individual — and the deemed-export liability lands on the GRANTOR. The plan says 'nationality is NOT an SSO claim' (good) but then the US-person determination must come from somewhere; if it is the grantor's institution-attested allowlist keyed by a grantee-asserted identity, the binding between the attested determination and the actual accessing human depends on the grantee IdP's honesty.
  - *Why:* Export-control and FERPA decisions are being made cross-trust-boundary on attributes from the less-trusted side. The whole point of deemed-export re-evaluation at the individual is defeated if the individual's eligibility attributes are spoofable by the institution that benefits from access.
  - *Fix:* Require that export-control US-person determinations be attested by the DATA OWNER's (grantor's) export-control officer against a specific, owner-verified identity of the accessing individual (e.g., an owner-side allowlist of named, verified persons per grant), NOT inferred from grantee-IdP-asserted attributes. For FERPA, require the grantor's registrar/compliance to authorize the specific accessing role/person. Treat grantee-asserted attributes as untrusted hints that must be confirmed against owner-side authoritative determinations before releasing controlled/education-record data. Add a test: a grantee IdP over-asserting US-person=true must NOT cause release without an owner-side verified determination.
- **[MEDIUM]** Revocation Authority is single-region linearizable and is the global correctness anchor for grants, records, AND subjects — making it a single point of total confidentiality failure whose compromise or unavailability is under-modeled. The plan makes it single-region for linearizability (defensible) and fail-closed on partition (good), but: (a) its compromise lets an attacker assign tombstone versions / advance watermarks arbitrarily — including REGRESSING the security posture by issuing fake 'fresh' watermarks or suppressing tombstones (the plan says nodes can't outrun the authority, but the authority itself is now the single trust root with no stated integrity protection — is it signing tombstone versions? who verifies?); (b) the control plane operator runs it, so for managed deployments the operator controls the revocation truth for ALL tenants including cross-institution grants; (c) its blast radius on availability is global confidential-discovery denial (correct fail-closed, but the plan's 99.95% control-plane SLO covers it loosely while it gates every confidential cross-tenant op).
  - *Why:* Concentrating grant+record+subject revocation truth in one operator-run single-region store re-centralizes a confidentiality-critical decision in exactly the layer the architecture promises is 'thin' and untrusted-for-confidential-bytes. Its bytes aren't confidential, but its DECISIONS are the confidentiality control plane. A compromise is a federation-wide authz bypass.
  - *Fix:* (1) Sign every tombstone/watermark with an authority key and have cells verify signatures, so a compromised replication path cannot forge freshness; rotate and HSM-protect that key. (2) Make watermarks monotonic and replay-protected at the consumer so a regressed/stale watermark cannot be replayed as fresh. (3) For sovereign/HYOK tenants, allow the tenant to run or co-sign their own revocation authority for their grants so the managed operator is not the sole arbiter of their confidential access. (4) Model authority compromise explicitly in the threat table (currently absent) with detection (anomalous watermark/version regressions) and containment. (5) Give the authority its own availability SLO and a defined global-fail-closed runbook, since it gates every confidential cross-tenant operation.
- **[MEDIUM]** 'Revoke access (enforced) vs revoke disclosure (contractual)' honestly admits that already-disclosed confidential bytes in a grantee cell are recoverable only by DUA + audit + best-effort technical revoke — but zero-copy-by-default is not zero-copy-always, and the conditions under which a durable copy DOES land in the grantee cell (and thus becomes only contractually revocable) are not enumerated or gated. The plan defaults to zero-copy query-where-data-resides, which is good, but workspaces (pillar 3) and any cached/materialized cross-institution result inherently create grantee-side state. The boundary between 'enforced revoke' and 'contractual revoke only' shifts silently depending on whether a given operation materialized data — and the user/grantor is not told, at grant time, which mode applies. So a grantor believes they have revocable control while a workspace operation has already created a durably-copied, only-contractually-revocable artifact.
  - *Why:* The platform's核心 selling point is REVOCABLE cross-institution sharing. If revocability silently degrades from technical to contractual depending on the operation, the grantor's mental model (and the contract's representation) is wrong, and a revocation that the grantor believes is enforced is actually a polite request. This is the gap between 'revoke' and 'unshare' that the plan names but does not operationally close.
  - *Fix:* Make copy-creation an explicit, audited, grantor-consented decision: any operation that would materialize a durable grantee-side copy of confidential data must (1) be disclosed to the grantor at grant/operation time, (2) require an explicit caveat flag (allow_durable_copy, default false), and (3) downgrade the revocation guarantee for that artifact to 'contractual + best-effort crypto-shred' EXPLICITLY in the UI and audit. Default workspaces to zero-copy/brokered; require opt-in (with grantor consent) for any durable materialization. Surface per-grant whether revocation is technically enforced or contractual.
- **[LOW]** DPoP-bound, short-lived, per-tenant-signed tokens are specified, but token replay/theft within the (short) TTL and the DPoP key-binding's protection against a compromised CLIENT are not analyzed for the confidential drill-down path. DPoP binds the token to a client-held key, defeating bearer-token replay by a network attacker — but not exfiltration by a compromised client/agent that holds the DPoP private key, nor a malicious requesting cell that legitimately holds the key and abuses it within scope. Given the plan's own agentic stages and brokered cross-cell calls, the DPoP key custody (where it lives, whether agents can access it, rotation) matters for the confused-deputy story.
  - *Why:* The confused-deputy mitigation leans on DPoP + audience-scoping; if the DPoP key is accessible to an in-cell agent or a compromised cell process, the binding adds less than implied. Minor relative to the structural controls, but worth pinning since agents act under tier-pinned capabilities elsewhere.
  - *Fix:* Specify DPoP key custody (per-session, non-exportable, not accessible to agent code; ideally in an HSM/enclave for confidential drill-down), short proof freshness windows, and per-jti single-use binding so a stolen proof cannot be reused. State that agent capabilities never include the DPoP signing key for cross-cell confidential requests.

### Lens: Maintainability & modularity: module boundaries, decoupling, blast radius, contract clarity, coupling, testability, monorepo-vs-services, swappability of AI-router and retrieval

*Verdict:* This is an unusually self-aware plan on the modularity lens — it has clearly absorbed a prior round of maintainability critique and bolted on real mechanisms: a machine-verified acyclic module DAG, DB-permission isolation atop import-linter, an owned TierLattice with compiler-enforced exhaustiveness, a versioned PublishableProjection with cross-version intersection testing, the IExpertiseGraph split, the router-as-pure-selection / transport-as-enforcement separation, the retrieval planner-graph, and a local federation test harness. These are the right shapes. But the plan confuses *naming a mechanism* with *having designed it*, and that gap is exactly where this architecture will rot. The most cross-cutting concept — the tier lattice and its classification semantics — is enforced by a "typed exhaustiveness check" that does not exist as described in Python and is asserted across five independently-deployed consumers with no shared compilation unit. The kernel is the real coupling sink: it now owns the classifier, lattice, router, planner, audit, identity resolution, egress, publish-metadata, AND two read-model projections — every module depends on it, and the plan never bounds kernel change frequency or versions the kernel as a whole. The "additive-only forever" rule for read-models and projections is an unmaintainable promise that guarantees schema bloat with no deprecation path actually exercised. And the monorepo-vs-services story is internally contradictory: deploy-as-independent-service modules sharing one consolidated Postgres with cross-schema role grants is distributed-monolith coupling, not decoupling. The plan earns credit for testability investment and for making boundaries machine-checkable in principle, but several of those checks are aspirational and the kernel's god-object gravity is unaddressed.

- **[CRITICAL]** The TierLattice 'compiler-enforced exhaustiveness check' that gates a new tier across all five consumers (router, MAX-derivation, egress allowlist, Cedar narrow-only, edition-posture derivation) is the load-bearing safety mechanism for the most cross-cutting concept in the system — but it cannot exist as described. The consumers are independently-deployed services (modules are 'deployed as independent service/Helm sub-chart', cells run different substrate profiles, nodes upgrade independently). There is no shared compilation unit across a managed cell, a sovereign single-appliance, and the central exchange, so a single typed total-match check cannot 'FAIL THE BUILD' across all of them. Python's type system has no exhaustive enum match that fails compilation regardless (it requires mypy + assert_never wiring per call site, and only catches sites that actually pattern-match — a consumer that does dict.get(tier) or a runtime config lookup is invisible to it).
  - *Why:* The plan's own decision log says 'adding a tier is a checklisted, test-gated policy-kernel release' and that the exhaustiveness check prevents a tier change from silently shipping a confidentiality leak (Risk #25). If the check is partial (catches some consumers, misses runtime-dispatch and remote consumers), then the team will believe a new tier is fully handled when it is not — the most dangerous failure mode, because it removes the human vigilance the checklist was supposed to enforce. A new tier that the egress allowlist or a remote node's projection logic doesn't handle is a leak, and this is sold as the thing that prevents exactly that.
  - *Fix:* Stop claiming compiler-enforced exhaustiveness across distributed consumers. Make the tier set a versioned artifact in shared/contracts/ with an explicit per-consumer conformance registry: each consumer (including each deployed node/substrate) must publish, at startup and in CI, a signed attestation that it handles tier set version N, and the control plane MUST refuse to activate tier N+1 until every live consumer has attested support. Pair mypy assert_never (which genuinely works for in-process pattern matches) with an explicit runtime registry test that enumerates every consumer's declared tier coverage. Treat unhandled-tier as a fail-closed runtime deny, not just a build gate, because the build gate provably cannot cover remote independently-versioned consumers.
- **[HIGH]** The kernel is a god-object by accumulation and the plan never bounds it. It owns: TierLattice, IClassifier, IPolicyEnforcement, IAuditSink, IPublishMetadata, IModelRouter, IRetrievalPlanner+orchestrator, IIdentityResolution, IEventBus, IExpertiseFingerprint, ICollaborationGraph, plus the cross-kernel descriptor/AuditEvent/OutboxRecord contracts. Every one of the four feature modules depends on the kernel; the kernel depends on nothing. The 'two coherence domains' (policy vs serving) split is asserted but the cross-kernel contract list (descriptor + AuditEvent + OutboxRecord) shows serving and policy are tightly coupled at runtime (serving emits audit that policy consumes; serving produces outbox that policy's egress PEP validates). There is no kernel-wide version, no statement of expected kernel change frequency, and no mechanism to add a kernel capability without every module potentially needing a contract-version bump.
  - *Why:* Modularity is measured by blast radius. When the single component everything depends on grows monotonically and is never decomposed or version-bounded, every kernel change is a potential fan-out to all four modules and both substrate profiles. This is the classic 'shared kernel rots into the bottleneck' failure the plan claims to avoid for IExpertiseGraph but reintroduces at a larger scale for the kernel itself. The decision log explicitly says it split IExpertiseGraph because 'a contract one module writes and all four read is the classic bottleneck where v1 rotted' — yet the kernel is precisely that pattern with eleven contracts instead of one.
  - *Fix:* Bound the kernel explicitly: (1) define what is NOT allowed in the kernel (no feature logic, no module-specific schemas), (2) version the policy kernel and serving kernel independently with stated compatibility windows and an expected-change-frequency budget per contract (e.g., TierLattice is frozen-after-Phase-1, IModelRouter expected to churn), (3) make IIdentityResolution and the read-model projections candidates for extraction into their own versioned services rather than kernel-resident, since they are not security-critical and their churn shouldn't force kernel releases. State the rule: a contract whose change frequency differs by an order of magnitude from the rest of its kernel must be evicted from that kernel.
- **[HIGH]** 'Additive-only forever' is the declared evolution rule for PublishableProjection, IExpertiseFingerprint, ICollaborationGraph, AuditEvent, OutboxRecord, and ClassificationCapabilityDescriptor — but additive-only with no exercised removal path is an unmaintainability guarantee, not a maintainability feature. The plan gestures at a 'per-field consumer registry' and 'a field is retired when no pillar reads it' and 'removal-time dependency check' (Section 5.7), but the central-index field-intersection mechanism means a field added in node version N must be supported by the index essentially forever to keep older nodes working, and there is no stated process for ever shrinking the projection. Over years of independently-upgrading nodes, the publishable surface and the read-models monotonically grow.
  - *Why:* The PublishableProjection is the security-critical federation seam — every field on it is an allowlisted leak surface that must carry a classification-review record. An ever-growing, never-shrinking allowlist means the audit surface and the field-intersection logic across deployed node versions grows without bound, the cross-version contract tests (node@N vs index@M) multiply combinatorially, and the 'classification review per field' debt accumulates. A seam that can only grow is a seam whose blast radius and review cost grow with age — the definition of architectural rot at the highest-stakes boundary.
  - *Fix:* Specify a real deprecation lifecycle, not just additive growth: per-field introduced-in-version AND deprecated-in-version AND removed-in-version, with a defined minimum-node-version floor (the index drops support for projection fields older than the oldest supported node version, and forces stragglers to upgrade on a stated cadence). Make the consumer-usage registry authoritative: a field with zero consumers for K releases is auto-flagged for removal with a migration. Cap the cross-version test matrix by maintaining at most M supported projection versions, not all historical versions.
- **[HIGH]** The monorepo-vs-services boundary is internally contradictory and produces a distributed monolith. Section 5.2/5.7 say modules are 'deployed as independent service/Helm sub-chart' and never import each other. But Section 5.11/6.3 say all four modules share ONE consolidated Postgres in the MVP, isolated only by per-module schemas + DB-role grants, and the split into separate stores is deferred behind a trigger (2M docs / 5M nodes / 60% RAM). Independently-deployed services sharing a single physical database with cross-schema grants is the textbook distributed monolith: you pay the operational cost of services (network calls, partial-failure, deploy coordination) while retaining the coupling cost of a shared store (shared failure domain, shared connection pool, shared backup/restore, coordinated migrations, contention — which the plan itself flags in 6.3). The DB-role-grant isolation is real for read-access but does nothing for the shared-failure-domain and shared-resource-contention coupling.
  - *Why:* This undermines the central modularity claim ('add/remove a feature with minimal blast radius'). A module deployed as a service but backed by the shared Postgres cannot be independently scaled, independently failed-over, or independently migrated without coordinating with every other module's schema in the same instance. The split trigger means the architecture's coupling profile silently changes under load precisely when the smallest, cheapest cells have the least headroom — and the plan admits contention bites there. You get neither clean service isolation nor clean monolith simplicity.
  - *Fix:* Pick the honest posture for the MVP: a modular monolith (single deployable, in-process module boundaries enforced by import-linter + DB schemas) is the correct choice for a 3-engineer team and one edition — say so, and drop the 'deployed as independent service' language until a module actually needs independent scaling. Reserve service extraction as a designed seam (the plan already has the design-vs-build discipline elsewhere). If modules truly must be separate services from day one, then they cannot share a Postgres instance — give each module its own database and make cross-module data flow exclusively via the event bus/read-models, which is what the plan already claims is the only allowed path.
- **[HIGH]** The router/transport 'disagreement = hard fail' contract proves the router is NOT actually swappable in the way claimed — it makes correctness depend on a second component (ITransportPolicy) independently re-deriving the same tier-routing logic. The plan frames the hard-fail as the safety net that lets the router be replaceable, but a buggy or malicious replacement router that selects cloud for confidential will simply hard-fail every confidential inference, taking the product down rather than leaking. That is safe but it means 'swap the router' is not a low-blast-radius operation: any router that disagrees with transport on tier semantics is a hard outage, so the router and transport are semantically coupled through the tier lattice they must both interpret identically.
  - *Why:* The locked requirement is a provider-agnostic, swappable router. The plan delivers a router that is swappable for provider-selection-among-cloud-options but is rigidly bound to ITransportPolicy on tier-to-locality mapping. If the routing-precedence invariant ('confidential ALWAYS local') ever needs to change, it must change in lockstep in two places (router selection logic AND transport validation), which is exactly the cross-cutting fan-out the plan elsewhere tries to eliminate. A reviewer should not accept 'two components independently implement the same rule' as decoupling — it is duplicated logic that must be kept in sync, the inverse of a single source of truth.
  - *Fix:* Make the tier-to-locality mapping a single owned policy in the TierLattice/routing-policy table that BOTH the router consults and the transport enforces — not two independent re-derivations. The router asks the policy 'what localities are legal for this tier?' and selects within that set; the transport enforces the same policy as a final gate. Then the router truly only owns provider-preference within an already-constrained legal set, the disagreement case becomes a router that violated the policy it was given (a clear bug class), and changing the precedence invariant is a single-table edit covered by the exhaustiveness mechanism rather than a two-site synchronized change.
- **[MEDIUM]** The retrieval planner-graph (IRetrievalPlanner emits a possibly-cyclic plan over micro-interfaces, orchestrator executes with loop caps + per-stage tier-clamp) is a strong shape but the orchestrator becomes an undocumented coupling point. Tier-clamping and loop caps are 'pinned at the orchestrator,' meaning the orchestrator must understand the tier semantics of every micro-interface (IRetriever, IFuser, IReranker, ICorrectiveLoop, IGraphAugment) and every planner strategy (Adaptive RAG, CRAG back-edges, multi-agent). Adding a new stage type or a new planner strategy that introduces a new control-flow shape (e.g., a fan-out/scatter-gather, or a stage that itself calls the model) requires orchestrator changes — so the 'swap a planner strategy without touching micro-interfaces' claim holds only for control flows the orchestrator already anticipates.
  - *Why:* The plan sells the planner-graph as the thing that makes retrieval evolution low-blast-radius (CRAG = a back-edge, multi-agent = another planner). But security enforcement (tier-clamp per stage) living in the orchestrator means the orchestrator is a kernel-grade security component that every new retrieval shape must integrate with correctly. A multi-agent planner where an agent step itself triggers retrieval-of-a-different-tier is a control-flow the orchestrator must explicitly support to clamp correctly — and the agent-action egress section (8.4) confirms agent actions are a distinct enforcement path. The decoupling is real for the data-flow stages but leaky for the control-flow + security dimension.
  - *Fix:* Specify the orchestrator's contract explicitly: what control-flow primitives it supports (sequence, bounded back-edge, bounded fan-out) and the invariant that every stage executes under a tier-pinned capability the orchestrator stamps. Make the tier-clamp a property the micro-interface declares and the orchestrator enforces, rather than orchestrator-internal knowledge of each stage, so a new stage type carries its own clamp contract. State which planner control-flow shapes are in-scope vs require an orchestrator change, so 'add a planner strategy' has an honest blast-radius.
- **[MEDIUM]** Two supported substrate profiles (full managed stack with SpiceDB-cluster + Kafka vs single-appliance with library-authz + outbox-polling) means the authz and event-substrate implementations are swapped, not just configured — and the plan rests the equivalence guarantee entirely on 'the same abstract conformance suite runs against both.' But library-RBAC-vs-SpiceDB and outbox-polling-vs-Kafka-CDC have materially different consistency, ordering, and failure semantics (e.g., ZedToken monotonicity and cell-local replication exist only in the SpiceDB profile; the appliance has no equivalent). A conformance suite can assert behavioral equivalence only for behaviors it enumerates; the partition/ordering/freshness edge cases that the plan spends most of Section 4.4 on are exactly the behaviors hardest to encode as portable conformance tests.
  - *Why:* The sovereign/appliance profile is the operator-zero-trust tier sold to the highest-stakes (export-class) buyers, where a divergence between the appliance's authz/revocation behavior and the managed stack's is a compliance/security failure, not a feature gap. 'Two stacks behind one contract, validated by a shared suite' is a maintainability liability if the suite under-specifies the security-critical concurrency behaviors — the two implementations will drift exactly where it matters most and the team will not know.
  - *Fix:* Enumerate the security-critical behavioral invariants that BOTH profiles must satisfy (fail-closed-on-stale-watermark, no-resurrection-after-tombstone, deny-on-PIP-unavailable, monotonic-read-after-grant) as explicit conformance tests with adversarial partition/skew injection, and run them against both substrates in CI — not just the happy-path authz checks. Where the appliance genuinely cannot match a managed-stack behavior (e.g., no cross-region replication), state the reduced guarantee explicitly per profile rather than implying parity.
- **[MEDIUM]** The local docker-compose federation harness (two cells + stub control plane + mock/quantized inference) is the right investment, but using a STUB control plane and MOCK inference for the seam-correctness tests means the harness validates the seam against a model of the control plane, not the real one. The revocation-authority freshness gate, per-tenant-signed token validation, and confused-deputy defense are only as correct as the stub's fidelity — and the plan reserves the real multi-region SpiceDB validation for a 'one-time spike,' not a standing gate. A stub that drifts from the real control plane's semantics (token format, watermark feed timing, replication ordering) gives green CI while the real seam is broken.
  - *Why:* The plan's own framing is that the federation seam is the existential risk and that 'seam correctness must be locally testable or it rots.' If the local harness tests against a stub whose behavior is maintained separately from the real control plane, the harness can rot independently of the production seam — passing CI while the production token/watermark/replication contract has diverged. This is the testability equivalent of the additive-only problem: a test double that is never reconciled with the real implementation is a maintenance liability that produces false confidence.
  - *Fix:* Make the stub control plane a thin adapter over the SAME contract artifacts (token-issuance library, watermark-feed protocol, projection-version logic) the real control plane uses — share the implementation of the security-critical primitives, stub only the deployment topology (single-region, in-memory). Add a periodic (per-release, not one-time) contract test that runs the seam tests against an ephemeral REAL control plane to detect stub drift, and gate releases on it rather than treating multi-region validation as a one-time spike.
- **[MEDIUM]** mod-discovery's relationship to the read-models is described inconsistently in a way that hides real coupling. Section 3.1 says mod-discovery 'emits RAW events; the kernel projects' and 'does NOT own the contract' — good. But that means the kernel's read-model projection logic must understand the SHAPE of mod-discovery's raw events to build IExpertiseFingerprint and ICollaborationGraph. So the coupling didn't disappear; it moved into a kernel-resident projection that is implicitly coupled to mod-discovery's event schema. If mod-discovery changes what it emits, the kernel projection breaks, and all four consumers downstream are affected. The plan calls this decoupling but it is an event-schema dependency from kernel→module-events, which is the kernel depending on a module's output format — the dependency arrow the architecture claims never to allow.
  - *Why:* The whole point of the IExpertiseGraph split was to remove the single-writer/all-readers bottleneck. But routing the write through 'raw events the kernel projects' just relocates the writer-side contract into the kernel without naming it as a versioned contract. The raw-event schema mod-discovery emits is now an unversioned, unmanaged coupling surface between a module and the kernel — exactly the kind of implicit contract that rots because nobody owns its evolution.
  - *Fix:* Name the raw-event schema as a first-class versioned contract in shared/contracts/ (e.g., DiscoveryFactEvent) with the same additive-only + consumer-registry discipline as the read-models. Be explicit that the kernel's projection logic is a consumer of that contract, so a mod-discovery event change goes through the same contract-versioning gate. Otherwise the 'kernel owns the projection' framing is just hiding the producer-side contract instead of managing it.
- **[LOW]** The directory layout (Section 5.12) bans 'concrete-store/SDK imports outside kernel adapters' and 'LlamaIndex/Qdrant/SpiceDB SDK types may not appear in shared/contracts/' via import-linter — a good rule — but import-linter only checks Python import statements. It does not catch leakage of SDK-shaped data structures (e.g., a contract field typed as dict that happens to carry a SpiceDB ZedToken string, or a LlamaIndex node serialized into an event payload). The plan treats import-linter as if it enforces semantic decoupling when it only enforces syntactic import boundaries.
  - *Why:* A contract that is syntactically free of SDK imports but semantically carries SDK-shaped payloads (stringly-typed tokens, opaque blobs that only one SDK can parse) is just as coupled as one that imports the SDK — the next engineer who swaps Qdrant for pgvector or SpiceDB for OpenFGA discovers the leak at runtime, not at lint time. This weakens the 'swappable retrieval/authz' claim that import-linter is cited to defend.
  - *Fix:* Augment import-linter with a contract-schema review rule: fields in shared/contracts/ must be typed with domain types, not opaque strings/dicts that smuggle SDK semantics, and any 'opaque token' field must document which abstraction owns its format and which alternative implementations can produce it. Add a swap-test in CI (e.g., run the authz conformance suite against both SpiceDB and OpenFGA, and retrieval against both pgvector and Qdrant) to prove the contracts are genuinely implementation-agnostic rather than merely import-clean.

### Lens: Product, GTM & business viability

*Verdict:* This plan has internalized prior GTM critique unusually well: the dyad is correctly demoted from beachhead to expansion proof, PLG is honestly reclassified as a loss-leader, pricing is value-validated before build, the export-lab "fallback" is correctly recognized as a multi-quarter CMMC vertical, and a runway/revenue-by-quarter model exists. Those are real fixes. But the plan still rests on a beachhead whose core value proposition is unproven AT N=1, mis-sequences its single hardest validation gate (it builds an entire product before testing whether anyone will pay the differentiated price), and quietly converts itself from a "federated cross-institution platform" (the locked product) into "a single-tenant confidential RAG appliance for one lab" for the first 12-18 months — which is a genuinely different, more crowded, and less defensible business that the competitive analysis never actually maps. The four-buyer "one product" claim survives architecturally but the GTM motions are not actually sequenced credibly against a 3-engineer/1-GTM team. Pricing realism, the procurement veto graph, and the race-window thesis are handled with above-average honesty but contain specific unproven leaps. Below, ranked by severity.

- **[CRITICAL]** The Phase-0 MVP is no longer the locked product — it is a single-tenant confidential RAG tool for ONE lab, and the entire differentiated value (the federation seam) is deferred to Phase 1, yet the competitive analysis (1.2) and white-space claim are built ENTIRELY on confidential federation. At N=1 there is no federation, no cross-institution sharing, no exchange layer. So the Phase-0 product the first dollar pays for sits squarely in a market the plan itself says is commoditized/free: 'AI-grounded Q&A + semantic search + expert discovery over the center's OWN corpus.' That is exactly what NotebookLM Enterprise, Glean, ChatGPT Enterprise w/ connectors, Elicit/Scholar workspaces, and every vertical RAG vendor already sell — most with bigger teams and SOC2 already done. The plan's stated moat ('Nobody federates confidential cross-institution research data') does not apply to the thing being sold first.
  - *Why:* First revenue is being earned on a product whose differentiation the plan never establishes. The 'differentiated security guarantee at N=1' (local-only inference + egress block + per-tenant keys) is not unique — on-prem/VPC-isolated RAG with no-data-leaves-your-boundary is table stakes for every enterprise RAG vendor selling to regulated buyers. A funded R1 center evaluating a 3-person startup's single-cell RAG tool against incumbents with the same security posture and a real support org has no reason to choose the startup. The plan validates buyer DESIRE (Week-1 spike) but never validates that the Phase-0 product WINS against incumbents on its own merits without the federation moat.
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit competitive teardown of the Phase-0 single-cell product specifically — name NotebookLM Enterprise, Glean, ChatGPT Enterprise, and any research-vertical RAG incumbent, and state in one sentence why a funded center picks you at N=1 BEFORE the network exists. If the honest answer is 'research-native graph + SPECTER2 expertise fingerprints + scholarly-corpus ingestion,' say that explicitly and make it the Phase-0 wedge thesis. If the honest answer is 'they don't, the federation roadmap is the reason they buy early,' then the dyad cannot be truly decoupled and the runway model must absorb a longer pre-revenue period. Do not let the Phase-0 product inherit the federation moat by association.
- **[CRITICAL]** The single most expensive validation — value-based WTP at or above the COGS floor — is scheduled as a pre-build interview (2.6, 19.3), but the BYO-compute escape hatch makes the whole pricing thesis circular and untested. The plan says: validate WTP; if WTP < COGS floor, make BYO-compute the default. But BYO-compute shifts GPU COGS to the buyer, which means the buyer now must procure, provision, and operate a GPU node for a 3-person vendor's software — a far heavier IT lift that triggers MORE procurement friction (the very veto graph in 2.8), not less. The plan treats BYO-compute as a clean economic escape when it is actually an adoption-friction escalation. And the $2.5-8k/mo ASP band is asserted, not derived from any comparable; the only named comparable (Atom at ~$179/mo) is 14-45x cheaper.
  - *Why:* A 14-45x price gap to the nearest named comparable in the same buyer's budget, justified only by 'confidentiality + a security line-item exists,' is the kind of assumption that sinks the unit economics if wrong. If WTP lands at, say, $1-2k/mo (closer to enterprise SaaS seat-bundles than to a clean-room contract), the BYO-compute fallback doesn't save the model — it makes the product harder to sell AND shifts you into competing with free/cheap RAG on software-only pricing. The plan never models the BYO-compute ASP or its adoption-friction cost.
  - *Fix:* Before build, get a written price indication (not just 'desire') from at least 2 of the 3 candidate centers anchored to a concrete budget line they would draw from (clean-room/secure-collaboration budget vs research-software budget — these are 10x apart). Model the BYO-compute scenario explicitly: software-only ASP, the buyer-side ops burden, and which veto offices that ops burden re-triggers. State the floor ASP below which the business is not viable and treat the Week-1 spike as a kill-gate, not a calibration.
- **[HIGH]** The 'one product serves all four buyers' claim is satisfied architecturally (editions + packaging) but the GTM is NOT credibly sequenced. The plan admits a 'one-GTM-hire team runs one motion at a time' (2.2), then assigns: Phase-0 = single-center direct BD, Phase-1 = consortium multi-party BD, Phase-2 = top-down institutional field sales + PLG loss-leader, Phase-3 = executive selling + grant monetization. That is FOUR distinct enterprise motions, each requiring different collateral, sales cycles, champions, and procurement navigation, run sequentially by one GTM person across ~3 years. Consortium BD (multi-party, multi-legal-entity) and top-down institutional field sales are different jobs requiring different hires; the plan defers the hire but not the motion.
  - *Why:* The locked requirement is one PRODUCT for four buyers, which the plan meets. But commercial viability depends on actually executing the motions, and a single non-specialist GTM hire cannot run consortium multi-party BD (the hardest enterprise motion that exists — you're herding 2+ universities' legal/IT/research offices simultaneously) in Phase 1 while also having closed a center in Phase 0 and prepping institutional sales for Phase 2. The roadmap implicitly assumes serial single-threaded GTM execution with no specialist hires until Phase 2, which is not how multi-party institutional deals actually close.
  - *Fix:* State the GTM hiring plan against the motion sequence: which motion needs which specialist hire, and when. If consortium BD is Phase-1, a consortium/alliance BD specialist (or a founder with existing consortium relationships) must be in place BEFORE Phase 1, not deferred to Phase 2. Tie the runway model (16.2) to GTM headcount, not just engineering. Acknowledge that 'one motion at a time' with one hire likely stretches each phase's GTM ramp by quarters beyond the engineering timeline.
- **[HIGH]** The race-window thesis (1.5, Risk #17) — 'lock ≥2 consortia under multi-year contracts before a funded incumbent ships confidential federation' — is the entire defensibility argument, and the timeline math defeats it. By the plan's own roadmap, the first dyad contract is Phase-1 (Q4-Q5), the first institutional SaaS is month 15-18, and '≥2 consortia under multi-year contracts' is a PHASE-3 milestone (Section 18). Phase 3 is realistically year 2.5-3+. The plan asks a 3-person team to win the race by year 3 against an incumbent (Atom, 50+ logos, funded) who, per the plan's own admission, 'can add per-tenant isolation as an engineering project faster than we can acquire their distribution.' The defensibility milestone lands AFTER the window the plan says is the threat.
  - *Why:* If the moat is a race and the race is won at Phase 3 (~year 3) while a funded incumbent with distribution can ship isolation in an engineering quarter or two, the plan's own logic says you lose the race. The compounding assets named (sharing-grant graph, trust relationships, certs) only accumulate AFTER you have multiple federated nodes — which is Phase 2-3. There is a multi-year window where you have the differentiation built but not the contractual lock, and that is precisely when an incumbent's distribution beats your moat.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) compress the path to multi-year consortium lock dramatically (sign LOIs/multi-year framework deals with consortia in Phase 0-1 BEFORE the full federation product ships, using the roadmap as the lock mechanism), or (b) honestly down-grade the defensibility claim from 'we win the race' to 'we occupy a niche the incumbent won't prioritize' and identify which sub-segment (export-adjacent? specific consortia like CTSA?) is too small/regulated for the incumbent to chase. The current framing claims a race you're sequenced to lose.
- **[HIGH]** The beachhead buyer is internally contradictory on the dimension that justifies the price. The Phase-0 buyer is defined as a 'single funded R1 center / security-sensitive lab (NOT export-controlled)' that has 'a security line-item budget.' But the plan also says (3.3, 16.1) the differentiator is confidential-tier local-only inference. A center that is genuinely security-sensitive ENOUGH to pay $2.5-8k/mo for no-data-leaves-the-boundary is very often export-adjacent, defense-funded, or handling regulated data (the exact buyers the plan pushes to the deferred Phase-2+ sovereign/CMMC vertical). Strip out export-controlled, defense, and CMMC-requiring buyers, and the remaining set — a non-regulated R1 center that nonetheless pays a premium for in-boundary inference on its OWN internal corpus — is a much narrower and less-proven segment than the plan implies.
  - *Why:* The plan resolves the export-lab over-claim correctly (export buyers need sovereign/CMMC, deferred). But in doing so it carves out the buyers with the strongest, most-budgeted reason to pay for confidentiality, and leaves a Phase-0 segment whose willingness to pay a premium for confidentiality-on-internal-data is asserted, not evidenced. A non-export R1 center can often use the university's existing NotebookLM Enterprise / Azure OpenAI VPC tenancy for 'data stays in our boundary' at far lower marginal cost.
  - *Fix:* Name the specific non-export segment that has both the budget AND a confidentiality requirement that incumbent VPC-tenancy RAG does NOT satisfy. Candidates: industry-sponsored research labs under corporate NDA/IP-confidentiality (not export, but contractually confidential), translational/clinical centers with PHI under BAA, or labs with pre-publication IP-sensitivity. Pick one, validate it specifically in Week 1, and make it the named beachhead — 'security-sensitive R1 center' is too vague to be a beachhead.
- **[HIGH]** The procurement/adoption-friction analysis (2.8, Risk #19) honestly maps a 4-6 office veto graph but then under-budgets its TIME impact against the runway model. The plan ships pre-empting collateral (HECVAT pre-fill, OGC DPA package, IRB data-flow) — good — but the revenue-by-quarter model (16.2) shows 'first pilot live' in Q2 (month ~6). A funded R1 center's security review + OGC sign-off + (if any human-subjects or licensed-corpus data is touched) IRB/library review for a NEW SaaS vendor with no SOC2 yet routinely takes 3-9 months on its own. The plan's own SOC2 timing (Type I at Phase-0→1 boundary, Type II at month 15-18) means the Phase-0 pilot is being sold to a security-conscious buyer BEFORE either SOC2 exists.
  - *Why:* Selling a confidential-data product to a security-line-item buyer with NO SOC2 (not even Type I) is the single biggest procurement blocker for exactly this buyer type — CISOs gate on it. The plan acknowledges 'design partners start on a questionnaire + roadmap,' but a questionnaire-and-roadmap rarely clears a security review for production confidential data at an R1. The Q2 first-pilot date is likely optimistic by 1-3 quarters, which compounds the runway problem (16.2 plans 24-30 months; a 2-quarter slip on first revenue + the SOC2-gated recurring revenue is material).
  - *Fix:* Reconcile the SOC2 timeline with the buyer's security gate explicitly. Either (a) the Phase-0 pilot is structured as a NON-production / de-identified / sandbox pilot that doesn't trip the full security review (state this — it changes what 'confidential tier' means in the pilot), or (b) pull SOC2 Type I fully into Phase 0 and re-cost it against the 3-engineer team (the plan currently defers it to the Phase-0→1 boundary). Stress-test the runway model with first-pilot slipping to Q3-Q4 and first-recurring to month 18-24.
- **[MEDIUM]** Network-effect bootstrap is handled honestly ('captures collaborations that already exist, doesn't manufacture them; rides existing consortia') but the cold-start economics are still unfavorable in a way the plan doesn't quantify. The standalone-useful intra-institution product is the node-acquisition engine (2.7) — but per Finding #1 that product competes with free/cheap incumbents at N=1. So the bootstrap depends on the standalone product being good enough to acquire nodes 1-10 on its own merits, which the plan asserts but Finding #1 disputes. If standalone acquisition is weak, the network never reaches the threshold where cross-node value compounds, and the platform degrades to a collection of disconnected single-tenant RAG installs.
  - *Why:* The federation value (the actual product) only turns on at N≥2 within the SAME consortium. So you don't just need 10 nodes — you need 2+ nodes that are ALREADY collaborating AND both bought the standalone product AND cleared the cross-vendor processor review. That conjunction is much rarer than '10 customers.' The plan's TAM honesty ('bounded by existing consortium membership') actually implies the serviceable beachhead for the FEDERATED product is tiny early on.
  - *Fix:* Quantify the realistic federated-value-bearing TAM: how many consortia have ≥2 research-active members who (a) already collaborate on confidential work, (b) would both adopt, and (c) whose master agreement permits a new processor (the 2.3 test)? If that number is in the low tens, say so — it reframes this as a high-touch, low-volume, high-ASP enterprise business (which changes the GTM and pricing), not a network-effect platform play. The 'platform / network effect' framing may be overstated for the realistic early TAM.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'integrate, don't displace' strategy (2.9) correctly flags competitor-controlled APIs (Pure/Elsevier, Symplectic/Clarivate) as strategic risk and names the data-layer incumbents as 'the most likely well-resourced entrant.' But the plan's response — 'reduce critical-path dependence; have an institution-provided-export fallback' — understates the threat. Pure/Symplectic/Academic Analytics ALREADY own the institutional research-metadata relationship, the data, AND the procurement relationship at most R1s. If Elsevier/Clarivate decides to add AI Q&A + a federation feature to Pure (they have the data, the install base, and the budget), they enter the white space with distribution the startup cannot match — and the startup's 'confidential federation' moat is an engineering project for them too.
  - *Why:* This is the same race risk as Atom (#17) but against a far better-resourced and better-distributed incumbent, and the plan treats it as a one-line footnote rather than a primary competitive threat. The defensibility thesis (race window) is weakest precisely against the incumbent best positioned to win.
