  - *Fix:* Elevate the data-layer-incumbent entry to a named primary competitive risk alongside Atom (#17), and state the specific reason they WON'T or CAN'T do it quickly (e.g., their architecture centralizes data — a confidential-federation pivot is anathema to their centralized-SaaS model and their data-monetization business; cite this as a structural disincentive, if true). If you can't articulate why the incumbent won't follow, the moat thesis has a hole at its most dangerous point.
- **[MEDIUM]** Pricing realism: the 'first 3-5 deals = single flat per-node fee, everything included' decision (2.6) is sound for cycle-time, but it collides with the BYO-compute escape hatch and the per-node COGS variance. A flat per-node fee with 'everything included' over a COGS that ranges $1.5-3k/mo (16.1) — and doubles if the buyer wants 99.9% warm-standby — means either the flat fee is set high enough to cover the worst case (pricing out the budget-constrained early buyers you need for proof) or it's set at the median and warm-standby/large-corpus buyers are sold at a loss. The plan defers multi-axis pricing to 'when a sales engineer exists,' but the COGS variance is a Phase-0 problem.
  - *Why:* Early reference customers are the most price-sensitive and the most likely to negotiate; a flat 'everything included' fee that must cover unpredictable GPU/corpus/SLA variance either kills the deal on price or kills the margin on delivery. Either outcome damages the proof points you need.
  - *Fix:* Even for the first 3-5 deals, gate the flat fee on explicit corpus-size and SLA tier (99.5% vs 99.9%) so the flat fee is flat WITHIN a tier, not across wildly different COGS. This is not multi-axis pricing — it's two named SKUs — and it protects the margin without needing a sales engineer.
- **[LOW]** The product-name change to 'MeridianExchange' (Risk GTM-low-branding) is fine, but 'Exchange' in the name foregrounds the federation/cross-institution capability that, per the build sequence, does not exist for the first 12-18 months. The Phase-0 buyer is buying a single-tenant intra-institution tool branded as an inter-institution exchange.
  - *Why:* Minor, but a name that promises a capability the product doesn't yet have can create expectation mismatch in early sales conversations and invites the 'so where's the exchange?' objection precisely when you're selling N=1 value.
  - *Fix:* Either keep the name but lead Phase-0 messaging on the intra-institution value (and position 'Exchange' as the roadmap), or use a neutral product name for the Phase-0 edition. Trivial to fix; flag it so it's a deliberate choice.

### Lens: Cost, ops & founder feasibility

*Verdict:* This is the most cost-honest version of a plan like this I've reviewed: it has a line-item COGS stack, a quarter-by-quarter burn/runway model, a value-validated pricing gate, a BYO-compute escape hatch for inverted unit economics, and it correctly reclassified the export-lab "fallback" as a capital-intensive Phase-2+ vertical. The single-cell Phase-0 re-cut genuinely halves the build and is the right call. That said, the plan systematically under-states the Phase-0 build surface relative to its own 3-engineer claim; the "designed-not-built" line is blurry in exactly the places that cost the most engineering (per-module DB-role isolation, TierLattice exhaustiveness machinery, the local 2-cell harness, the dual classifier, AGE benchmark are all placed in Phase 0); the GPU/managed-index COGS floor is still optimistic against real billing-floor and load behavior; and the runway model omits the largest startup cost line entirely (loaded engineering salaries). The wedge buyer ("a single funded R1 center with a security line-item that will pay $2.5-8k/mo for an internal RAG tool over its own corpus before any federation exists") is asserted, not evidenced, and the entire sequence is gated on a Week-1 discovery spike finding one which is a real risk the plan acknowledges but does not de-risk. The architecture is buildable in principle; the schedule and team-size claims are where this would hurt a small team.

- **[CRITICAL]** Phase-0 'single-cell, 3-engineer-buildable' scope is materially larger than the plan admits. The Section 18 Phase-0 build list includes, simultaneously: a dual independent classifier with agreement logic, a pure router + independent ITransportPolicy enforcement layer + router-disagreement hard-fail, IPolicyEnforcement/PEP-PDP abstraction over library RBAC, hash-chained IAuditSink, deterministic identity resolution (anchors+blocking+graph-feature classifier), the IRetrievalPlanner+orchestrator+micro-interface retrieval seam, a functional confidential tier (vLLM + per-tenant Vault/KMS keys + network egress block), a consolidated Postgres with pgvector+AGE+tsvector+audit+outbox AND per-module schemas with DB-role grants, a Dagster pipeline, the owned TierLattice module + typed exhaustiveness check + import-linter + DB-permission CI gates, a scaffolded local docker-compose 2-cell harness, an AGE/PPR synthetic benchmark, RAGAS-in-CI with a GPU-staging judge, and the versioned PublishableProjection contract types. Much of this is platform/abstraction work (the kernel, the contract machinery, the CI gates, the modular DB isolation) that delivers ZERO customer-visible value but consumes the bulk of senior-engineer time. A pre-revenue 3-person team building all of this before first dollar is the classic 'we built the platform before we found out if anyone wanted the product' failure.
  - *Why:* This is the founder-feasibility crux. If Phase-0 realistically takes 9-12 months for 3 engineers rather than the ~2 quarters the burn model implies, the pre-recurring-revenue valley (already 12-18mo to SaaS) blows past the stated 24-30mo runway. The plan's own runway math depends on Phase-0 shipping a paid pilot by Q2.
  - *Fix:* Ruthlessly separate 'walking-skeleton MVP that proves the wedge' from 'platform investment.' For Phase-0, defer the kernel/contract/exhaustiveness/DB-role/local-harness machinery to Phase-1 and ship the single-cell confidential RAG product as a deliberately-monolithic, less-modular app (the modularity tax is only justified once a second module/edition/cell exists). Re-derive the Phase-0 timeline bottom-up from the actual build list and reconcile it against the Q2-first-pilot assumption in Section 16.2.
- **[CRITICAL]** The burn/runway model (Section 16.2) omits the single largest cost line for a startup: loaded engineering + GTM salaries. It models infra COGS ($1.5-3k/cell), SOC2 (~$40-70k/yr), and staging, but '5 FTE' loaded cost (roughly $150-250k/yr each fully loaded = ~$0.9-1.25M/yr) never appears in the runway arithmetic. The '24-30 months runway' assertion is therefore unanchored to a monthly burn number.
  - *Why:* Runway = cash / monthly burn, and burn is ~90% salaries at this stage, not infra. A runway claim that excludes payroll is not a runway model. This directly affects how much seed must be raised and whether the 12-18mo-to-recurring valley is survivable, which is the exact question the section purports to answer.
  - *Fix:* Add a real monthly-burn line: 5 FTE loaded + fractional GRC/counsel + infra + tooling + audit, summed to a monthly number, divided into the seed to produce the runway figure. Then show first-dollar (Q2 pilot) and first-material-recurring (mo 15-18) against that burn so the cash-out date is explicit. Without this, the seed-size ask is unjustified.
- **[HIGH]** The beachhead buyer is asserted, not evidenced, and the ENTIRE sequence is gated on a Week-1 spike finding it. The wedge is 'a single funded R1 center / security-sensitive lab with a security line-item budget that will pay $2.5-8k/mo for an internal AI-grounded Q&A/search tool over its OWN corpus, with no federation and no second institution.' That is a single-university RAG tool with a confidential tier, i.e., very close to what TigerResearchBuddy already is, minus polish. The differentiator at N=1 is 'the data never leaves the boundary,' but a lab can already run Ollama/vLLM + a vector DB on its own hardware for near-zero marginal cost. WTP of $30k-96k/yr for that, from a single lab, before any cross-institution value exists, is the load-bearing assumption of the whole plan and has no evidence behind it.
  - *Why:* If the N=1 confidential-RAG value isn't worth $2.5-8k/mo to a single center (because they can self-host the open-source stack, or because internal-only RAG isn't a budgeted pain), there is no Phase-0 revenue, and the dyad (Phase-1) is even harder. The plan's federation moat only matters at N>=2; at N=1 you are selling a commodity the buyer can assemble from free parts.
  - *Fix:* Make the Week-1 spike a hard go/no-go gate with a falsifiable threshold (e.g., 'at least 2 of 3 named centers give a written price indication >= $30k/yr for internal-only deployment'), and pre-commit to pivoting the wedge if it fails. Sharpen the N=1 value prop beyond 'data stays in-boundary' (which self-hosting also gives), e.g., the managed/compliance/audit burden you remove, or the library-corpus connector, and validate THAT is the willingness-to-pay driver, not federation.
- **[HIGH]** The 'designed-only vs built-now' split is blurry precisely where it costs the most, and several heavy items are placed in Phase-0. Section 2.5 says 'through Phase-1 the only fully-built topology is the single-cell managed Institution edition,' and PLG scheduler / sovereign image / BYO-compute are 'design-reserved seams with zero implementation.' But Phase-0 (Section 18) still builds the owned TierLattice with compiler-enforced exhaustiveness across all consumers, per-module DB-schema isolation with role grants and a migration check, the local 2-cell docker-compose harness (scaffolded), AGE PPR benchmarking, and the versioned PublishableProjection contract, all of which are modular-platform investments whose payoff only arrives at Phase-1+. The 'interfaces present, zero implementation' principle is not applied to these.
  - *Why:* Designing five isolation postures and a contract/exhaustiveness regime up front is exactly the over-engineering the plan elsewhere warns against. A team selling ONE edition for 18 months pays a large abstraction tax for variation points it won't exercise until Phase 2+. This is the difference between a Phase-0 that ships in 4-5 months and one that ships in 10.
  - *Fix:* Apply the 'interface present, impl deferred' rule consistently: in Phase-0 the TierLattice can be a simple enum + MAX function (no compiler-enforced cross-consumer exhaustiveness machinery until a second tier-consumer exists), DB isolation can be import-linter-only (DB-role grants when a second module ships), and the 2-cell harness can be deferred to the start of Phase-1 (you have no second cell to test until then). Move the AGE/PPR benchmark to the Phase-0/1 boundary unless the graph is actually on the Phase-0 critical path.
- **[HIGH]** Per-cell COGS floor is still optimistic in two ways that compress margin. (1) The GPU line ($735-1454/mo) is on-demand list for an A10G/L4 24GB instance running a 7-8B model; under real concurrent load with TEE (required early for the operator-zero-trust/export-class buyers the plan now courts in Phase-1) and any headroom you move to larger/more instances, and TEE-capable GPU instances carry a premium. (2) The central index is assigned to a managed service (OpenSearch Serverless / managed vector) to keep ops off 3 engineers, but managed serverless search has a non-trivial IDLE billing FLOOR (OpenSearch Serverless bills minimum OCUs, ~several hundred $/mo even at zero traffic), and that floor exists from the FIRST consortium node, not at scale, yet it's described as 'amortized via the exchange add-on' which only works once you have paying consortium customers.
  - *Why:* A $1.5-3k/mo floor that is really $3-5k/mo once you add TEE-capable compute, warm vLLM, and the managed-index idle floor materially changes the breakeven customer count and the BYO-compute decision. The plan's whole unit-economics argument rests on this floor.
  - *Fix:* Re-cost the GPU line specifically for TEE-capable instances (which the Phase-1 export/zero-trust buyer requires) and state that premium. Surface the managed-index IDLE billing floor as a fixed cost that exists from the first federated node and decide who eats it before consortium revenue arrives. Re-run the breakeven-customer-count with the corrected floor.
- **[HIGH]** Per-cell isolated Vault Community 'one Vault per cell' is presented as a cost win (no Enterprise license) but is silently an OPS cost. Each managed confidential cell now runs its own Vault Community instance that must be unsealed, backed up, key-rotated, monitored, and upgraded. At N cells that is N Vault instances for a 3-engineer team, plus the unseal/recovery operational burden Vault is notorious for. The plan counts the license savings ($0-100/mo) but not the per-cell operational toil.
  - *Why:* The plan's stated principle is to keep ops off the 3 engineers (it does this for the central index by going managed). Running N self-operated Vault instances directly contradicts that for the most operationally finicky component in the stack; Vault unseal/recovery incidents are a classic small-team time sink.
  - *Fix:* Default to cloud KMS per-tenant keys (the stated alternative) rather than per-cell Vault Community, since KMS is managed and eliminates the unseal/backup/upgrade toil entirely. Reserve self-run Vault for sovereign/on-prem only (where the university operates it). State the per-cell Vault operational cost explicitly if it is kept anywhere managed.
- **[HIGH]** SpiceDB 'cell-local replica' is treated as a configuration choice but is custom infrastructure the plan must BUILD and OPERATE. SpiceDB has no native 'replicate a subset of tuples into a regional read-replica cell with home-minted ZedToken carry' primitive. The plan's revocation-correctness story, partition behavior, and the p99<50ms cell-local Check SLO all depend on this replication layer existing and being correct, yet it is listed as a Phase-1 build item alongside the revocation authority, the freshness watermark feed, the per-shard revocation bitmaps, and the egress PEP. That is a dense cluster of bespoke distributed-systems infrastructure for a 3-person team in a single phase.
  - *Why:* Bespoke cross-region authz replication with ordering/freshness guarantees is genuinely hard distributed-systems work, the kind that takes a dedicated team quarters to get right and harden. Compressing it into Phase-1 alongside everything else is the schedule risk most likely to slip and the one most likely to ship a subtle correctness bug (which here is a confidentiality leak).
  - *Fix:* Validate in the Week-1/Phase-0 spike (already Open Q #5) whether the cell-local replication can be done with SpiceDB's actual primitives or whether it requires building a replication layer, and if the latter, treat it as its own multi-month workstream with a dedicated owner, not a line item. Consider whether Phase-1 can ship with synchronous cross-region Checks (simpler, slower, available) and defer cell-local replication until latency actually demands it.
- **[MEDIUM]** The M4 Max 36GB dev constraint vs prod parity is acknowledged but under-resourced for the build cadence. The plan correctly moves the RAGAS judge and perf/scale testing to GPU staging and keeps logic dev-testable on the Mac. But day-to-day development of the confidential tier (vLLM behavior, quantization, TEE behavior, multi-tenant GPU contention, batching) cannot be meaningfully exercised on a single 36GB Mac running Ollama, and the only prod-like environment is EPHEMERAL on-demand staging. A team that can only see prod-representative GPU behavior in spun-up-then-torn-down staging will iterate slowly on exactly the differentiating (confidential-inference) part of the product.
  - *Why:* Ollama-on-Mac vs vLLM-with-TEE-on-prod-GPU is a large behavioral gap (batching, PagedAttention, throughput, TEE memory-encryption overhead, cold-start). Developing the core differentiator against a dev environment that doesn't represent it slows the most important workstream and risks late-discovered prod-only issues.
  - *Fix:* Budget at least one persistent (not purely ephemeral) small GPU dev box for the engineer owning the confidential-inference path, separate from the ephemeral scale-staging. The cost (~$600-1500/mo) is small against the iteration speed on the product's core differentiator and should be a line item, not folded into 'on-demand staging.'
- **[MEDIUM]** SOC2 and the GRC owner are timed as a cost the plan tries to minimize, but HECVAT 'before any pilot' (Section 11.6) collides with the Phase-0 first-pilot timing. The plan says HECVAT is required before any pilot and the first pilot is the Phase-0 revenue event, yet the fractional GRC owner is only 'pulled in when SOC2 Type I work begins (Phase 0->1 boundary).' A completed HECVAT and the security questionnaire a research-IT/CISO buyer demands at pilot require policy/evidence work that competes with the 3 engineers' build time during Phase-0 itself, before the GRC owner is staffed.
  - *Why:* Security-sensitive R1 centers, the explicit beachhead, route procurement through Research IT/CISO, who will demand HECVAT and a security questionnaire at the pilot stage. If no GRC owner exists until the Phase-0->1 boundary, that work lands on the founding engineers during the heaviest build, or stalls the pilot (and thus first revenue).
  - *Fix:* Pull the fractional GRC/compliance owner into early Phase-0, not the Phase-0->1 boundary, given the beachhead is explicitly a CISO-gated security buyer. Reconcile the 'HECVAT before any pilot' requirement with the GRC staffing timeline, and cost the GRC fractional engagement into the burn model from Q1.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'one GTM/domain hire runs one motion at a time' is realistic, but the plan loads that single hire with the Week-1 dyad-discovery spike, single-center BD, pricing interviews (8-10), the procurement veto-graph mapping across 4-6 offices, AND the security-questionnaire/HECVAT pre-fill collateral, concurrently with a multi-month enterprise sales cycle. That is more than one person can carry, and it is the function that gates ALL revenue.
  - *Why:* Founder-feasibility includes GTM capacity, not just engineering. If the single GTM hire is the bottleneck on first revenue (and the plan makes revenue the gating constraint on runway), under-resourcing GTM is as fatal as under-resourcing engineering, and cheaper to fix.
  - *Fix:* Either explicitly scope the GTM hire's Phase-0 mandate to ONLY the Week-1 spike + first-center close (deferring pricing interviews and veto-graph mapping, or assigning them to a founder), or acknowledge a second commercial/founder resource is needed. Make the GTM-capacity assumption explicit in the team section rather than implied.
- **[MEDIUM]** The dual classifier as a Phase-0 'trust root' is over-engineered for N=1. At Phase-0 there is no central index, no egress PEP, no federation seam, confidential data physically cannot leave the single cell (network egress is blocked). The catastrophic failure the dual classifier guards against (a confident-WRONG downgrade letting confidential data egress) cannot occur in a single cell with egress blocked, because there is no outbound path. Building two independent classifiers + agreement logic + classification-time injection corpus in Phase-0 spends scarce engineering on a control whose threat doesn't yet exist.
  - *Why:* This is over-engineering against the plan's own MVP-discipline principle. The dual classifier earns its cost when the egress seam exists (Phase-1). In Phase-0 a single classifier that fail-closes to confidential is sufficient because the worst case is 'over-classify and route local,' which is harmless when everything is local anyway.
  - *Fix:* Ship a single fail-closed-to-confidential classifier in Phase-0; introduce the dual-classifier agreement-for-tier-down logic in Phase-1 alongside the egress PEP, where a wrong downgrade can actually cause a leak. This removes a meaningful chunk of Phase-0 scope with zero security loss at N=1.
- **[LOW]** The plan never costs the self-hosted scholarly-corpus ingestion and storage as an ops/infra line. OpenAlex + Crossref + OpenCitations + arXiv + ORCID dumps + Semantic Scholar bulk + PMC OA are each multi-GB-to-hundreds-of-GB snapshots that must be downloaded monthly, parsed, deduped, embedded (SPECTER2/Qwen3 over tens of millions of records), and stored, a real recurring compute + storage + engineering-maintenance cost that appears in Section 6.4 as a licensing table but never in the COGS model or the team workload.
  - *Why:* Embedding tens of millions of OpenAlex records on the team's own infra is a non-trivial one-time-plus-monthly GPU/compute bill and a pipeline-maintenance burden (snapshots change schema and break parsers). It's a foreseeable cost the burn model omits.
  - *Fix:* Add a 'scholarly corpus ingestion + embedding + storage' line to the COGS/infra model and acknowledge the monthly-snapshot pipeline-maintenance toil as part of the ML/RAG engineer's ongoing load, not a one-time setup.

## Review of plan4  (9 critical / 20 high)


### Lens: Distributed-systems & scalability: federation + exchange + control/data-plane split, consistency model, sync mechanics (CDC/events), bottlenecks, failure domains, hot tenants, KG + vector-index growth, cross-node latency.

*Verdict:* This plan is unusually self-aware for its lens — it has already absorbed three rounds of distributed-systems critique and converted the obvious failure modes (revocation fail-open, clock-skew ordering, authority SPOF, shard skew, distributed monolith) into named decisions with mitigations. The control/data-plane split is correct in shape: confidential data stays cell-local, only a public/shared-tier projection and a thin authorization/revocation control plane cross the seam. That is the right topology and it genuinely defends Risk #1. BUT the plan repeatedly resolves hard distributed-systems problems by ASSERTING a mechanism name ('synchronous authoritative revocation check,' 'cell-local SpiceDB replication+revision-translation,' 'fenced monotonic version,' 'tombstone coalescing') without specifying the protocol that makes it correct, and several of these names paper over genuine impossibility-adjacent problems. The most dangerous moves: (1) putting a synchronous cross-region authority check on the un-sheddable confidential hot path while simultaneously claiming a 6.5s composite SLO and 'fail-closed under partition' — these three goals are in direct tension and the latency/availability math is hand-waved into an '80-150ms p99' guess that is asserted, not derived; (2) the conformance-attestation 'exhaustiveness' mechanism for TierLattice is a distributed-consensus-on-schema problem disguised as a CI gate, and 'control plane refuses activation until all consumers attest' is itself a global-coordination bottleneck and liveness hazard; (3) CDC via Debezium/Kafka is named but the ordering/idempotency/replay semantics across the privacy projection and the central index are undefined, which is exactly where a confidentiality leak or a stale-grant leak hides. The federated graph and index growth are costed only 'at N=10/50/200' as an open question — i.e., not actually costed. None of these sink the platform, but several are high-severity gaps where the plan's confidence outruns its specification.

- **[CRITICAL]** The synchronous authoritative revocation check sits on the un-sheddable confidential drill-down hot path AND is multi-region AND must fail-closed under authority partition AND must hold a 6.5s composite SLO. These four constraints are mutually corrosive and the plan resolves the tension by asserting a single unsourced number ('~80-150ms p99'). A synchronous check against a multi-region consensus authority (Risk #4: 'multi-region consensus') on the hot path inherits that consensus's tail latency and its CAP posture: under a control-plane partition you chose fail-closed (correct for confidentiality), which means every confidential drill-down DENIES during any authority partition or leader election. That is not 'metadata-only degradation' — it is a total outage of the flagship confidential-collaboration feature (locked Feature #3) precisely when the network is stressed. The plan never derives p99 from the consensus protocol (Raft/Paxos round trips + cross-region RTT + HSM signing latency), never bounds leader-election stall time against the SLO, and never reconciles 'synchronous consensus check per request' with 'horizontally scalable' (NFR) — a per-request synchronous global authority is an anti-scaling serialization point.
  - *Why:* This is the load-bearing fix for the 'v3 leak' and the plan's headline claim ('the v3 leak is closed'). If the latency/availability math doesn't close, the choice is between (a) relaxing to async/allow-window on confidential data (reopening the leak the plan claims to have closed) or (b) accepting frequent confidential-feature outages under normal cloud partition rates. Either reopens a locked requirement (end-to-end enforced confidentiality, or horizontal scalability).
  - *Fix:* Derive the budget, don't assert it. Specify the authority's consensus protocol and quorum geometry, then compute worst-case hot-path latency = client→cell RTT + cell→authority-quorum RTT (cross-region) + consensus commit + HSM sign, with a separate leader-election stall bound. Then either: (a) make the authority READ path lease-based and locally-replicated so the hot-path check is a local read of a fenced lease (not a cross-region consensus round trip) with a bounded lease TTL that defines the actual fail-open window — and state that TTL as the honest confidential allow-window; or (b) keep synchronous-cross-region only for a narrow 'highest-sensitivity' subset and publish the measured outage minutes/year that fail-closed-under-partition implies at realistic partition rates. Open Question #4 must report a measured number from the Phase-1 spike as a release gate, not a target.
- **[HIGH]** Cell-local SpiceDB 'replication + revision-translation table' for monotonic carried-ZedToken reads is named as a Phase-1 deliverable with a 'fallback to synchronous cross-region Checks,' but the plan itself flags (Risk #28, OQ #5) it may be a bespoke build. SpiceDB's consistency model is Zookie/ZedToken-based 'at-least-as-fresh' reads against its own datastore; there is no first-class primitive for replicating the relationship graph to a remote cell AND translating revisions so a carried ZedToken from the authority maps to a monotonic local revision. Building that correctly is a distributed-consistency project (causal-token translation across two independent revision spaces). The 'fallback to synchronous cross-region Checks' is not a smaller thing — it is finding #1's per-request cross-region latency applied to EVERY authorization check, not just revocation.
  - *Why:* Authorization is on every private/confidential request and every discovery hit (Risk #14: 'on every private/confidential request'). If cell-local replication doesn't pan out, the fallback puts a cross-region Check on the entire read path, blowing the p95<800ms discovery SLO (Phase 2) and the 6.5s confidential SLO simultaneously. This is a single unproven primitive that two SLOs depend on, gated behind a Week-1 spike that may simply say 'no.'
  - *Fix:* Treat this as a go/no-go architectural fork, not a fallback. Before Phase 1 build, prototype ZedToken→local-revision translation against SpiceDB's actual replication story (watch/LookupResources API + local materialized cache with a staleness bound) and MEASURE replication lag + fail-closed-on-lag behavior. If translation can't guarantee monotonic reads under lag, do not ship 'synchronous cross-region Check on every request' — cache authorization decisions cell-locally with a short bounded TTL and synchronous re-check ONLY on the confidential-sensitive subset (collapsing into finding #1's design). State the cache staleness bound as a first-class consistency guarantee.
- **[HIGH]** CDC sync mechanics (Debezium + Kafka, per-tenant partitioned, tombstone coalescing) are named but correctness-critical semantics are undefined: (1) ordering across the cell→central-index boundary — Kafka gives per-partition order only, so 'per-tenant partitioned' lets a grant-revoke and a re-publish for the same record reorder if they traverse different keys; (2) idempotency/exactly-once on replay — Debezium snapshots and connector restarts replay events; a non-idempotent index applier can RESURRECT a revoked or downgraded record; (3) the interaction between the synchronous revocation authority and CDC-eventual index membership is unspecified — revocation of a SHARED record (un-sharing) is a shared-tier CDC event and therefore eventual, NOT synchronous, so there is an unbounded window where an un-shared record stays discoverable across the federation.
  - *Why:* Revocation of a SHARED record is the common case (Feature #1 discovery + revocable grants, locked). If shared-tier revocation is CDC-eventual with no bounded staleness and no idempotent-apply guarantee, you have a stale-discovery leak: an institution un-shares a profile/paper and it stays discoverable for an unbounded window, or reappears after a connector replay. This violates 'explicit, revocable sharing grants' as a first-class enforced concept.
  - *Fix:* Specify the index consistency contract: (a) every index record carries the PublishableProjection version + grant epoch; the applier is idempotent and MONOTONIC (rejects lower-epoch applies, so replays cannot resurrect); (b) post-filter shared-tier discovery results against the fast revocation bitmap/epoch at query time — promote the bitmap from 'perf only' to a correctness gate for shared-tier discovery so CDC lag never yields a stale HIT; (c) state and test a bounded staleness SLO for share→discoverable and revoke→undiscoverable. Make CDC ordering per-(tenant, record) key, not per-tenant, so grant/revoke/republish for one record are totally ordered.
- **[HIGH]** The TierLattice 'exhaustiveness via signed conformance attestation per consumer + control plane refuses activation until all attest + runtime fail-closed deny' (Risk #30) is a distributed-coordination mechanism masquerading as a maintainability fix, with two new hazards. LIVENESS: 'control plane refuses activation until ALL consumers attest' makes a single stale/offline/slow consumer node block a tier-lattice change platform-wide — a global barrier across N autonomous university nodes you don't operationally control; in a federation you will never get all nodes to attest promptly, so it stalls forever or you add a timeout that silently degrades to the non-exhaustiveness it was meant to prevent. CORRECTNESS: no protocol prevents a window where some nodes know the new lattice and others don't, during which cross-node tier comparisons (the MAX-rule, join semantics) operate on DIFFERENT lattices — a correctness fault in the confidentiality computation itself.
  - *Why:* The TierLattice is the algebraic core of confidentiality (MAX-rule, sticky flags, joins). A heterogeneous-lattice window across federated nodes means two nodes can disagree on whether a derived/joined artifact is confidential — a leak or over-block, both correctness failures of a locked requirement. And 'refuse activation until all attest' is an availability footgun in a system whose premise is autonomous, intermittently-connected nodes.
  - *Fix:* Make tier-lattice changes ADDITIVE and versioned with a min-node-version floor (you already use this in Risk #36): an un-upgraded node treats unknown tiers as MOST-restrictive (fail-closed), so heterogeneous lattices are safe-by-construction, not blocked-by-barrier. Drop 'refuse activation until all attest'; replace with 'gate FEATURES that emit a new tier on the consuming node's attested version,' so an un-upgraded node simply can't receive new-tier records, never blocks the platform, never compares on a stale lattice. Specify a two-phase rollout: all nodes RECOGNIZE the value (fail-closed default) before any node EMITS it.
- **[HIGH]** The federated knowledge-graph and central-index GROWTH/cost is not actually costed — it is deferred to Open Questions #7 and #9 ('cost the materialized central graph at N=10/50/200', 'cost the re-clustering migration') with no numbers, no growth model, no scaling analysis in the plan body. 'Materialized public-tier central graph' + 'managed central index (hybrid topical-macro + per-tenant sub-partition)' is a centralized component growing with (institutions × researchers × papers × embeddings). At N=200 R1 universities the public-tier corpus is plausibly tens of millions of papers/profiles with multi-vector embeddings (SPECTER2 + BGE/Qwen3) — a vector index in the 10s-100s GB to TB range plus a property graph of similar cardinality. The plan asserts 'bounded bitmap stays in a bounded shard set' and 'per-shard admission control' but gives no memory/cost ceiling, no re-embedding cost model when the embedding model changes (a 'first-class migration' that re-embeds the ENTIRE central corpus — a multi-day GPU job scaling with corpus size), and no answer to whether the materialized central graph is one logical store (HA/scaling bottleneck) or itself sharded.
  - *Why:* The central index/graph is the one component NOT cell-isolated and therefore the one place where 'horizontally scalable' (NFR) is actually tested under cross-tenant aggregate load. If its growth model is unknown, the COGS floor (Risk #37, '$3-5k/mo') is unfalsifiable at scale — plausibly a single-node-era figure; idle-index OCU floor for a TB-scale managed vector index at N=200 is far higher. Whole-corpus re-embedding is presented as 'exercised once on synthetic data' but its cost grows linearly and recurs every model upgrade.
  - *Fix:* Put a real growth model in the plan body, not the appendix: corpus-size estimates per node tier, embedding dim × count → index bytes, graph node/edge cardinality → store size, recurring re-embedding cost as a function of corpus size × upgrade cadence. Decide explicitly whether the central index/graph is itself sharded+replicated (across how many nodes) or a single managed instance — and if single, name the vertical ceiling and the N at which it breaks. Tie COGS to the growth model so '$3-5k/mo' is a function of N, not a constant.
- **[MEDIUM]** Hot-tenant handling is named ('hot-tenant quotas + cell-split' in Phase 2, 'per-shard admission control') but cell-split is undefined and brutal. A 'cell' holds confidential data, per-tenant KMS/HYOK keys, a SpiceDB relationship-graph slice, vector index shards, and graph partitions. Splitting a hot tenant means re-keying/key-scoping, re-partitioning the authorization graph (carried ZedToken/revision translation must survive the split — finding #2), and re-sharding its index bitmap (which must 'stay in a bounded shard set'). The plan treats cell-split as a Phase-2 line item with no migration protocol, no statement of online vs downtime, and no interaction analysis with the in-flight CDC stream and active grants/revocations during the move.
  - *Why:* The beachhead is industry-NDA labs and clinical centers; a single large pharma or hospital-system tenant is exactly the hot-tenant case and arrives early. If cell-split needs downtime or risks an authorization/revocation consistency gap mid-move, you can't safely scale your best customers, and a botched split during active grants is a confidentiality-correctness incident.
  - *Fix:* Specify cell-split as a migration protocol: online vs offline; how SpiceDB relationships + revision tokens migrate without breaking monotonic reads; how the CDC stream is fenced/replayed against the new cell; how active revocations are quiesced or carried across the cut. If online split isn't feasible early, state the per-cell tenant-size ceiling and provision the largest beachhead tenant in a dedicated cell from day one to avoid ever splitting it.
- **[MEDIUM]** The brokered cross-institution drill-down ('per-tenant-signed, HSM-DPoP, confused-deputy-safe', 'brokered-path circuit-breakers + per-node health', 'degraded-result verified with an offline node') is the cross-node data-plane latency path, and its composite budget double-counts against the same 6.5s SLO that already absorbs the synchronous revocation check (finding #1). A brokered drill-down is: query node → exchange broker → owner-node authorization (SpiceDB Check, possibly cross-region per finding #2) → synchronous revocation-authority check (finding #1) → owner-node retrieval → HSM signing → return: at least 3-4 cross-network hops plus two control-plane checks plus HSM signing, all serialized inside 6.5s, with the only latency artifact being the same asserted '80-150ms p99' for one hop. Failure-domain-wise, an offline owner node is handled ('degraded result'), but a SLOW owner node (gray failure) — the dominant federation failure mode — has an unspecified circuit-breaker trip threshold vs the 6.5s budget.
  - *Why:* Cross-institution confidential collaboration (Feature #3) is the differentiated feature and the highest-latency path. If per-hop budgets aren't enumerated, the 6.5s SLO is aspirational, and gray-failure (slow node) behavior — the dominant real-world failure mode in federations — is undesigned; only the clean offline case is.
  - *Fix:* Publish a per-hop latency budget table for the brokered confidential drill-down summing to 6.5s with explicit slack, including both control-plane checks and HSM signing. Set the circuit-breaker trip threshold as a fraction of remaining budget via deadline propagation (each hop passes a shrinking deadline; a hop that can't meet it fails-closed-fast rather than consuming the whole budget). Test gray-failure (injected owner-node latency), not just the offline node, in the Phase-2 milestone.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'fenced monotonic version (signed) + HLC clamp + enforced max_skew_bound' for revoke ordering (Risk #3) correctly refuses to let self-asserted timestamps order security decisions — good — but creates an unanalyzed availability coupling: if a node's clock skew exceeds max_skew_bound, the clamp+alarm implies that node is fenced from security decisions until skew is corrected. In a federation of autonomous nodes with independent NTP, skew excursions are routine; the plan doesn't state whether an over-skewed node degrades to fail-closed-deny (availability loss for its users) or is merely alarmed (in which case ordering correctness isn't actually enforced). 'No self-asserted timestamp orders a security decision' is the right principle but pushes ALL ordering onto the authority's fenced counter — re-centralizing the very serialization bottleneck finding #1 worries about onto the revocation write path.
  - *Why:* Either you fence skewed nodes (availability incidents driven by NTP outside your control) or you don't (the ordering guarantee is theater). And funneling all revoke ordering through the authority's monotonic fenced counter makes the authority's write throughput a hard ceiling on revocation rate across the entire federation — directly relevant to bulk-revoke saturation (Risk #29).
  - *Fix:* State the skew-excursion behavior explicitly (recommend fail-closed-deny for the affected node's security decisions plus alarm, since a skewed node making security decisions is unsafe) and quantify expected excursion rate / blast radius. For ordering throughput, confirm the authority's fenced-counter write path sustains the worst-case bulk-revoke rate (tie to Risk #29 coalescing) and publish the revocations/sec ceiling.
- **[MEDIUM]** The modular-monolith-with-shared-Postgres decision (Risk #32) is the right MVP call, but the extraction trigger ('extraction = own DB + event-bus-only') has no criteria, and the consolidated Postgres is a single failure domain for the entire Phase-0/1 cell (library RBAC, hash-chained audit, identity-resolution read-models, all module schemas). The hash-chained audit log is on the write path of every classified operation (FERPA-grade, fair-exchange receipts, commit-then-serve) — a hash chain is inherently SERIAL (each entry hashes the prior), so it is a write-throughput serialization point and a single-writer bottleneck per cell. At real ingestion/query volume the hash-chain append becomes the cell's throughput ceiling, and 'commit-then-serve' (Risk #13) puts that serial append synchronously in front of serving.
  - *Why:* A serial hash chain in front of every served confidential operation caps per-cell throughput at the hash-append rate and creates head-of-line blocking. For a hot tenant (finding #6) this is the first thing to fall over, and it's structural, not tunable.
  - *Fix:* Specify the audit-chain throughput design: per-(tenant/stream) parallel chains rather than one global chain (serial constraint becomes per-stream, not per-cell), with periodic cross-stream anchoring to the 'shared external timestamp' you already cite (Risk #13) to keep global tamper-evidence without global serialization. State the audit-append rate ceiling and confirm it exceeds peak served-operation rate per cell. Define the modular-monolith→service extraction trigger as a concrete metric (module change-frequency or independent-scaling need), not a vibe.
- **[LOW]** PSI (private set intersection) + global DP ledger for cross-institution collaborator-overlap (Phase 2, OQ #11) is a known-expensive cryptographic protocol whose cost scales with set sizes and participant count, treated as a Phase-2 build with usability deferred to an open question. PSI across N institutions is not simple pairwise — multiparty PSI or repeated pairwise PSI both scale poorly with N, and a global per-subject/per-target ε ledger is a centralized accounting bottleneck and a coordination point across autonomous nodes.
  - *Why:* Collaborator-overlap is part of discovery value; if PSI is too slow or the DP ledger too restrictive to return useful answers (the plan's own OQ #11 admits this), the feature degrades to uselessness — but the cost only surfaces at Phase 2 when the architecture is committed.
  - *Fix:* Move the PSI usability + scaling question to a Phase-1 spike so the Phase-2 commitment isn't blind. State whether PSI is pairwise (O(N^2) across the federation) or multiparty, and bound set sizes. Document a fallback (e.g., k-anonymized bucketed overlap via the central index instead of cryptographic PSI) so the feature isn't architecturally dependent on an unproven-at-scale primitive.
- **[LOW]** The 'per-shard incomplete covert channel' mitigation (Risk #6: 'coarse+noised completeness or whole-query-fail-closed; topology decoupled from the signal') and the central-index presence DP-noise (Risk #5) together degrade query CORRECTNESS in a way the plan doesn't quantify against the discovery SLO. Whole-query-fail-closed on a single unhealthy shard means one slow/partitioned shard fails the entire federated discovery query; DP-noised presence/count injects false-positive/false-negative noise into discovery. Neither the recall/precision impact nor the shard-failure→whole-query blast radius is bounded.
  - *Why:* A discovery product whose results are noised for privacy and fail-closed on any shard hiccup can deliver visibly wrong/empty results under normal partial failure, undermining the core 'research & literature intelligence' value (Feature #2) and trust in discovery.
  - *Fix:* Prefer per-shard partial-results-with-honest-completeness-indicator (coarse+noised) over whole-query-fail-closed for the COMMON public/shared discovery path (reserve whole-query-fail-closed for the confidential path). Quantify the DP-noise impact on top-k recall so the privacy/utility tradeoff is a measured number, not a deferred decision. State the shard-failure blast radius (one shard down = which fraction of results degraded).

### Lens: Security, privacy & confidentiality: end-to-end enforcement of the public/private/confidential model, authz completeness (ABAC+ReBAC), cross-institution sharing/revocation, confidential-tier crypto/BYOK, federation confused-deputy/IDOR risks, and FERPA/GDPR/export-control correctness.

*Verdict:* This is the most security-literate plan I have reviewed in this lens, and it has clearly absorbed prior rounds: the synchronous authoritative revocation check on the confidential hot path (Risk #2) genuinely closes the fail-open-during-propagation class of bug; the PublishableProjection structural type with a MAX-rule that extends to codes/embeddings is the correct way to make confidentiality structurally unpublishable rather than policy-asserted; the authority-anchored fenced monotonic version (Risk #3) is the right answer to malicious-node clock games; HYOK-at-rest with an explicit plaintext-at-use residual disclosed to the CISO before build is an honest crypto posture rather than an overclaim. Those are real and I credit them. BUT the plan repeatedly conflates "we named the risk in a table" with "the enforcement is specified," and the hardest leak paths live in the gaps it has not closed: (a) the classifier is the single root of the entire confidentiality lattice yet Phase-0 ships ONE fail-closed classifier with no human-in-loop default-deny semantics for unclassifiable records — a misclassification is a silent, permanent leak with no detection surface; (b) the embedding/index "MAX-rule + DP-noise + k-anon" is asserted as a GA gate but the index is built BEFORE the red-team proves the bound, and a single bad bound is unrecoverable because embeddings already shipped; (c) the BYO-confidential-endpoint verification ("network proof + mTLS + no-retention") is technically unenforceable — you cannot cryptographically prove a remote provider does not retain; (d) cross-tenant authz at the exchange relies on per-tenant-signed brokered tokens but the confused-deputy defense is named, not specified, and the projection/PEP trust boundary between two mutually-distrusting institutions is underspecified; (e) audit integrity is hash-chained per-node but there is no specified defense against a malicious NODE OPERATOR rewriting its own pre-disclosure chain. The bones are good; the leak paths are in the unspecified joints and in the parts deferred past the point where they become unrecoverable.

- **[CRITICAL]** The entire confidentiality lattice is rooted in a classifier, yet Phase-0 ships a SINGLE fail-closed classifier with no specified default-deny semantics for the unclassifiable/ambiguous case, and no human-in-the-loop adjudication. 'Fail-closed' is asserted but undefined: fail-closed to WHICH tier? If an unclassified record defaults to 'confidential' the product is unusable (everything locks); if it defaults to anything less, a misclassified confidential record is silently public/private-indexed and leaks. The plan never states the default tier for classifier abstention, and dual-classifier + agreement-for-tier-down is explicitly deferred to Phase 1 (Risk #25, Phase-0 build list). So the beachhead's confidential lab data is protected by one model's point estimate with no second opinion and no quarantine-on-uncertainty.
  - *Why:* Misclassification is the root leak path for a classification-driven system: every downstream control (router locality, egress PEP, index admission, MAX-rule) trusts the tier label. A false 'public' on a confidential record bypasses ALL of them simultaneously and the leak is silent — there is no detector because every control downstream believes the label. For an industry-NDA lab (the named beachhead), one such leak is a contract-terminating, possibly litigable event. Shipping single-classifier to the exact buyer whose value proposition IS confidentiality is the highest-stakes corner cut in the plan.
  - *Fix:* Define classifier-abstention semantics explicitly: any record the single classifier cannot confidently label MUST quarantine to a default-deny 'unclassified=confidential, excluded-from-all-retrieval' state requiring human adjudication before it enters any index — Phase 0, not Phase 1. Make the confidence threshold and the human-review queue part of the Phase-0 build, not the dual-classifier deferral. Add a Phase-0 contract test: inject low-confidence and adversarial records (e.g., confidential content with public-looking metadata) and assert zero leak into any retrievable surface. The dual classifier can wait; the default-deny-on-abstention semantics cannot.
- **[CRITICAL]** The central index privacy bound is gated on a red-team (Risk #1, #5, #8) but the index is BUILT and POPULATED before the bound is proven, and embeddings are irreversibly published once written. 'GA-GATED on the LSH/membership red-team' gates the GA launch, not the data creation. If the red-team in Phase 2 finds that presence/count/churn DP-noise + k-anonymity + per-consortium scope-keys do NOT hold the claimed bound (a likely outcome — embedding-inversion and membership-inference against cross-tenant indices is an open research problem, not a solved one), the public/shared embeddings are already in a shared central store and cannot be un-published. There is no specified rollback of already-centralized embeddings, and the MAX-rule only prevents confidential-DERIVED content — it does not prevent membership/linkage inference across PUBLIC-tier embeddings that, in aggregate, re-identify confidential collaborations.
  - *Why:* This is a one-way door deferred past its point of no return. The plan's own load-bearing assumption ('no confidential-derived content in the index') does not defend against the subtler attack: correlating public-tier presence/churn signals across consortium members to infer the EXISTENCE and membership of a confidential project (e.g., two institutions' public embeddings suddenly co-cluster → infer an undisclosed joint confidential effort). That is a confidentiality leak via metadata even with a perfect MAX-rule. A DP bound that is wrong after embeddings ship is unrecoverable.
  - *Fix:* Prove the privacy bound on SYNTHETIC/de-identified corpora BEFORE any real embedding is written to the shared central index — move the membership-inference + embedding-inversion + cross-tenant-linkage red-team to a Phase-1 gate on the index design, not a Phase-2 GA gate on the launch. Specify the quantified resistance bound (ε, k, churn-noise) in shared/contracts/ as the plan promises, AND specify the rollback/re-embed procedure if the bound is later found insufficient. Explicitly add cross-tenant co-occurrence/linkage inference (not just per-record inversion) to the threat model — the current red-team scope (#5, #8) is per-record and misses the aggregate-correlation attack.
- **[HIGH]** BYO-confidential-endpoint verification is claimed via 'in-boundary network proof + mTLS model-identity + no-retention enforcement' (Risk #12), but no-retention is not technically enforceable against a remote provider. mTLS proves you are talking to a claimed endpoint; a network-egress proof shows traffic stays in a boundary you control — but if the BYO endpoint is the institution's OWN cloud account (the whole point of BYO), the institution's provider can log/retain prompts and completions and you cannot cryptographically prevent or detect it. 'No-retention enforcement' against a third party is a contractual control dressed as a technical one — exactly the overclaim the plan correctly calls out for at-rest (HYOK) but reintroduces here for inference.
  - *Why:* Confidential data sent to a BYO inference endpoint is plaintext-at-use at a boundary the platform does not control. For export-controlled (ITAR/EAR) or EU-personal data, an unverifiable no-retention claim is a compliance fiction — a foreign-person-accessible log of ITAR data is a deemed-export violation regardless of contract. The plan forbids BYO for export/EU-personal 'unless sovereign-verified' but does not define what sovereign-verified means technically, leaving the strongest-confidentiality buyers depending on an undefined control.
  - *Fix:* Relabel no-retention as a CONTRACTUAL control with a TEE-attestation technical upgrade path, matching the honesty applied to HYOK-at-rest. For export/EU-personal, require TEE-with-remote-attestation on the BYO endpoint (the provider proves enclave + no-log via attestation), and if the BYO provider cannot attest, the router MUST refuse and fall back to the in-boundary self-hosted model — make that a fail-closed routing rule in the policy table (Risk #33's single routing table), not a contractual caveat. Define 'sovereign-verified' concretely (attested enclave + jurisdiction proof + named-person access controls) in shared/contracts/.
- **[HIGH]** The confused-deputy / IDOR defense for cross-institution brokered drill-down is named ('per-tenant-signed, HSM-DPoP, confused-deputy-safe') but not specified, and the trust model between two mutually-distrusting institutions at the exchange is underspecified. When Institution A requests drill-down into Institution B's shared confidential artifact, WHO performs the authz check and on WHOSE assertions? The plan says 'owner-side authoritative determination' and 'grantee attributes untrusted hints' (Risk #9) for export, which is correct — but it does not generalize that to the brokered-path authz: the broker must not be confusable into using A's identity to access B's data beyond the grant scope, and B must re-derive the grant scope from ITS OWN policy, never from the broker's or A's claim. With per-tenant-signed tokens, a malicious or compromised exchange node could mint or replay a token for a scope it was not granted unless B independently validates scope against its own grant store on every request.
  - *Why:* This is the classic federation confused-deputy: the exchange/broker is a deputy with credentials to talk to many tenants; if any tenant's authz trusts the broker's framing of the request rather than re-deriving authority from its own grant store, a single compromised or buggy broker becomes a cross-institution IDOR that defeats the entire isolation guarantee. DPoP binds a token to a key but does not by itself prevent scope confusion if the resource server trusts the presented scope. The plan's strongest authz statement (owner-side determination, untrusted grantee hints) is scoped to export only, not made the universal rule.
  - *Fix:* Make 'owner-side authoritative re-derivation of grant scope, broker/grantee assertions are untrusted hints' the UNIVERSAL invariant for ALL cross-tenant access, not just export US-person checks. Specify: every brokered drill-down request carries a grant-ID; the OWNER node looks up that grant in its own authoritative grant store, re-derives scope + tier + caveats + revocation status, and ignores any scope claim in the token. Add a Phase-1 contract test (the plan has 'grantee-over-assert-denied' — good, but add 'broker-over-assert-denied' and 'replayed-token-for-revoked-grant-denied'). Specify token audience-binding and single-use/short-TTL + the synchronous revocation check (Risk #2) on the SAME path so a replayed token hits a live deny.
- **[HIGH]** Audit integrity defends against tampering by external parties and divergence between nodes (hash-chain, fair-exchange receipts, external timestamp, owner-WORM, divergence auto-suspend — Risk #13) but does NOT specify a defense against a malicious or compelled NODE OPERATOR rewriting its OWN local audit chain before any cross-node receipt exists. In a federated model each university operates its own node and owns its own audit log; the hash-chain is only tamper-EVIDENT to a verifier who holds a prior anchor. For purely-internal confidential access (no cross-institution receipt generated), there is no external anchor, so a node operator (or an attacker with node-admin) can truncate/rewrite the local chain and re-seal it. The plan's 'shared external timestamp' and 'fair-exchange receipts' only cover DISCLOSURE events, not internal confidential access.
  - *Why:* FERPA/GDPR/export audit obligations require a non-repudiable record of WHO accessed WHAT — including internal access. If the operator who is also the data controller can silently rewrite internal-access history, the audit log fails its core compliance purpose precisely where it matters most for an insider-threat or a compelled-access scenario. The repudiation defense is asymmetric: strong across institutions, weak within one.
  - *Fix:* Anchor every node's audit chain to an external, operator-independent commitment on a regular cadence (e.g., periodic signed chain-head checkpoints to the exchange/control plane and/or a public transparency log / RFC 3161 TSA), so the chain is tamper-evident even for internal-only access and even against the node operator. Specify the checkpoint interval as the maximum undetectable-rewrite window and make it a compliance-tier parameter. Add insider/operator threat to the audit threat model explicitly — the current model assumes the node operator is trusted, which contradicts the multi-institution mutual-distrust premise.
- **[HIGH]** Revocation correctness is well-handled on the SYNCHRONOUS confidential hot path, but the plan explicitly accepts a 'bounded ALLOW window' for lower tiers (private/shared non-high-sensitivity) and for the durable-buffer recovery path under authority outage (Risk #2, #4, Q4). A bounded allow-window means that after a grant is revoked, private/shared data remains accessible to the (former) grantee for up to the window (stated as 5s or 30s). For a revocation triggered by a SECURITY event (compromised grantee account, terminated employee, withdrawn consent under GDPR Art. 7(3), FERPA consent withdrawal), a 30s — or worse, a recovery-measured — allow window is a window of unauthorized access to private/personal data. The plan honestly labels it, which I credit, but honesty about a leak is not closure of the leak.
  - *Why:* GDPR consent withdrawal and FERPA consent revocation are supposed to take effect promptly; a documented 30s (or longer-under-degradation) window where revoked personal data is still served is a documented compliance gap, and 'we labeled it' is not a defense to a regulator or a DPA. More dangerously, the allow-window for the recovery path is 'recovery-measured' — i.e., potentially unbounded during a prolonged authority outage for lower tiers, which means a multi-hour control-plane outage = multi-hour stale-grant serving of private data.
  - *Fix:* Distinguish revocation REASON, not just tier: any revocation flagged security/consent-withdrawal/compromise MUST take the synchronous deny-correct path regardless of tier (treat it like high-sensitivity), so the allow-window applies only to benign administrative revocations of low-sensitivity public/shared data. Cap the recovery-path allow-window with a hard ceiling that triggers fail-closed for personal-data tiers when exceeded, rather than 'recovery-measured.' State the GDPR/FERPA-facing maximum-staleness commitment explicitly per tier and reason, and confirm it with the DPO (Q13) and registrar (Q14) — the current validation questions ask about gating correctness but not about the acceptability of the allow-window staleness for withdrawn consent.
- **[MEDIUM]** Sticky compliance flags (FERPA/IRB/export taint propagates via UNION-on-join, Risk #8) are specified for derivatives WITHIN the system, but the taint model has no specified handling for data EXITING via legitimate channels (export/download/copy) or for the AI completion itself. When the RAG layer synthesizes an answer grounded in a FERPA-flagged or export-tainted record, the COMPLETION is a derivative that should inherit the taint — but completions are ephemeral, returned to a user, and potentially copied/pasted/exported by that user outside the system's control. The 'allow_durable_copy default false' (Risk #16) covers stored copies but not the inherent leak of synthesized answers, and the export gate 'fires on classification' of source records but the plan does not state that GENERATED text grounded in export-controlled data is itself subject to the export gate (it often is — a technical summary of ITAR data can itself be ITAR).
  - *Why:* RAG's core function is to extract and re-present source content; a grounded answer over confidential/export-controlled sources is a confidentiality/export event at the moment of generation, displayed to whoever issued the query. If the query issuer is a foreign person (deemed export) or lacks FERPA authorization, the synthesis step leaks even when source-record access was correctly gated, because the user is authorized to QUERY but the ANSWER reveals protected content. The plan gates retrieval but does not clearly gate generation-output against the requester's attributes for tainted sources.
  - *Fix:* Make the taint flow through to the completion and re-check the REQUESTER's attributes (FERPA authorization, US-person status for export-tainted sources) at generation/response time, not just at retrieval time — the egress PEP must cover the model OUTPUT channel to the user, not only cross-node publication. Specify that completions grounded in export-controlled sources inherit export classification and are gated by the same US-person determination. Add a contract test: foreign-person requester gets a deny/redaction when the grounded answer would reveal export-tainted content even though they could retrieve the (differently-classified) surrounding context.
- **[MEDIUM]** Per-tenant/per-consortium key management (KMS + HYOK, per-consortium scope-keys for the index) is specified for confidentiality-at-rest and index scoping, but key LIFECYCLE under revocation and cross-institution offboarding is not. When a sharing grant is revoked or a consortium dissolves, the plan revokes ACCESS (synchronous check) but does not specify crypto-shredding / key rotation for the per-consortium scope-key, nor what happens to derivative-store data encrypted under a tenant KEK when that tenant LEAVES a consortium. Access-revocation without key-rotation means any party who retained ciphertext + had the scope-key at any point retains decryptability of already-observed data. Combined with 'allow_durable_copy' grants, a former consortium member could retain readable copies.
  - *Why:* Revocation that is enforced only at the access-control layer is reversible by anyone who can bypass that layer (e.g., a former member who kept ciphertext and a cached key, or a compromised node). True revocation of confidential cross-institution data requires the crypto to stop working, not just the policy to say no. The plan's strong revocation story is access-layer only; it does not close the retained-ciphertext path.
  - *Fix:* Specify key lifecycle: per-consortium scope-keys rotate on membership change, and confidential shared artifacts use envelope encryption where revocation triggers crypto-shred of the data-key wrapping (so retained ciphertext becomes undecryptable). Define what 'revocation downgraded explicitly' (Risk #16) means cryptographically for durable copies — if a durable copy was allowed, state plainly in UI/audit that revocation is best-effort and the copy is permanent, since you cannot recall a key the grantee already used. Validate the offboarding/consortium-dissolution key-rotation flow as an explicit Phase-2 milestone alongside the joint-controller arrangements.
- **[MEDIUM]** The export-control US-person model relies on 'owner-side authoritative determination + named-individual allowlist' (Risk #9, Q12) but does not address the deemed-export risk from the platform's OWN operational staff and infrastructure. ITAR/EAR deemed-export covers access by foreign persons to controlled technical data — including the VENDOR's support engineers, SREs, and any foreign-person personnel with break-glass/operational access to the managed cell, and the physical location of the compute. HYOK-at-rest makes the vendor blind at rest, but the plan's own admission is 'plaintext-at-use' in the managed cell; a foreign-national SRE with operational access to a cell processing ITAR data at use is a deemed-export event regardless of the owner-side determination about END USERS.
  - *Why:* The owner-side determination correctly governs who can READ the data through the product, but export control also governs who can ACCESS the systems processing it operationally. A vendor SRE roster that includes foreign persons, or a cell hosted in a non-US region, is a deemed-export violation that the end-user allowlist does not touch. The plan defers TEE-at-use and sovereign hosting to later phases / export-class buyers, but the deemed-export exposure exists the moment any export-controlled data is processed plaintext-at-use with foreign-person operational access.
  - *Fix:* State explicitly that export-class cells require: US-person-only operational staff (vendor side), US-region hosting, and TEE-at-use to remove vendor operational access from the deemed-export surface — and that until those are in place, export-controlled data is FORBIDDEN on the managed cell (not merely 'priced/mandatory for export' as Risk #11 says). Make the router/cell policy refuse to place export-classified data on any cell that is not export-conformant. Add vendor-personnel-nationality and hosting-region to the export conformance attestation, and confirm with the export-control officer (Q12) that the operational-access surface — not just end-user access — is covered.
- **[MEDIUM]** GDPR controllership is determined per-plane (cell=processor, exchange=joint-controller, Risk #15, Q13) and lawful basis is an EU-index release gate — good — but cross-border TRANSFER mechanics for the synchronous revocation check and brokered drill-down are not addressed. The multi-region Revocation Authority (Risk #4) and synchronous auth-check endpoint mean that EVERY confidential access on the hot path consults a possibly-cross-region authority, and brokered drill-down moves data/metadata across institutional (and potentially national) boundaries. For EU personal data, those cross-region control-plane consultations and any metadata in the exchange are international transfers requiring SCCs/adequacy — the plan mentions SCCs in the team table but does not tie the transfer mechanism to the actual cross-region control-plane and exchange data flows, and 'metadata-only for the beachhead' under degradation still transfers metadata that may be personal data (researcher identities).
  - *Why:* Researcher names, affiliations, and collaboration signals in the exchange/public-tier graph are personal data under GDPR; moving them cross-region (or to a US-region exchange) without a valid transfer mechanism is a transfer violation, and the materialized public-tier central graph (Risk #27) is a centralized store of EU personal data outside the cell boundary. The plan's index opt-out-default and DP-noise help but do not by themselves establish a lawful transfer basis or address that the public-tier graph IS a centralized personal-data store the locked requirement says to avoid centralizing.
  - *Fix:* Map every cross-region/cross-border data flow (synchronous auth-check consultation, brokered drill-down metadata, public-tier graph contents, exchange feed) to a transfer mechanism (regional control-plane pinning, EU-resident exchange instance, or SCCs/adequacy) as a Phase-1/2 design artifact, not a team-table line item. Pin the EU control-plane and exchange to EU regions so confidential-path consultations for EU data never leave the EEA. Confirm with the DPO (Q13) that the materialized public-tier graph's contents qualify as a lawful published/legitimate-interest basis for EU researcher personal data, and that opt-out-default (not opt-in) is defensible for EU subjects — GDPR often requires opt-in/consent or a documented legitimate-interest balancing test, and opt-out-default may not satisfy it.
- **[LOW]** The TierLattice exhaustiveness mechanism (Risk #30: signed conformance attestation per consumer + control-plane refuses activation until all attest + runtime fail-closed) is strong for ENUMERATED consumers, but a new tier or a tier-semantics change requires re-attestation from all distributed consumers, and the plan does not specify the behavior of in-flight data or already-published projections when the lattice changes. If a tier is added or a MAX-rule edge changes (Risk #33 says precedence change is a single-table edit), already-published projections and already-indexed embeddings were computed under the OLD lattice and are not re-derived. A tier-semantics tightening (something that was shareable becomes confidential) does not retroactively pull back already-published derivatives.
  - *Why:* Confidentiality policy is not static — institutions reclassify, regulations change, projects become sensitive. A lattice change that does not trigger re-evaluation of already-published data leaves stale, now-over-shared derivatives in the index and exchange. This is a slower leak than the critical ones but a real one for a long-lived platform.
  - *Fix:* Specify that any tightening lattice change (or per-artifact reclassification) triggers a re-derivation/withdrawal pass over published projections and indexed embeddings for affected artifacts, tied to the same declassification/reclassification audit trail (Risk #7). Version the lattice and stamp every published projection with the lattice-version it was derived under, so a change can identify and recall affected derivatives. This is the same one-way-door concern as the index bound — make published derivatives recallable by construction (versioned + provenance-carrying, which the plan already says for PublishableProjection — just extend the recall semantics to lattice changes, not only revocations).

### Lens: Maintainability & modularity: real module boundaries, decoupling, blast radius, interface clarity, testability, monorepo vs services, swappable AI-router and retrieval

*Verdict:* The plan is unusually self-aware about modularity hazards — it explicitly names the distributed-monolith trap (Risk 32), the kernel god-object (31), router/transport tier-logic duplication (33), additive-only contract rot (36), and TierLattice exhaustiveness across distributed consumers (30). That naming is genuinely better than most plans at this lens. But naming a risk is not the same as having a buildable boundary, and the plan repeatedly substitutes a mitigation label for a design. The deepest maintainability flaw is structural and self-inflicted: confidentiality is correctly made a first-class concept, but it is enforced through SO MANY crosscutting mechanisms (sticky compliance flags, MAX-rule projection, synchronous revocation checks, conformance attestations, source-class re-derivation, owner-side determinations) that confidentiality becomes a global invariant every module must cooperate on — which is the precise opposite of low blast radius. You cannot add or remove a feature module without reasoning about all of these. The 'modular to the bone' requirement and the 'confidentiality designed in end-to-end' requirement are in direct tension, and the plan resolves it by piling enforcement into shared contracts rather than isolating it. Add the team-size reality (three engineers building a modular monolith whose extraction story is 'own DB + event-bus-only' but with zero stated event schema, evolution, or ordering contract), and the most likely rot is: (a) the shared kernel/contracts package becomes the coupling point everything depends on and nothing can change cheaply, and (b) the Phase-0 monolith's import-linter boundaries silently decay into a big ball of mud because import-linter cannot enforce data/schema coupling, only import direction.

- **[CRITICAL]** The cross-cutting confidentiality enforcement makes the 'pluggable module, minimal blast radius' requirement structurally unachievable. Confidentiality is enforced via at least seven mechanisms that every feature must cooperate with: fail-closed classifier, MAX-rule PublishableProjection, sticky-UNION compliance flags on joins/derivatives, synchronous revocation check on the hot path, source-class re-derivation, owner-side determinations, and per-consumer conformance attestation. Any new feature module (e.g. mod-funding consuming IExpertiseFingerprint/ICollaborationGraph in Phase 3) must correctly thread ALL of these or it becomes a leak vector. There is no single enforcement boundary a module can sit behind; enforcement is smeared across the data plane. Adding feature #4 has maximal, not minimal, blast radius — it must re-derive source class, propagate sticky flags, respect projection rules, and ship a conformance attestation.
  - *Why:* Directly contradicts the locked NON-FUNCTIONAL requirement ('features are pluggable modules added/removed with minimal blast radius') AND the locked confidentiality requirement simultaneously. The plan treats these as independently satisfiable; they are coupled. A new module cannot be reasoned about in isolation, which is the definition of a non-modular system. This is the rot that sinks the 'modular platform' thesis.
  - *Fix:* Make confidentiality enforcement a mediated chokepoint, not a contract every module reimplements. Force ALL retrieval/egress/derivation through a single Policy Enforcement Point service (the egress PEP) and a single data-access broker such that feature modules physically CANNOT touch classified data except through the broker — i.e. modules receive already-projected, already-tier-checked result objects and never see raw classification logic. Prove it with a 'module cannot import the raw store / cannot construct a PublishableProjection' import-linter + runtime contract. Then adding mod-funding inherits enforcement for free instead of re-threading seven invariants.
- **[CRITICAL]** The shared contracts/kernel package is the central coupling point and the plan has no contract-evolution mechanism that scales to the four-feature, multi-tenant, multi-version reality. Risk 30 (TierLattice exhaustiveness) is 'resolved' by requiring a signed conformance attestation per consumer + control-plane refuses activation until all attest. This means every TierLattice change (adding a tier, changing a code-classification, refining the MAX-rule) requires re-attestation from EVERY consumer across EVERY node before activation. With N federated nodes each running possibly different versions, a single lattice change is a global, synchronized, all-consumers-must-cooperate migration. That is the highest-coupling possible design for the most security-critical shared type.
  - *Why:* The TierLattice and shared contracts are load-bearing for confidentiality (locked) AND for modularity (locked). If every change to them is a fleet-wide blocking migration, the platform ossifies: nobody dares change the lattice, so confidentiality posture freezes at v1, and feature modules accrete workarounds. This violates maintainability at the exact point the architecture is most safety-critical. 'Control plane refuses activation until all attest' is a global liveness coupling masquerading as a safety property.
  - *Fix:* Separate the lattice's SAFETY semantics (must be globally consistent) from its EXTENSIBILITY (per-feature classification codes). Make the core lattice a tiny, near-frozen, formally specified type (3 tiers + ordering) and push feature-specific classification into per-module policy that maps onto the frozen lattice. Define an explicit lattice-version negotiation: nodes advertise supported lattice versions; the exchange operates at the min-common version; new tiers are additive-with-default-deny so a non-upgraded consumer fails closed WITHOUT blocking the upgrade of others. Get rid of the synchronous all-attest-before-activation gate for additive changes.
- **[HIGH]** 'Extraction = own DB + event-bus-only' is stated as the modular-monolith-to-services escape hatch (Risk 32), but there is NO event contract design anywhere: no event schema/versioning, no ordering/idempotency guarantees, no consumer-group/replay semantics, no schema registry, and Kafka is explicitly deferred out of Phase 0 and only appears in Phase 2 as CDC plumbing. So the declared decoupling mechanism does not exist when the first extraction would happen, and CDC (Debezium reading Postgres) is data-coupling, not an event contract. A module extracted this way reads another module's table changes — that is the distributed monolith the plan claims to avoid.
  - *Why:* The entire 'modular monolith now, services later, blast radius stays small' story rests on a clean extraction seam. If the seam is 'Debezium off a shared Postgres', extracted services are coupled to internal table schemas of the module they left behind. Changing that module's schema breaks the extracted service silently. The plan's own Risk 32 mitigation reintroduces Risk 32.
  - *Fix:* Define module-to-module communication as published domain EVENTS with an explicit versioned schema (e.g. a registry + Avro/Protobuf + compatibility rules) from Phase 0, even inside the monolith (in-process event bus with the same contract). CDC/Debezium is acceptable ONLY for replicating a module's OWN data to its OWN read store, never as a cross-module integration API. State idempotency, ordering, and replay guarantees per event topic. Without this, do not claim 'event-bus-only' as a decoupling property.
- **[HIGH]** import-linter is the ONLY isolation mechanism for the entire Phase-0 monolith ('consolidated Postgres with import-linter isolation only', explicitly NO DB-role isolation until Phase 1). import-linter constrains Python import direction only. It cannot stop module A from reading module B's tables via the shared connection, cannot enforce data ownership, cannot prevent shared mutable state, and cannot enforce transaction boundaries. So the Phase-0 'module boundaries' are enforceable only at the import graph, while the real coupling (shared schema, shared transactions, shared session) is unconstrained.
  - *Why:* This is exactly how modular monoliths rot into big balls of mud: the import graph stays clean while the database becomes a shared global variable. By the time Phase-1 tries to add per-module schemas + DB-role grants, every module will already depend on cross-reading tables, making the extraction the plan promised is cheap into an expensive retrofit. The 'blast radius' claim is false at the data layer for the entire MVP.
  - *Fix:* Even in the consolidated Postgres, enforce per-module schemas + per-module DB roles with REVOKE on other modules' schemas from Phase 0 (this is nearly free — it's DDL + GRANT, not new infra). That makes data ownership a checkable boundary on day one and makes Phase-1 extraction mechanical. Pair import-linter with a 'no cross-schema query' lint/runtime check. Do not defer DB-role isolation to Phase 1 — that defers the only enforcement that actually preserves the extraction seam.
- **[HIGH]** The AI model-router's swappability is asserted but undercut by the tier→locality coupling and BYO-provider requirement, with no model-capability abstraction. Risk 33 mandates a 'single tier→locality routing table both router and transport consult.' Routing is therefore by DATA CLASS, not by model CAPABILITY. But the locked AI requirement also demands provider-agnostic routing AND bring-your-own-provider-per-institution. There is no stated abstraction for 'what can this model do' (context window, embedding dim, tool-use, structured-output, cost) — so adding a new provider or a tenant's BYO endpoint means the router must learn provider-specific quirks, and the locality table must be re-reconciled per tenant.
