Embedding-model swaps (nomic->Qwen3/BGE in Risk 40) change vector dimensionality, which ripples into the index, the projection codes, and the membership-inference bound (Risk 5/8) — a cross-cutting change with no migration contract.
  - *Why:* Provider-agnostic + BYO-per-institution is a locked requirement and a core sell. If the router couples model selection to a global locality table without a capability/cost abstraction, every new provider or tenant key is a router edit with unbounded blast radius, and embedding swaps silently invalidate the central index's privacy guarantees. 'Swappable' is claimed but not structurally true.
  - *Fix:* Define an explicit ModelProvider interface (capabilities, locality-class it satisfies, cost, retention/no-train attestation, embedding dim if applicable) and make the router select over a registry of providers that DECLARE which locality classes they satisfy — rather than hardcoding tier->locality. BYO endpoints register as providers with attested locality. Treat embedding-model identity as a versioned property of the index with an explicit re-embed migration contract, and pin the membership-inference bound to (embedding-model-version, index-version) so a swap is gated, not silent.
- **[HIGH]** Testability of the federation/revocation/PEP core depends on a 'stub = thin adapter over REAL contract artifacts' plus a 'per-release contract test vs ephemeral real control plane' (Risk 34). This is the single most security-critical and distributed part of the system, and the test strategy is one ephemeral-real-control-plane integration test per release. There is no stated component-level contract test suite, no consumer-driven contract testing between the cell and exchange, no property-based testing of the TierLattice/MAX-rule, and the synchronous-revocation 'deny-correct (commit-1s-ago)' test is a single named milestone, not a continuous invariant suite. The hardest-to-test behaviors (clock skew, partition, authority outage, bulk-revoke saturation) are listed as one-time milestone verifications, not regression-protected contracts.
  - *Why:* Confidentiality is locked and the federation seam is the existential risk (Risk 1/2). If its correctness is protected by once-per-release integration tests against an ephemeral environment rather than fast, deterministic contract + property tests, regressions in tier propagation or revocation ordering will not be caught until late, and refactoring the kernel (which the plan WANTS to do via eviction, Risk 31) becomes terrifying. Hard-to-test = won't-be-refactored = rot, precisely in the safety-critical core.
  - *Fix:* Specify consumer-driven contract tests (e.g. Pact-style) between every cell and the exchange so each side's expectations are versioned and CI-enforced. Make the TierLattice MAX-rule, sticky-flag UNION, and source-class re-derivation property-tested (generate random tier/flag combinations, assert the published projection never exceeds source class). Encode partition/skew/revoke-ordering as deterministic simulation tests (a la deterministic-sim or injected-clock harness) that run every CI, not as one milestone. Without continuous invariant tests, the eviction/refactor plan (Risk 31) is unsafe to execute.
- **[MEDIUM]** The 'bounded kernel with eviction' (Risk 31) names IdentityResolution and read-models as things to evict but pins no objective criterion for what belongs IN the kernel versus what gets evicted, beyond 'change-frequency budgets' and a stated 'eviction rule.' The eviction rule is referenced but not defined in the plan. A kernel whose membership is governed by an unstated rule and a 'change-frequency budget' will accrete whatever is convenient, because there is no falsifiable test for 'this does not belong in the kernel.'
  - *Why:* God-object-by-accumulation (the named risk) is prevented only by a hard, checkable membership rule. 'Change-frequency budget' is a soft signal that gets overridden under deadline pressure (a three-person team in Phase 0). Without a concrete rule (e.g. 'kernel contains only policy types + interfaces with zero outbound deps on feature modules; anything with its own persistence or its own deploy cadence is evicted'), the kernel becomes the coupling sink.
  - *Fix:* Define the eviction rule as a checkable architectural fitness function: kernel = the set of types/interfaces with (a) zero dependencies on feature modules, (b) no persistent state, (c) referenced by >=2 features. Anything failing these is evicted. Enforce with import-linter contracts + a CI check on kernel package size/fan-in. State the rule in the plan, not by reference.
- **[MEDIUM]** GTM packaging is supposed to be 'configurable editions, not four products' (locked), but the plan never defines the feature-flag / capability-gating architecture that makes editions a config rather than a fork. Editions span top-down institutional, PLG self-serve, consortia, and campus-wide — across the SAME modular platform. With confidentiality enforcement smeared across modules (finding 1) and no stated capability/entitlement layer, the risk is that an 'edition' becomes a build-time configuration that disables modules in ways that interact with the policy plane (e.g. PLG self-serve must NOT enable confidential tiers; consortium edition must enable the exchange). There is no entitlement/feature-gate contract described, so editions will likely be enforced ad hoc per module.
  - *Why:* If editions are not a first-class, centrally-evaluated entitlement layer, the four GTM motions drift into four code paths — the exact 'four separate products' outcome the locked requirement forbids. Ad hoc per-module gating also creates security holes (a module enabled in the wrong edition exposing a tier it shouldn't).
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit Entitlement/Edition service that resolves a tenant's edition into a capability set, evaluated centrally and consumed by modules as a contract (capability X on/off). Make confidential-tier availability, exchange participation, PLG surface, and grant-intelligence gated by capabilities, not by build configuration. Add a contract test: 'PLG edition tenant cannot construct a confidential-tier request' as an enforced invariant, not a per-module check.
- **[MEDIUM]** Two parallel pipeline definitions are inherited from TigerBuddy (run_pipeline.py standalone + Dagster pipeline_v2) and the plan picks 'Dagster only' for Phase 0 but later adds Temporal (Phase 1, grant-lifecycle) and CDC/Kafka (Phase 2). That is three orchestration substrates (Dagster, Temporal, Kafka-CDC) with overlapping responsibilities (Dagster does batch DAGs, Temporal does durable side-effects, Kafka does streaming) and no stated boundary for which orchestrator owns which class of work. Overlapping orchestrators with fuzzy ownership is a classic maintainability sink — a developer won't know where a new background job belongs.
  - *Why:* Each orchestrator is its own operational + mental-model burden for a tiny team, and unclear ownership means logic migrates between them opportunistically, coupling them. This is workflow-engine sprawl, which rots fast precisely because each is independently 'justified.'
  - *Fix:* State a one-line ownership rule per substrate now: e.g. Dagster = ingestion/index batch DAGs only; Temporal = durable, compensating, long-lived business workflows (grant lifecycle, revocation propagation side-effects) only; Kafka/CDC = data replication to read stores only, never business logic. Add the rule to the contracts doc and gate new background work against it in review. Reconsider whether Temporal is needed at all in Phase 1 vs. a Dagster sensor, to defer a whole substrate.
- **[MEDIUM]** Retrieval swappability is claimed via 'retrieval planner + orchestrator (single-shot)' with later 'planner strategies (CRAG/PPR)', but the plan hardcodes specific engines deep into other layers: AGE/Neo4j/Memgraph for graph (Risk 39), HippoRAG2/GNN (Phase 2), Qdrant/OpenSearch split (Phase 2), SPECTER2/Qwen3/BGE embeddings (Risk 40). These are named as decisions, not behind a stable retrieval interface. The privacy guarantees of the central index (Risk 5) are coupled to the specific embedding model and index engine. So 'swappable retrieval' is contradicted by the fact that swapping the graph DB (Risk 39 explicitly anticipates this) or the embedding model ripples into the index privacy bound, the projection codes, and the team-assembly quality.
  - *Why:* The locked requirement values modular/swappable retrieval, and the plan's own Risk 39 expects to swap the graph engine. If there's no IRetrievalStrategy / IGraphStore / IVectorStore interface insulating callers from engine choice — and the privacy bound is pinned to a concrete model — every swap is a cross-layer change with security implications. The current TigerBuddy code (hybrid_retriever.py) has a single concrete HybridRetriever class with no interface, so the team has no existing abstraction to build on; the plan must specify it.
  - *Fix:* Define IVectorStore, IGraphStore, ILexicalIndex, and IRetrievalStrategy interfaces with the fusion (RRF) and planner consuming only those. Make the central-index privacy bound a property of an IIndexProfile (embedding-model + engine + DP params) so a swap forces re-validation through a gate rather than silently invalidating Risk 5. Provide a contract test that any IGraphStore impl must pass (the multi-hop/PPR correctness suite) so the Neo4j/Memgraph fallback is a drop-in, not a rewrite.
- **[LOW]** The plan is delivered as a single monolithic document where 'the architecture is constant; only build order changes' — but the modular boundaries themselves are described almost entirely as prose mitigations in a risk table (Section 17) rather than as a dependency-graph / module map with explicit interface inventory. There is no canonical list of modules, their owned data, their consumed/published contracts, and their allowed dependency edges. Maintainability reviews, onboarding, and blast-radius analysis all require that artifact and it is absent.
  - *Why:* If the module boundaries live only as scattered risk-mitigation sentences, they are not enforceable or even consistently knowable. New engineers (and the eviction/extraction decisions) need a single source of truth for the module graph. Its absence is itself a maintainability defect.
  - *Fix:* Produce a one-page module dependency map + an interface inventory (each interface: owner module, consumers, version, stability tier) as a committed artifact in shared/contracts/, and make the import-linter config the executable mirror of that map. This turns the prose mitigations into a single checkable boundary spec.

### Lens: Product, GTM & business viability

*Verdict:* This plan is unusually disciplined on the *mechanics* of GTM risk: it names a beachhead segment (industry-NDA labs, PHI-under-BAA clinical centers), imposes a Week-1 written-price-indication KILL-GATE, decouples first revenue from the rare consortium dyad via a paid sandbox pilot, models real salary burn and runway, and confronts the data-layer incumbent (Pure/Symplectic/Clarivate) as the primary market risk. That is far better than typical founder optimism. But the discipline is concentrated on *de-risking the act of selling Phase-0*, and it papers over three fatal-class business problems. First, the Phase-0 product the plan actually ships — a single-tenant, single-IdP, HYOK-at-rest confidential RAG box for one lab — has ZERO of the federation/cross-institution value that is the entire locked thesis and the entire moat (the plan even admits "does NOT inherit the federation moat"); the beachhead buyer is therefore being sold a commodity secure-RAG appliance against Glean/NotebookLM-Enterprise/Azure-OpenA0-on-your-data with no durable differentiation, and the leap from "lab bought a secure RAG box" to "institutions federate confidential research" is a GTM chasm, not a build-order toggle. Second, the cross-institution network effect — the only thing that makes this a platform rather than a feature — has a brutal cold-start that the plan addresses only with "framework LOIs" and a "structural disincentive" hand-wave; a non-binding LOI is not a node, and you need ~2 willing institutions with a *pre-existing reason to share confidential data* before tier-3/4 value exists at all. Third, the "one product, four buyers" requirement is asserted via packaging but the buyers have contradictory procurement realities, price ceilings, and value props that the plan never reconciles — PLG researchers will not pay, research offices buy slowly via committee, consortia buy on multi-year cycles, and the Phase-0 wedge serves NONE of them (it serves a corporate-adjacent NDA lab). The $30k/yr floor ASP is almost certainly below the fully-loaded COGS+CAC of a HYOK-deployed, SOC2-gated, security-reviewed enterprise sale with a regulated buyer. The plan has engineered its way out of building the wrong thing but has not proven anyone will pay enough, soon enough, for the thing that earns revenue first to fund the thing that is actually defensible.

- **[CRITICAL]** The Phase-0 revenue wedge is a single-tenant confidential-RAG appliance with NONE of the locked federation/cross-institution value, and the plan explicitly concedes it 'does NOT inherit the federation moat.' First revenue therefore validates demand for a commodity 'secure RAG over my lab's docs' product — a category already served by Glean, NotebookLM Enterprise, Azure OpenAI on-your-data, Microsoft Copilot, and a dozen vertical secure-RAG vendors — not for the federated platform the company is actually betting on.
  - *Why:* If the only revenue-bearing thing you can ship is the part with no moat, you are funding the company on a feature that incumbents can match in a quarter, while the differentiated platform (federation) remains entirely unvalidated by paying customers. The KILL-GATE proves a lab will pay $30k for secure RAG — it proves NOTHING about whether institutions will pay for cross-institution confidential collaboration, which is the whole thesis. You can pass Phase-0 with flying colors and still have a dead platform.
  - *Fix:* Add a Phase-0 *demand* gate for the federation thesis itself, not just the appliance: in the same Week-1 interviews, secure written evidence that the SAME buyers have a concrete, funded cross-institution confidential-sharing pain (a named collaboration they cannot currently do, with a budget owner). If you cannot find a buyer who wants BOTH the box AND the federation, the federation thesis is unvalidated regardless of Phase-0 revenue — say so and treat Phase-1 as a second, independent kill-gate with its own pivot option, not a build-order continuation.
- **[CRITICAL]** Cross-institution network-effect bootstrapping (cold-start) is hand-waved. The entire tier-3 (shared confidential workspaces) and tier-4 (cross-institution grant team assembly) value, plus the central public-tier graph, are worthless at N=1 and marginal at N=2. The plan's only cold-start instruments are non-binding 'framework LOIs' and a presumed 'structural disincentive' that keeps incumbents out. An LOI is not a deployed node, does not generate cross-institution data, and does not de-risk the dyad's master-agreement (which the plan itself flags as a separate falsification risk).
  - *Why:* Federated platforms die in the cold-start gap: institution A won't deploy because there's no one to federate with; institution B says the same. The cross-institution features need a PRE-EXISTING reason for two specific institutions to share confidential data (an active multi-site grant, an industry consortium, a clinical network) — and the named beachhead (industry-NDA labs) is precisely the segment LEAST likely to want to federate confidential IP with a peer institution. The plan sells the box to buyers structurally hostile to the federation feature, then hopes to retrofit federation. That is the textbook bootstrapping failure.
  - *Fix:* Identify and validate, before Phase 1, at least one *organic* multi-institution unit that already shares confidential data under an existing legal vehicle (e.g., an NIH multi-site U01/U54 center, an NSF AI institute, an existing research consortium with a master DUA). Sell the FIRST federation dyad into a relationship that already exists, where the platform digitizes an existing sharing agreement rather than creating a new sharing relationship from scratch. Make 'one pre-existing confidential-sharing relationship identified and its legal vehicle confirmed' a hard Phase-1 entry gate, ranked above the consortium-BD hire.
- **[HIGH]** The $30k/yr floor ASP almost certainly sits below fully-loaded COGS + CAC for the Phase-0 motion the plan describes. The sale requires: HYOK key custody integration, per-tenant vLLM GPU inference, idle-OCU/index floor (~$3-5k/mo COGS owned 'from node 1' per Risk #37 = $36-60k/yr COGS alone), a security review (even if sandboxed), HECVAT, fractional GRC counsel, SOC2 Type I, and a regulated buyer's procurement cycle. A $30k/yr deal against $36-60k+/yr of dedicated COGS plus a multi-month founder-led enterprise sale is gross-margin-negative and CAC-payback-never.
  - *Why:* The plan's own Risk #37 raises COGS to ~$3-5k/mo and states the idle index floor is owned from node 1, yet Risk #21's KILL-GATE floor is $30k/yr. Those two numbers are internally contradictory: the floor price is below the floor cost. A KILL-GATE that you can 'pass' while being structurally unprofitable per deal is not a real gate — it greenlights a business that loses money on every customer and cannot scale out of it because each confidential tenant carries dedicated GPU/index cost.
  - *Fix:* Reconcile the price floor to COGS: either raise the KILL-GATE floor ASP to clear loaded COGS+CAC with positive contribution margin (likely $75-150k/yr for a HYOK+GPU+SOC2 enterprise deal), or re-architect Phase-0 COGS to shared/burstable GPU and shared index so per-tenant fixed cost collapses (contradicting the 'idle OCU floor from node 1' decision). State the target contribution margin per deal as an explicit gate condition alongside the price indication.
- **[HIGH]** 'One product, four buyers via packaging' is asserted, not demonstrated, and the four buyers have contradictory economics that packaging cannot reconcile. PLG researchers/labs (bottom-up) have ~$0 individual budget authority and churn; research offices/library/IT (top-down) buy via 6+ month committee cycles with veto chains; consortia/alliances buy on annual/multi-year governance cycles with their own procurement; campus-wide is an enterprise license negotiation. The Phase-0 wedge (industry-NDA lab) is arguably a FIFTH buyer (corporate-research-adjacent) that maps cleanly to none of the four locked motions.
  - *Why:* Editions/packaging change SKUs and entitlements, not the fundamental sales motion, price elasticity, security-review depth, or buying-committee structure. A PLG self-serve funnel and a consortium multi-year contract are not two configs of one motion; they require different pricing, onboarding, support, security posture, and CAC. The plan defers PLG to Phase 3 and calls it 'not a revenue funnel,' which quietly admits the bottom-up motion does not actually carry revenue — yet 'bottom-up PLG' is a LOCKED requirement. Calling PLG a 'brand surface' may violate the locked GTM requirement that the product be *sellable* (revenue-bearing) to all four.
  - *Fix:* Make the PLG-as-loss-leader posture explicit against the locked requirement and get sign-off that 'sellable to PLG' is satisfied by a free/cheap brand-surface tier whose purpose is land-and-expand into institutional, not standalone PLG revenue. Then map each of the four (five) buyers to: budget owner, typical deal size, sales-cycle length, security-review depth, and which Phase introduces real revenue for that motion. If any motion has no credible revenue path, surface it as an unmet locked requirement now rather than discovering it in Phase 3.
- **[HIGH]** Competitive differentiation rests on a single load-bearing assumption (Risk #18): that data-layer incumbents (Pure/Symplectic/Clarivate) face a 'structural disincentive' to build confidential federation because it cannibalizes their centralized-SaaS/data-monetization model. This is a hope, not a moat. These incumbents already sit inside the research office, already have the procurement relationship, already hold the faculty/publication data, and can add a 'private/confidential tier' or partner/acquire for federation without cannibalizing their core — they would gate it as a premium add-on, exactly as enterprise SaaS incumbents always do.
  - *Why:* If the moat is 'incumbents won't bother,' the company is one incumbent product-roadmap decision away from losing its only differentiation — and incumbents have the distribution, the existing contracts, and the CISO trust that the startup must earn deal-by-deal. The plan even elevates this to PRIMARY risk and then mitigates it with the very assumption that makes it dangerous. Incumbent disruption theory says the cannibalization argument fails precisely when the new model is high-margin and defensible, which is what the plan claims federation is.
  - *Fix:* Replace 'incumbent won't enter' with a concrete, testable defensibility thesis: (a) a multi-institution data/trust network whose switching cost compounds with N nodes and accumulated sharing grants (real network-effect lock-in, not roadmap LOIs), and/or (b) regulatory/export-control certification depth (CMMC/ITAR/FedRAMP-adjacent) that incumbents are slow to acquire. Quantify the time-to-replicate for an incumbent and ensure the company can reach defensible N before that window closes. Do not rest the primary market risk on a competitor's presumed inaction.
- **[HIGH]** Adoption-friction timing is internally inconsistent and likely optimistic. Risk #23 claims the Phase-0 sandbox/de-identified pilot 'doesn't trip full prod security review' and SOC2 Type I lands in Phase 0 — but the beachhead is industry-NDA labs and PHI-under-BAA clinical centers, the two segments with the MOST aggressive security/compliance gating (BAA execution, IRB, vendor risk assessment) precisely because the data is regulated. A de-identified sandbox sidesteps the data, but the BUYER (CISO, privacy office, contracts) still runs vendor onboarding before signing a paid contract, even for a sandbox, if the intent is production.
  - *Why:* The plan's first-revenue thesis depends on collecting cash from a paid sandbox pilot BEFORE the full security review. For an NDA lab or a HIPAA-covered clinical center, even a paid pilot typically triggers at least a lightweight vendor risk assessment, a mutual NDA, and often a BAA before any data (even synthetic) touches vendor infra under a procurement contract. If the procurement floor is months not weeks, the runway-to-first-recurring (the plan's mo 18-24 stress case) is the optimistic end, and the $80-130k/mo burn against a 25-35 month runway gets dangerous fast.
  - *Fix:* Validate the sandbox-avoids-review claim with a real CISO/contracts officer at a named target (the plan's Open Question #17 gestures at this — promote it to a hard Week-1 KILL-GATE sub-condition: 'named buyer confirms in writing that a paid sandbox pilot can be signed without full vendor security review / BAA'). If it cannot, re-baseline the runway and first-recurring date against a full-review procurement cycle, and consider a non-regulated design-partner segment for the very first paid pilot to actually achieve fast cash.
- **[MEDIUM]** The defensibility-race timeline is self-contradictory. Risk #17 frames the threat as 'incumbent isolation in a quarter vs our lock at year 3,' and the moat (network effects across N nodes) only materializes at Phase 2-3 (N>=5 nodes, then >=2 consortia under multi-year contracts in Phase 3). The plan's mitigation is framework LOIs as a 'roadmap lock' — but an LOI signed in Phase 0-1 does not create switching cost or network value; it is a letter. The company is therefore defenseless during the exact 2-3 year window the plan identifies as the race.
  - *Why:* If real lock-in is a year-3 phenomenon and the competitive threat is a one-quarter incumbent response, the company spends its most fragile early period (single-node, no network value, commodity product) maximally exposed. The down-scope-to-regulated-niche fallback (export-adjacent/industry-NDA) is sensible but quietly abandons the broad federation thesis and most of the four-buyer mandate — i.e., the fallback is a different, smaller company.
  - *Fix:* Make the regulated-niche down-scope a *primary* plan-B with explicit trigger conditions, and pull at least one true switching-cost mechanism earlier than Phase 2: e.g., accumulated, hard-to-export sharing grants + audit history + per-tenant tuned eval gold-sets that create real data gravity even at N=2. State the minimum N and minimum accumulated-grant count required before the company is defensible, and confirm it is reachable inside the race window with the modeled GTM ramp.
- **[MEDIUM]** GTM capacity is thin to the point of risk: a single 'Founding GTM/domain' person is scoped in Phase 0 to ONLY the Week-1 kill-gate spike and first-lab close, with pricing interviews, veto-graph mapping, and HECVAT pre-fill 'assigned to a founder or fractional GRC.' Enterprise/regulated sales, even for a wedge, is a full-time multi-quarter motion (discovery, security questionnaire, legal redlines, pilot-to-production conversion). The consortium-BD specialist is correctly placed before Phase 1, but Phase 0's single GTM person closing a regulated-buyer deal solo while founders do compliance is under-resourced for the stated burn.
  - *Why:* The plan ties runway to GTM headcount and admits 'GTM ramp runs quarters behind engineering' — which means revenue lands late while burn (3 founding engineers + GTM + fractional counsel = the $80-130k/mo) runs early. A single under-supported GTM hire missing the first close cascades directly into the runway model the plan calls 'stress-tested to mo 18-24.' The mitigation for recurring-revenue slip past month 24 (shrink team / lean on services / raise more) are all dilutive or distracting.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) ensure a founder owns the GTM/regulated-sales motion full-time (not just the kill-gate spike) with the hired GTM person in support, or (b) explicitly budget the first regulated close as a 6-9 month founder-led motion and re-derive the cash-out date against that, not against the optimistic 'first paid sandbox pilot' milestone. State the pilot-to-recurring conversion assumption (% and months) as an explicit, validated number, since the whole runway model hinges on it.
- **[MEDIUM]** BYO-provider/compute and HYOK — sold as buyer-trust features — are simultaneously COGS-savers in the buyer's mind but cost/ops/CAC-amplifiers for the vendor, and the plan only partially acknowledges this (Risk #21 notes 'BYO-compute is friction not escape'). Every BYO-key, BYO-endpoint, BYO-cloud configuration multiplies the security-verification surface (Risk #12: in-boundary network proof, mTLS model-identity, no-retention enforcement PER target cloud), re-triggers institutional security review, and fractures the support/COGS model — directly undercutting the 'one product via packaging' simplicity thesis.
  - *Why:* BYO is a procurement-unblocker that buyers will demand, but each BYO permutation is a bespoke integration and a fresh security review, which slows deals and raises CAC — the opposite of the scalable, packaged motion the GTM requirement assumes. If most regulated buyers insist on BYO (likely, given the segment), the company inherits enterprise-services economics, not SaaS economics, and margins/scaling assumptions break.
  - *Fix:* Constrain BYO to a small, pre-certified matrix (e.g., 2-3 named clouds, 2-3 named model providers) in early phases and price BYO as a higher-touch edition with explicit professional-services attach, so the support/verification cost is recovered in price. Model the % of beachhead buyers expected to demand BYO and its effect on per-deal CAC and gross margin; if it is the majority, re-baseline the business as services-heavy in the early phases rather than SaaS-margin.
- **[LOW]** Pricing realism beyond the $30k floor is undeveloped. The plan defers 'multi-axis pricing once a sales engineer exists' to Phase 3 and otherwise references a 'floor ASP' and 'two-SKU PLG pricing' without value-metric definition (per-seat? per-node? per-grant-team? per-confidential-workspace?). For a platform spanning four buyers and four feature pillars, the pricing axis IS the packaging strategy, and leaving it to Phase 3 means the four-buyer packaging claim is unspecified at the architectural level where it must be designed in.
  - *Why:* The locked GTM requirement demands one product serve four buyers via 'configurable packaging/editions' — that is fundamentally a metering and entitlement architecture decision (what is metered, what is gated per edition), which must be designed from day one to be modular, per the non-functional requirements. Deferring the value metric to Phase 3 risks an entitlement/metering retrofit that violates the 'minimal blast radius' modularity requirement.
  - *Fix:* Define the value metric(s) and entitlement axes now (even if list prices come later): decide what is metered per tier/buyer (nodes, confidential workspaces, federation grants, seats, grant-team assemblies) and ensure the entitlement/metering module is a first-class pluggable module in the architecture. Confirm the chosen metric aligns incentives across all four buyers and does not penalize the federation network effect (e.g., do not meter per cross-institution grant if that taxes the very behavior you want to bootstrap).

### Lens: Cost, ops & founder feasibility

*Verdict:* This is the most cost-honest version of the plan I've seen in the series: it names a real monthly burn (~$80-130k/mo), ties runway to it (~25-35 mo), includes salaries, names a Week-1 kill-gate with a written-WTP threshold ($30k/yr), pulls compliance counsel to fractional, and explicitly shrinks Phase-0 to a modular monolith with a single classifier and import-linter isolation. Those are the right moves and they fix the worst founder traps from earlier rounds. But the plan still smuggles an enormous amount of production machinery into a 'three-engineer' Phase-0/Phase-1 envelope, and — critically — the thing it is selling (federation, cross-institution discovery, revocable confidential grants) is almost entirely deferred to Phase 1+, while Phase-0 itself is, by the plan's own Risk #19, an undifferentiated single-tenant RAG tool competing with Glean/NotebookLM/ChatGPT-Enterprise. The team sizing is internally inconsistent (a $30k/yr ACV wedge cannot service the burn the plan itself states), the Phase-1 build list is a 2+ year program disguised as one phase, and several 'Phase-0' line items (HYOK, hash-chained audit, multi-IdP OIDC, GPU dev box + vLLM parity, SOC2 Type I, fractional GRC from Q1) are individually weeks-to-months of specialist work that three generalist founders will not absorb on the implied schedule. The plan over-engineers the security substrate relative to a first paying customer who, per the plan, is buying a sandbox/de-identified pilot that deliberately avoids prod security review — meaning most of the at-rest HYOK / fail-closed-classifier / egress-block machinery delivers no revenue-gating value at the only milestone that earns money.

- **[CRITICAL]** Unit economics are self-contradictory: the kill-gate floor is $30k/yr ACV but the stated burn is $80-130k/MONTH (~$1-1.56M/yr). At the named beachhead (industry-NDA labs / single PHI clinical center, sold as N=1 sandbox pilots), you need ~35-50 paying logos JUST to cover burn, before any margin, CAC, or the COGS floor (~$3-5k/mo/node per Risk #37, i.e. $36-60k/yr) that nearly equals the entire ACV. A $30k/yr deal whose dedicated-cell COGS is $36-60k/yr is sold at a structural loss.
  - *Why:* This is the founder-feasibility core. A wedge whose floor price is below its own per-tenant infra cost, sold one isolated cell at a time, cannot fund a 3-4 person team plus fractional counsel/GRC, let alone the Phase-1 federation build. The plan models runway (seed / burn) but never reconciles ACV x logos against that burn — the revenue side of the runway equation is absent.
  - *Fix:* Add an explicit Phase-0 revenue model: target logos x ACV vs monthly burn, with a gross-margin floor per cell. Either (a) raise the kill-gate WTP floor to where ACV > 2-3x per-cell COGS (likely $80-150k/yr, which changes who the beachhead buyer is), or (b) collapse Phase-0 onto shared/multi-tenant infra so per-logo COGS drops below $1k/mo, or (c) state explicitly that Phase-0 is a funded-loss validation phase covered by seed, not a self-sustaining business, and size the raise accordingly.
- **[CRITICAL]** Phase 1 is not a phase — it is a multi-year platform program listed as a single milestone block. Its build list includes: inline egress PEP + structural PublishableProjection, dual classifier, multi-region Revocation Authority with consensus + HSM-signed tombstones + synchronous hot-path check, cell-local SpiceDB replication with revision-translation (the plan itself flags as a 'bespoke build', Risk #28), Cedar ABAC, fair-exchange audit receipts, Temporal-driven grant lifecycle, subject-deprovision/SCIM session-kill, SOC2 Type I, AND a consortium-BD hire and a signed dyad. That is easily 18-36 months of work for a 3-4 person team that is simultaneously running a paying pilot and selling.
  - *Why:* The startup constraint is 'sequence an MVP that ships and earns revenue first.' But the only differentiated, sellable thing (federation + revocable cross-institution confidential sharing — the actual product) lives entirely in Phase 1. If Phase 1 is a 2+ year monolith of hardest-in-the-industry distributed-security problems, the company spends its entire seed runway (25-35 mo) reaching the point where it first has the product it's pitching. The 'architecture constant, build order is the lever' framing hides that the build order still front-loads a research-grade security program before the second customer.
  - *Fix:* Decompose Phase 1 into at least three shippable sub-phases with their own revenue/proof gates: (1.0) public-tier-only 2-node discovery with NO confidential grants (no revocation authority, no SpiceDB replication) — proves the federation seam cheaply; (1.1) single revocable confidential grant with a SIMPLE synchronous central check (single-region, no consensus, accept the SPOF for the dyad); (1.2) multi-region authority + cell-local replication only when N>2 or an SLO demands it. Defer Cedar ABAC, fair-exchange receipts, and Temporal until a customer's contract requires them.
- **[HIGH]** 'Buildable by three engineers' is asserted for a Phase-0 that contains at least six specialist subsystems each worth weeks-to-months: hash-chained tamper-evident Postgres audit, per-tenant KMS + HYOK key custody wiring, fail-closed classifier with router-violation hard-fail, k8s/IaC + persistent GPU dev box + local vLLM serving, RAGAS-in-CI with a GPU-staging judge, and Dagster classify-gates-index. HYOK alone (customer-held keys, vendor-blind-at-rest, key-rotation, break-glass) is a multi-week effort that most teams never get right. None of these are off-the-shelf 'install and go' for a 3-person team coming from a Chroma/Ollama/Streamlit prototype.
  - *Why:* The plan's credibility rests on the claim that Phase-0 fits three founders. If even two of these subsystems each slip 4-8 weeks (HYOK and SOC2-Type-I prep are the obvious candidates), the Week-1-kill-gate-to-first-pilot timeline blows out and the runway math (which assumes pilot revenue starts early) breaks. The plan has no buffer and no explicit Phase-0 calendar duration.
  - *Fix:* Attach an engineer-weeks estimate to each Phase-0 build item and a calendar duration to Phase-0 itself. Cut or defer anything not strictly required for the FIRST sandbox pilot: HYOK is unnecessary for a de-identified/sandbox pilot (Risk #23 says the pilot deliberately uses de-identified data — so the at-rest customer-key story gates no revenue yet). Move HYOK to the gate where a customer puts real confidential data in, which by the plan's own structure is later.
- **[HIGH]** The Phase-0 product is, by the plan's own Risk #19, undifferentiated against Glean / NotebookLM / ChatGPT-Enterprise, and explicitly 'does NOT inherit the federation moat.' So the revenue-earning MVP competes head-on with VC-funded incumbents on a feature (grounded Q&A over docs) those incumbents ship today, while the team spends its security/HYOK/audit budget on machinery the buyer in a sandbox pilot doesn't exercise.
  - *Why:* From a founder-feasibility view this is the worst kind of over-engineering: you pay full freight for production confidentiality infrastructure (classifier, egress block, HYOK, audit chain) at the exact stage where your differentiation is weakest and your buyer is using de-identified data. The money and the moat are misaligned in time.
  - *Fix:* Either (a) move ONE cheap federation-flavored differentiator into Phase-0 (e.g. cross-institution public-tier expert discovery using already-public OpenAlex data — no confidentiality machinery needed) so the wedge is differentiated, or (b) strip Phase-0 confidentiality machinery down to what a de-identified sandbox pilot actually requires and defer HYOK/audit-chain/dual-key to the first real-confidential-data customer. Do not pay for both the undifferentiated product AND the full security substrate simultaneously.
- **[HIGH]** Self-host / per-node operational burden per university is not costed or staffed for the federated deployment model. The locked requirement is each university owns a private node or strongly-isolated tenant. The plan's COGS line (~$3-5k/mo, Risk #37) and the 'managed cell' language imply the STARTUP operates every node. There is no model for the case where a university IT/research-office insists on self-hosting in its own cloud/on-prem (a near-certain demand from the ITAR/export and sovereign buyers the plan courts) — that requires a shippable installer, upgrade tooling, remote support, and per-node SRE that a 3-4 person team cannot provide.
  - *Why:* The deployment requirement is locked as 'its own infra OR a dedicated data plane.' If even one early buyer (especially export-control/CMMC, which the plan targets) requires customer-premises deployment, the team owes a self-host distribution, version-skew management across heterogeneous nodes, and field support — an entire product surface the roadmap never funds. Conversely if the team only ever offers managed cells, it cannot serve the export/sovereign buyers it explicitly names.
  - *Fix:* Make the deployment topology explicit per phase: state that Phase-0/1 are MANAGED-cell-only (startup-operated), and that customer-self-host is an explicitly-deferred, separately-priced edition with its own engineering line (installer, upgrade CD, support SLA) that does NOT exist until Phase 2+. Remove or asterisk the export/CMMC/sovereign buyer pursuit until that line is funded, because those buyers most often mandate self-host.
- **[HIGH]** SOC2 Type I in Phase 0 and Type II in Phase 2, plus fractional GRC from Q1, plus ISO 27001 (Phase 3), plus HECVAT pre-fill, plus DPA/DUA/SCC/joint-controller templates — this is a continuous, expensive compliance program running in parallel from quarter one, on a fractional GRC + fractional counsel. The cost of audits alone (SOC2 Type I+II auditor fees, pen-test, ISO) is real cash ($50-150k+ cumulative) that is not visible in the burn model, which only mentions 'loaded FTE.'
  - *Why:* Compliance is a classic founder cost trap: the auditor fees, pen-test vendors, and the engineering time to remediate findings are recurring cash outflows separate from salary burn. Pulling SOC2 Type I into Phase-0 (pre-meaningful-revenue) front-loads this cost at the worst cash moment, and the burn model appears to exclude it.
  - *Fix:* Add a discrete compliance-cost line to the burn model (audit fees, pen-test, GRC tooling like Vanta/Drata, remediation engineering) separate from FTE. Reconsider whether SOC2 Type I is needed in Phase-0 at all: Risk #23 says the pilot is structured (sandbox/de-identified) specifically to avoid the full prod security review — if so, SOC2 Type I may be deferrable to the Phase-0/1 boundary, saving Phase-0 cash. Make the SOC2 timing a function of buyer demand, not a fixed Phase-0 commitment.
- **[MEDIUM]** The M4 Max (36GB) dev constraint vs prod parity is acknowledged (Risk #38 adds a 'persistent small GPU dev box') but under-scoped. Dev is Ollama on Mac; prod is vLLM + (eventually) TEE attestation (Nitro/SEV-SNP/TDX, Risk #19/#11). A 'small GPU dev box' does not reproduce TEE attestation flows, multi-GPU vLLM tensor-parallel serving, or the memory envelope of the production models the confidential tier will run. The confidential-inference owner will be developing against an environment that cannot exercise the actual confidential-path failure modes.
  - *Why:* The single most safety-critical path (confidential data must not leave the boundary, must run on in-boundary models) is the one with the worst dev/prod parity. Bugs in egress-block, model-identity, and TEE attestation will surface only in staging/prod, where they are most dangerous and most expensive to debug — and where a leak is the existential Risk #1.
  - *Fix:* Specify the GPU dev box precisely (GPU model, VRAM, whether it supports the prod TEE technology) and require the confidential-egress-block + router-violation tests to run in CI against a vLLM container (not Ollama), not just the Mac local harness. Budget a real cloud GPU staging environment as a recurring line item, not a one-time 'small box.' Treat M4 Max as UI/pipeline dev only, never as the confidential-path test target.
- **[MEDIUM]** The founding team is 3 engineers + GTM founder + fractional GRC + fractional counsel, with the consortium-BD hire deferred to 'before Phase 1' and enterprise sales deferred further. But the GTM-founder's Phase-0 mandate is scoped to ONLY the kill-gate spike + first-lab close, while simultaneously pricing interviews, veto-graph mapping, and HECVAT pre-fill are 'assigned to a founder or fractional GRC.' This overloads a single GTM founder with sales + compliance ops + market research while the engineers are heads-down, and assumes a fractional GRC can carry HECVAT + SOC2 prep + 5 framework reviews (FERPA/GDPR/ITAR/DPA/SCC).
  - *Why:* Fractional resources do not scale to a continuous, parallel, multi-framework compliance program plus active deal support. The plan's own COST-med-grc-timing and COST-med-gtm-capacity tags claim resolution, but the resolution is to pile the work onto fractional/founder slots that are already nominally full. Capacity is asserted, not budgeted.
  - *Fix:* Show a Phase-0 capacity ledger: hours/week each fractional role provides vs the task list assigned. If HECVAT + SOC2 Type I prep + 5-framework review exceeds a fractional GRC's bandwidth, either reduce Phase-0 compliance scope (per the SOC2-deferral recommendation above) or convert GRC to a fuller engagement and re-show the burn. Do not assign HECVAT pre-fill to a GTM founder who is also closing the first deal.
- **[MEDIUM]** Cloud licensing / vendor cost traps are largely uncosted. The plan names CILogon (Q19 flags 'hard quote' needed — good), but also assumes SpiceDB (AuthZed — managed is paid; self-host SpiceDB cell-local replication is the bespoke build the plan dreads), Temporal (Cloud is metered per-action; grant-lifecycle side-effects could be high-volume), Debezium+Kafka (MSK/Confluent is a meaningful monthly floor), Qdrant/OpenSearch split, KMS+HSM (CloudHSM ~$1-2/hr/instance = ~$1.5k+/mo per HSM, x multi-region for the Revocation Authority), and a managed graph (Memgraph BSL / Neo4j licensing). Several of these are individually $1-5k/mo floors that idle from node 1.
  - *Why:* The COGS floor was revised to ~$3-5k/mo (Risk #37) but the multi-region HSM-backed Revocation Authority alone (CloudHSM in 3 regions + consensus) plausibly exceeds that before any tenant load. Idle managed-service floors are the canonical seed-stage cash leak, and the plan stacks ~7 of them.
  - *Fix:* Produce a per-service monthly-floor table (idle cost at N=1 and N=5) for SpiceDB/AuthZed, Temporal, Kafka/MSK, CloudHSM x regions, managed graph, Qdrant/OpenSearch, KMS. Validate the ~$3-5k/mo COGS claim against it — I expect the multi-region HSM Revocation Authority alone breaks that ceiling, which would push it to Phase-2 and force the simpler single-region authority in Phase-1 (consistent with the Phase-1 decomposition recommendation).
- **[MEDIUM]** The maintainability/modularity risk table (Risks #30-#36) commits the team to building a conformance-attestation mechanism per consumer, a control-plane that refuses activation until all consumers attest, a contract-deprecation lifecycle (introduced/deprecated/removed-in versions, min-node-version floor, M-version test-matrix cap, zero-consumer auto-flag), and signed conformance attestations. This is platform-governance machinery appropriate for a mature multi-team org, being specified for a 3-engineer pre-revenue startup.
  - *Why:* This is over-engineering of the meta-layer. A 3-person team with one or two nodes does not need a distributed conformance-attestation activation gate or a formal deprecation lifecycle with version-matrix caps — those solve coordination problems that only exist at many-consumer scale. Building them in Phase-0/1 is pure platform tax against zero customers, exactly the trap Risk #25 claims to avoid while Risks #30-36 reintroduce it.
  - *Fix:* Defer all multi-consumer governance machinery (conformance attestation, activation gates, formal deprecation lifecycle, version-matrix caps) to the phase where node count or consumer count actually creates the coordination problem (realistically N>=5, Phase 2). For Phase-0/1 a modular monolith with mypy assert_never (already listed) and ordinary contract tests is sufficient. State this deferral explicitly so it is not silently built early.
- **[LOW]** The burn range ($80-130k/mo) is a 60% spread with no breakdown, and the runway is derived as 'seed / burn = 25-35 mo' without stating the seed size. The cash-out date is called 'explicit' but no date or seed amount appears in the provided text.
  - *Why:* A 25-35 mo runway and an undated cash-out are not actually fundable-grade numbers without the seed quantum and the burn composition (heads x loaded cost + tooling + compliance + COGS). Investors and the founders themselves cannot manage to a range this wide.
  - *Fix:* State the assumed seed (e.g. $2.5-3.5M), the headcount ramp by quarter, loaded cost per head, and the non-salary lines (compliance audits, GPU staging, managed-service floors) so the burn resolves to a tighter figure and a dated cash-out. Tie the Phase-1 federation-build start to a runway checkpoint, not just a 'second real node.'
- **[LOW]** Phase-0 assumes 'Direct OIDC to the buyer's single IdP,' but the GTM is locked to serve top-down institutional, bottom-up PLG, consortia, and campus-wide — several of which (campus-wide, consortia) involve multiple IdPs, SAML (eduGAIN/InCommon federations are heavily SAML, not just OIDC), and federation brokering from day one for any real university buyer.
  - *Why:* Universities predominantly federate via SAML through InCommon/eduGAIN. 'Single OIDC IdP' is fine for an industry-NDA lab beachhead but will not connect to a real university research office without SAML/eduGAIN brokering, which the plan defers (CILogon/eduGAIN brokering is Q10/Phase-1). This is consistent IF the beachhead is genuinely a private industry lab, but creates a hidden integration cost the moment the first university campus-wide or consortium deal lands.
  - *Fix:* Confirm in the kill-gate that the named beachhead labs use a plain OIDC IdP (many corporate labs use Okta/Entra OIDC — plausible). Explicitly flag that ANY university-side or consortium buyer triggers SAML/eduGAIN brokering work (size it in engineer-weeks now, per Q10) so it is not discovered mid-deal. Keep university campus-wide/consortium GTM motions gated behind that brokering line being built.
